Cooling of Buildings 
A holistic guide for preliminary actions, supply, distribution and production of

Preface 
This report is a master thesis in Civil Engineering at the Department of Civil Engineering at the Technical 
University of Denmark. 
The thesis corresponds to 35 ECTS and was conducted over 8 months from January 25th 2016 to 
September 25th 2016 including 2 months paternity leave.  
 
The content should be seen as a knowledge base for my future career as a consultant. It is intended to 
provide an overview for the options and challenges that will occur during a building design process in 
regards to creating a sustainable cooling solution. 
 
Special thanks to my supervisors Professor J√∏rn Toftum and Ph.D. Ongun Berk Kazanci. 
 
Cover page photos: (Geoclimate 2015; PDHcenter 2016; Bolashikov 2015). 
 
 
Kongens Lyngby, September 2016

Abstract 
Cooling of buildings is a multi-discipline process involving architecture, building layout, space 
dimensions, solar protection, space supply, distribution network, production, and control.  
The aim of this study was to make a holistic investigation of possibilities and limitations when designing 
high temperature cooling systems for office buildings and similar building types in relation to building 
design, preliminary actions, space supply, distribution, and production. The ideas and approaches may be 
applied universally when designing cooling systems. The investigation was mainly based on literature 
studies and dialogs with relevant companies and specialists.  
A building design process includes an extensive amount of parameters which can greatly influence the 
selection and operation of a cooling system. Therefore, a holistic design approach with close collaboration 
between the architects and all the engineering disciplines is necessary to minimize the heat loads in the 
early design phase and to optimize the overall performance, investment cost, operation and maintenance 
of the cooling system.  
Selection of space cooling supply is highly dependent on space type, dimensions, layout, occupancy and 
heat load ‚Äì every solution has its own advantages, disadvantages and limitations. Therefore, an 
unambiguous selection cannot be made, but it is clear that one solution does not fit all scenarios. Schemes 
have been developed to highlight and to create an overview of suitable solutions for given scenarios, thus 
narrowing the design options. 
A combination of a ventilation system, to handle the latent load, the indoor air quality and a minor part 
for the sensible loads, and a water based system, to handle the main part of the sensible loads (both 
heating and cooling), seems like the optimal solution to constantly achieve a good indoor environment 
with high energy efficiency. For cases with low cooling loads all-air systems may be the most energy, 
spatial and cost efficient solution. However, air may not be the only source of heating according to BR20, 
thus a radiant system is always necessary. 
The energy efficiency of cooling systems can be optimized by minimizing the temperature difference 
from heat sink to space supply - lower temperature differences increase the efficiency of chillers. Chillers 
should be selected based on the highest possible ESEER and high controllability to optimize part-load 
operation and performance.  
High temperature cooling enables more sustainable heat sinks such as ground, ground water, sea, and air, 
while increasing the possible use of passive cooling to a much wider extent, thus leading to energy 
savings.  
The design of distribution networks for air and water must be simple and should be optimized with focus 
on minimizing the auxiliary energy consumption and increasing the controllability. 
Mechanical cooling should be avoided by all means to reduce the energy consumption of the building. A 
proper building design in terms of layout, orientation, window design and solar shading, is essential to 
minimize the cooling demand. Solar radiation is usually the highest contributor to the cooling load and it 
must be minimized by implementing proper solar protection - most effective is mechanical external 
shadings. 
Overall, this study has illustrated the very broad knowledge required to be able to guide the building and 
system design processes towards satisfactory and energy efficient cooling solutions.

1 Introduction 
1.1 Background 
Cooling of buildings is a rapidly growing theme in Denmark and probably even more in the future. The 
clients have increasing demands towards higher quality indoor environments, while the building 
regulation demands lower energy consumption ‚Äì two aspects that may not easily come hand in hand.  
The policy in Denmark has been to continuously reduce the overall energy consumption of buildings 
considerably every 5th year. The focus has been to reduce unnecessary energy use in the building sector, 
while pushing manufacturers to improve their product and enforce contractors to use high quality 
products. In the next coming building regulation, BR20, the energy frame to obtain building permission 
for new buildings in Denmark 25 kWh/m2 per year for non-residential buildings. Consequently, the 
cooling systems must be optimized and a more holistic design approach is necessary. 
The need for cooling should by any means be avoided by other non/low energy consuming solutions. For 
new buildings the need for cooling can be minimized by a good building design and by solutions like 
solar shading, insulation and heat-accumulation. Mechanical cooling should only be applied when other 
means are insufficient. 
Mechanical cooling notoriously consumes energy and may cause thermal discomfort and the energy 
optimization getting more complicated with the traditional thinking. The tendency goes towards 
implementing high temperature cooling and low temperature heating in order to reduce heat loss in 
distribution, reduce energy consumption in chillers, and supply media to thermal units with temperature 
closer to the room temperature in order to reduce thermal discomfort and satisfy the indoor environment 
requirements.  
Basically cooling controls the indoor temperature, but it may have some side effects like draught, cold 
drop, radiant asymmetry, and so forth, which have to be avoided. The problems typically occur when the 
system is poorly designed, poorly balanced, or simply a wrong type of cooling system has been selected 
for given conditions.  
Mistakes in the design or the construction of a cooling/refrigeration system can lead to dissatisfied 
occupants and just as important a dissatisfied building owner due to unnecessary high energy 
consumption. Thereby, it is outmost important that every part of a cooling system is carefully design to 
facilitate indoor environment and energy consumption, as well as the construction and maintenance cost. 
A building design process is a multi-collaborator discipline, where design decisions are influenced by 
architects, engineers, contractors, building owners, regulations, and policy. Countless different interests 
are at stake which often results in many compromises.  
The design of the optimal cooling solution for a building consists of numerous aspects with just as many 
interfaces. Therefore, in order to possess the ability to guide a building design process towards an optimal 
cooling solution one need to have a broad knowledge of architecture, user behavior, solar influence, 
building envelope, ventilation, space supply, distribution system, refrigeration plants, control strategies, 
economy, building codes, and the list can go on. 
 
1.2 Aim 
The aim of this study was to investigate high temperature cooling of buildings and compare different 
cooling solutions in larger non-residential buildings, mainly office buildings and similar building types, 
from production to supply in terms of energy consumption, impact on indoor environment, 
constructability, integration and interaction with other building components/systems. In this context, 
==PAGE:11==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
10 of 168 
 
cooling is meant as an extra mean to control the in the indoor temperature and thereby obtain satisfying 
conditions for the occupants. 
The study should for instance help to answer the following questions:  
ÔÇ∑ 
When should the different cooling solutions be used in terms of space supply?  
ÔÇ∑ 
Can multiple solutions be combined with benefit?  
ÔÇ∑ 
How to select the correct type of refrigeration system?  
ÔÇ∑ 
How can cooling systems be optimized?  
1.3 Use of Study 
The study should create a base of knowledge for architects, engineers, and building owners, in order to; 
reduce/remove the need for mechanical cooling by preliminary actions, select the optimal type of cooling 
principle for any given building while simultaneously select the optimal production unit(s) and 
distribution system.  
It is necessary to spread knowledge about the importance of creating the right cooling solutions, otherwise 
cooling is just something that ‚Äúhas to be there‚Äù and not many building designers or building owners have 
sufficient knowledge to select the most optimal overall solution. Today, the general tendency seems to be 
to select the safe and well-tested solutions where often more optimal solutions could have been selected in 
terms of energy efficiency, cost, complexity, and so forth. 
 
1.4 Reading guide 
The report covers five of the most relevant topics in relation to cooling of buildings; 
Regulation and Design Criteria: covers rules and regulations for building energy performance, indoor 
environment, building service installations, refrigeration plants, heat pumps, refrigerants. 
Preliminary Actions: covers necessary architectural actions before applying cooling such as layout, 
shape, orientation building materials, and most importantly solar protection.  
Supply of Cooling: covers relevant solutions for high temperature cooling of various space types in terms 
of selecting supply devices and strategies. 
Distribution System: covers the basic design options and best practice solutions when transporting 
media for cooling from production unit to supply terminal.  
Refrigeration: covers the production of cooling in terms design options and best practice when selecting 
refrigeration plants, refrigerants, district cooling, and heat sinks. 
Throughout the report, sections with recommendations will appear. The recommendations are to provide 
the reader with an overview of design choices, system selections, and best practice based on the covered 
literature mixed with the opinion and practical experiences of the author.  
It is important to notice that this report a holistic design guide addressing the possibilities and limitations 
throughout the entire process when designing sustainable cooling solutions for non-residential buildings. 
Load calculations are not included, but selection and design consequences of given loads are addressed. 
The specific values provided in this report possess relatively large uncertainties and should only be seen 
as guideline values. Many values origin from experimental studies made in controlled and often 
simplified environments. Limited valid experimental studies are available which includes larger or more 
complicated spaces. Also, limited independent studies of high temperature refrigeration system and their 
functioning with high temperature cooling applications are available.

2 Regulation and Design Criteria   
The current Danish energy requirements for new buildings are based on the EU Directive Energy 
Performance of Buildings Directive (EPBD) launched in 2002 and subsequently clarified in 2010. The 
directive required member states to introduce energy labeling and minimum requirements for the energy 
consumption of new buildings. More specifically, all new buildings starting from 2021 have to be ‚Äònearly 
Zero-Energy Buildings' (nZEB), while public buildings should be nZEB from 2019.  
This requirement is a so-called functional requirement in the sense that it requires a specific function: 
Namely, that the overall net energy consumption of new buildings is close to zero. The formulation of 
'nearly zero' allows countries to interpret what is sufficiently close to zero in their relation.  
New buildings in Denmark must currently be in compliance with the Danish Building Regulation 2015 
(BR15), and building owners are already encouraged to aim for the even tighter and more ambitious 
requirements in the coming building regulation, BR20. The focus in this section will be on building 
design and thereby to fulfill the future requirements of BR20.  
2.1 Energy Performance 
In general, and according to BR15, buildings must be constructed in ways which avoid unnecessary 
energy consumption for heating, cooling, domestic hot water, ventilation and lighting while at the same 
time achieving a healthy indoor environment. 
In Denmark and several other European countries the energy performance certificate requires a 
calculation of the total energy demand over a year, not separated in heating, cooling and other utilities. 
Therefore, if excessive cooling is necessary compensation have to be made by other means such as 
reduced heating demand, implementation of sustainable energy production, or other energy saving 
initiatives in order to comply with the energy performance certificate.  
The current energy frame requirement in BR15 for new building and the voluntary requirement for BR20 
are showed in Table 1. Also included are the two new voluntary renovation classes in BR15. 
Table 1: Danish Energy Frame in kWh/m¬≤ per year. A is total heated floor area

2.1.1 Renewable Energy 
For new buildings and existing building undergoing extensive renovations a part of the total energy 
supply must be Renewable Energy (RE). However, it should be technically possible and financially 
viable.  
RE supply could be obtained with district cooling, district heating, wind power, solar, geothermal, and 
heat pumps. The proportion of renewable energy in the electricity supply may not be included. The 
maximum electricity production includable in the energy frame is 25 kWh/m2. 
Establishment of RE units in connection to a building allow the produced energy to be taken into account 
in the energy frame and thereby extending the energy frame by the produced amount. The calculation 
must take account of all losses in the RE system. The RE units can be placed on/inside the building or in 
the vicinity. The units can even be shared with other buildings. However, it is required that the owner of 
the building contributes financially to the establishment. 
 
2.1.2 Exceptions to the Energy Frame 
Exceptions to the energy frame can be made for buildings or building sections in class 2020 with special 
needs for e.g. high level of illumination, extra ventilation, large consumption of hot water, long/extended 
occupancy time, or tall rooms. 
The exception will grant permission to increase the energy frame with a supplement corresponding to the 
calculated energy consumption to meet the special needs.  
Process energy is not included in the energy frame. It comprises of energy usage which has nothing to do 
with any standard building functions e.g. cooling and electricity consumption for server rooms. All 
process energy in a building must be measured individually in order to subtract it from the energy 
framework.  
 
2.1.3 Production of Electricity 
The yearly environmental declaration of 1 kWh electricity in Denmark published by Energinet  the 
average emission of greenhouse gasses in the production of electricity is shown in Table 3 (Energinet & 
pwc 2015). The CO2-emission in Denmark is ~200 g/kWh while the European average is somewhere 
around 450 g/kWh.  
The fuel distribution of electricity in Denmark on average in 2015 consisted of: 58% wind, water and sun, 
19% coal and lignite, 13 % waste, biomass and biogas, 6% natural gas, 4% nuclear power, and 0% oil.

2.2 Indoor Environment 
The indoor environment is the reason why buildings are constructed in the first place and why all sorts of 
technical installations are necessary. This chapter aims to determine the most important design parameters 
for cooling in order to obtain a satisfying indoor environment for the majority of occupants within the 
cooling season (summer), the heating season (winter), and the transition seasons (spring/autumn).  
The goal is to provide the lowest average dissatisfaction amongst occupants at the lowest possible energy 
cost. However, the relationship between thermal comfort and energy efficiency is challenging and mostly 
opposing. The building regulation keeps on tightening the requirements for energy consumption and air-
tightness simultaneously to the clients rapidly increased demands for ‚Äòclose to perfect‚Äô indoor conditions 
regardless of varying external or internal effects and without comprising the architectural expression. 
Building designers have to keep in mind that occupants are not necessarily satisfied with the indoor 
environment just by achieving outstanding thermal comfort. Equally important parameters in the overall 
assessment of the indoor environment are air quality, visual comfort and acoustical comfort. The latter 
two are often sacrificed to achieve thermal comfort e.g. due to noisy installations or solar shadings 
blocking the view to the outside.  
The tricky question is what is most valued to the occupants; a slight drift outside the temperature range or 
improved acoustic and visual comfort? Difficult to answer since the perception of the parameters are very 
individual. Some are easily annoyed by noise, some by glare, and others dislike high temperatures.  
 
 
‚ÄúGood indoor climate can be achieved, not so much by introducing extravagant 
concepts, but by developing a rational approach to identifying the needs and 
applying the necessary tools to deal with these needs (W. Liddament 2000)‚Äù 
 
 
2.2.1 Category 
In general, the building owner decides the indoor design conditions as long as it follows the building 
regulation. Nevertheless, several guidelines and standards have created lists of categories rating indoor 
environment on a four step scale (best to worst) I, II, II and IV, or A, B, C and D.  
The recommended category for new buildings and extensive renovations is II. Explanations of the most 
commonly used index from DS/EN 15251 can be seen in Table 4. Category IV is not further used in this 
report. 
Table 4: Description of indoor environment categories (Dansk Standard 2007) 
Category 
Explanation 
I 
High level of expectation and is recommended for spaces occupied by very sensitive and 
fragile persons with special requirements like handicapped, sick, very young children and 
elderly persons.  
II 
Normal level of expectations and should be used for new buildings and renovations. 
III 
An acceptable, moderate level of expectation and may be used for existing buildings. 
IV 
Values outside the criteria for the above categories. This category should only be accepted 
for a limited part of the year.

2.2.1.1 
Parameter Deviation and Exceedance   
Various parameters can be expected and allowed to deviate from its design criteria for shorter periods of 
time. A parameter should however not deviate for extended continuous periods of time (multiple 
minutes). According to EN 15251 (Dansk Standard 2007) a parameter should not exceed its design 
category more than 3% of the time depending for Category I and 5 % of Category II and III. The duration 
of maximum exceedance can be found in Table 5. 
For new non-residential buildings following BR15 the client may determine how many hours a given 
parameter for the thermal indoor environment must exceed its design criteria.

2.2.2 Air Velocity 
Air velocity influences the convective heat exchange between a person and the environment. Increased air 
velocity can be used to offset the warmth sensation caused by increased temperatures (DS/EN ISO 7730). 
According to BR15 the mean air velocity must not exceed 0.15 m/s in the occupied zone for sedentary 
activity at any time during the year. However, in the event of indoor temperatures above 24 oC during 
summer, slightly higher air velocities may be acceptable. The limit depends on activity, temperature and 
turbulence intensity. DS/EN ISO 7730 (Dansk Standard 2006) allows variations over the year and slightly 
higher velocities up to 0.19 m/s in summer for the normal design Category II, see Table 6. The voluntary 
indoor climate classification DS 3033 (Dansk Standard 2011a) permits up to 0.20 m/s for category A as 
long as the temperature is within the corresponding comfort range and here the BR15 requirement 
correspond to a category A++.  
For personalized ventilation higher air velocities up to 1 m/s can in general be acceptable if the ventilation 
is under individual control. See DS 447 Annex C (Dansk Standard 2013) for more information. 
Frequent impulses from air supply device or rapidly changing air movement (turbulence) may cause 
discomfort due to draught even with mean velocities below the limit.  
Table 6: Maximum allowable mean air velocity for single office, landscape office, conference room, auditorium, classroom and 
similar according to BR15, DS/EN ISO 7730 and DS 3033(Dansk Standard 2006; Dansk Standard 2011a)

2.2.3 Air quality 
2.2.3.1 
Sensory Pollution 
The sensory pollution is a result of bioeffluents from human activity (body odor) and other pollution 
sources such as furniture and building materials. High concentration of bioeffluents in the air is perceived 
as smelly or thick, and is essentially a poor quality of air which reduces productivity and energy level of 
the occupants (Wargocki et al. 2006). Bioeffluents can be difficult to quantify in a sufficient and cheap 
way in order to use it as control parameter for demand controlled ventilation. Instead CO2 is used as an 
indicator due to its presence of high concentration levels.  
Sensory pollution emission of a standard sitting person is 1 olf. The combined level of pollution from all 
sources can be described with the unit decipol(dp) (1 dp = 0.1 olf/(l/s)).  
Pollution levels from various materials can be found in the literature, but it can be difficult to estimate for 
every room in a building. Therefore, as stated in Table 7 and  
Table 8 standards have simplified it to an area specific assumption according to the overall pollution level 
of the building. 
The required air change rate to overcome sensory pollution can be calculated as:

2.2.3.2 
CO2 
In general there is no standard way of evaluating the overall indoor air quality (IAQ) in a room since 
many different factors and components affects the IAQ such as particles, gasses, temperature, relative 
humidity and so forth. As a measurable indicator the concentration of CO2 can be used to evaluate the 
level of bioeffluents and the occupant‚Äôs acceptance of these.  
The CO2 concentrations in outdoor air usually range from 380 to 500 ppm. For calculations 380 ppm is 
widely used. Typically, an indoor CO2 concentration below 1000-1200 ppm indicates that the vast 
majority of the occupants will be satisfied with respect to bioeffluents. Very high concentrations of CO2 
(>5000 ppm) over extensive periods can pose a health risk. However, in office buildings and similar the 
concentration never rise to these levels (Wargocki et al. 2006). 
In offices, schools, and institutions the ventilation rate must ensure that the CO2 concentration of the 
indoor air does not exceed 1000 ppm for extended periods according to BR15 and 900 ppm according to 
BR20 (Table 9). The above calculated air flow ›ç‡Øß‡Ø¢‡Øß‡Øî‡Øü is not necessarily sufficient to obtain sufficient CO2 
concentrations! Ventilation systems should therefore have variable output depending on the load, so the 
air change rates can be increased in the spaces with high current loads and reduced in the spaces with less 
current load.  
Table 9: Limit for CO2 concentration given by DS/EN 15251 and BR15. *Concentration above outdoor level; ~380 ppm have to 
be added to get the absolute concentration.

can also be evaluated based on the build-up equation where the CO2 concentration over a time period is

However, the CO2 concentration is not a good indicator for occupant acceptance and the concentration of 
other indoor contaminants, such as volatile organic compounds (VOC) off-gassing from furnishings and 
building materials. Therefore, the CO2 concentration is not a reliable indicator of overall building air 
quality.

2.2.4 Temperature 
2.2.4.1 
Temperature Range 
The operative temperature should be kept within a certain range in order to maintain thermal comfort for 
the occupants. Table 10 displays some of the most common recommendations for temperature ranges. 
In most scenarios the operative temperature and the air temperature is said to be equal, but in cases with 
radiant panels a significant difference may occur.

According to BR15 for non-residential buildings with mechanical cooling installed the maximum 
temperature should in general not exceed 25¬∞C. However, the owner determines the maximum number of 
hours per year during which an indoor temperature of 26¬∞C and 27¬∞C, respectively, may be exceeded. 
DS 474 (Dansk Standard 1993) suggest an exceedance maximum of 100 hours of occupied time above 26 
oC per year (~5%) and a maximum of 25 hours of occupied time above 27oC per year (~1%). Short and 
infrequent exceedance of the requirements should therefore be acceptable.  
EN 15251 (Dansk Standard 2007) suggest a maximum exceedance of 108 hours of occupied time (~5%)  
outside a given temperature range which mostly also is above 26 oC during summer.  
Additionally, DS 474 suggest a maximum temperature drift of 4¬∞C/day and 2¬∞C/hour within the comfort 
interval.

displayed in Table 11. The guideline limit value for cold surfaces may be difficult to reach under normal 
conditions. Here it is essential to take the dew-point temperature into account to avoid surface

2.2.5 Humidity and Dew-point 
The air humidity influences the evaporative heat loss from a person. Normally the air humidity does not 
have a major influence on the thermal comfort of a person, but it will slightly influence the thermal 
sensation. However, if the humidity is very low (< 20%) for extended periods of time then problems with 
respiratory infections, dry skin, itchy eyes and so forth can occur. On the other hand, extended periods 
with very high humidity (> 70%) increase growth of fungi and mites, increase chemical interactions from 
materials, and give problems with most cooling systems due to a rise in the dew-point temperature.  
At moderate temperatures (<26oC) and moderate activity levels (<2 met) the influence of humidity on 
thermal comfort is rather limited. Typically a 10 % higher relative humidity is felt to be as warm as a 0.3 
oC rise in operative temperature (Dansk Standard 2006). For higher temperatures and activities the 
influence is greater. Under transient conditions the humidity can also have a significant influence. 
It is advisable to aim for a relative humidity between 40% and 60%. A commonly used design value is 
therefore 50%.   
For normal cooling scenarios the indoor air temperature is between 24oC and 26oC with a relative 
humidity between 50% and 70%. For 24oC the dew-point temperature for RH 50-70% is in range from 
13oC to 18oC, whereas for 26oC the dew-point temperature for RH 50-70% is in range from 15oC to 20oC.  
Table 13: Dew-point temperatures at difference combinations of indoor temperatures and relative humidity 
Indoor Temperature 
/ Relative humidity 
Dew-point temperature 
40% 
50% 
60% 
70% 
80% 
24oC 
9.5oC 
13.0oC 
15.5oC 
18.0oC 
20.0oC 
26oC 
11.0oC 
14.5oC 
17.5oC 
20.0oC 
22.0oC 
28oC 
13.0oC 
16.5oC 
19.0oC 
22.0oC 
24.0oC 
Risk of condensation on cold internal surfaces such as radiant cooling panels, chilled beams, etc., usually 
occurs as a result of unintended high air humidity. It is therefore most important to keep the temperature 
of cold surface above the dew-point temperature of the space by controlling of the supply temperatures or 
reduce the humidity according to the required surface temperature by increased air flow or 
dehumidification.  
At high external absolute humidity levels it is important that the AHU in the ventilation system is able to 
drain/dehumidify the air by cooling it below the supply temperature and reheat it to the supply 
temperature in order to reduce the relative humidity. However, this process is very energy consuming and 
should be reduced to a minimum and consequently large amount of the air is recirculated in hot and 
humid climates. Nevertheless very high external absolute humidity levels are relatively rare in temperate 
climates. Precautions like drainage of the cooling coil in the AHU should be made. 
==PAGE:20==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
19 of 168 
 
2.2.5.1 
Humidification 
Equipment for humidifying intake air may only be installed if this is warranted by reasons of safety, 
production, preservation or health (Bygningsstyrelsen 2015). 
For special buildings (museums, historical buildings, churches) additional humidity requirements shall be 
taken into account. Humidification or dehumidification of room air is usually not required but if used 
excess humidification and dehumidification shall be avoided. 
 
2.2.6 Activity Level 
The activity level has a great influence on the thermal sensation of a person. Low activity level increases 
the sensitivity for especially the level and the variation in temperature and air velocity. Opposite high 
activity level decreases the sensitivity; thereby both higher temperatures and higher velocities can be 
accepted.  
A moderate activity level at a metabolic rate of 1.2 met (70 W/m2), corresponding to sedentary work, is 
adequate for the majority of non-residential buildings, especially office buildings. An average person has 
approximately a body area of 1.8 m2 (Dansk Standard 2006). Thereby, 1.2 met is equal to 126 W which is 
a combination of latent and sensible heat. The amount of latent and sensible heat respectively varies with 
the temperature. Metabolic rate for other activities can be found in DS 474 (Dansk Standard 1993) or in 
DS/EN ISO 7730 Annex B (Dansk Standard 2006). 
Different activity levels and requirements for the thermal indoor environment can be expected in building 
functions such as kitchens and fitness rooms.  
 
2.2.7 Thermal Comfort  
Thermal comfort is defined as the state when occupants express satisfaction with the thermal 
environment. Discomfort or dissatisfaction occurs when the environment is experienced either as too 
warm or too cold either for the whole body or for body part e.g. cold feet, warm head or draught. This 
thesis will not go into details with evaluation of thermal comfort, but some overall thoughts have been 
made on the topic. 
The level of thermal comfort can be evaluated by utilizing Predicted Percentage of Dissatisfied (PPD) and 
Predicted Mean Vote (PMV) which is calculated from the following six parameters; clothing (clo), 
activity (met), air temperature (oC), mean temperature radiation (oC), mean air velocity (m/s), and relative 
humidity (%).  
Table 14: Recommended category for indoor environment according to PPD and PMV (Dansk Standard 2007) 
Category 
PPD [%] 
PMV 
I 
< 6 
-0.2 < PMV < +0.2 
II 
< 10 
-0.5 < PMV < +0.5 
III 
< 15 
-0.7 < PMV < +0.7 
IV 
> 15 
-0.7 < PMV or +0.7 < PMV 
According to BR15 the thermal indoor environment on sunny days shall be supported by calculations. 
The documentation may be based on simulation of conditions in the critical space. The most commonly 
used evaluation is the Hourly Criteria or Percentage Outside the Range (POR) which evaluates the 
number or percentage of hours of occupation outside/inside in relation to the chosen comfort category for 
e.g. the operative temperature and/or the PMV.  
==PAGE:21==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings

20 of 168 
 
One of the largest challenges is the individual perception of the optimal indoor environment. Therefore, 
perceived control over the thermal environment is an important parameter for many persons - the 
possibility to manually adjust cooling, heating, ventilation and lighting. 
 
2.2.8 Acoustics 
The noise from building service systems and traffic may disturb the occupants and prevent the intended 
use of the space or building. Therefore, buildings must be designed, built and fitted to ensure satisfactory 
sound conditions for the users.  
No specific noise level requirements exist for office buildings in BR15. It is advices to set up project 
specific noise level requirements which obtain a satisfying indoor environment for the given building. As 
an example the following requirements for indoor noise levels in teaching rooms is set in BR10; from 
building services LA,eq ‚â§ 30 dB and from traffic with closed windows LA,eq ‚â§ 33 dB.  
In general it is advisable to have an absorption area: A ‚â• 1.1 x floor area (Energistyrelsen & Delta 2013). 
Two types of noise have to be taken into account when designing technical installations for buildings; 
direct noise to the space and building transmitted noise.  
 
2.2.8.1 
Direct noise 
An upper limit for noise level at workplaces is set by Arbejdstilsynet to 85 dB1 (Arbejdstilsynet 2015), but 
in reality it is desirable to have much lower noise levels and each building owner is able to set his own 
limits. Different guidelines provide maximum values for the noise level; EN 15251 (Dansk Standard 
2015), SBi 196 Indeklimah√•ndbog, SBi 230 anvisning til bygningsreglementet, Lyd i VVS anl√¶g. 
Normally requirements are established for the indoor noise level in offices and meeting rooms, typical 
requirements for meeting rooms and single offices is a maximum on 30 dB(A) and for open plan offices 
on 35 dB(A). Outdoor noise, typically from traffic, can challenge/decrease the use of natural ventilation 
by openable windows. 
An example of noise level requirements set for an office building is shown in in Table 15 below.

21 of 168 
 
2.2.8.2 
Building Transmitted Noise 
The requirements for building transmitted noise and vibration is usually ensured inside the technical 
rooms with high noise levels by conducting sound insulation measures in the form of staggered walls, 
sound ceilings and floating concrete floors so that the concerned unit (AHU, heat pump, chiller, etc.) has 
no mechanical connection to the building structure. 
It is usually also a requirement that the concerned unit is established on correctly dimensioned vibration 
dampers. Details on vibration and noise should be provided by the manufacturer.  
Furthermore, if pipes and ducts are connected to a vibration unit then it must be constructed in a way so 
transfer of vibrations is limited. Pipe/duct could be constructed with either ‚Äúsoft‚Äù connection and/or with 
pipework dampers to decrease the spread vibrations. E.g. Instead of mounting pipes and duct in the 
conventional way directly to the ceiling or wall they can be hung on steel frames mounted on vibration 
damper at the floor. The frame construction then absorbs the vibration which provides a significant 
reduction in the transmitted vibrations from the production unit to the distribution pipes/ducts and further 
on to the building. Reduction of vibrations also increases the durability of the overall installation by 
avoiding cracks and failures caused by vibrations.  
 
2.2.9 Light 
Visual comfort is essential for the health and well-being of occupants. Appropriate lightning also allows 
occupants to perform visual tasks more efficiently and accurately. A visual contact to the surroundings is 
one of most important factors in the experience of a room.  
Numerous studies have been made to cover the influence of visual comfort in terms of windows and 
daylight in relation to workers and students. Sufficient daylight results in increases productivity, lower 
health complaints, reduced absenteeism, increases test performance, faster learning progress. Insufficient 
daylight or too much direct sunlight results in poorer performance in test and decreased learning abilities, 
combined with increased local thermal discomfort. Therefore, the combination between daylight and solar 
protection is important.  
According to EN-16798 (European Standard 2015) windows shall be the primary source of light during 
daylight hours supplemented with artificial light. However, large window areas without effective solar 
shading may cause overheating and glare problems. A more even distribution of windows with majority 
of north-facing windows may reduce the need for artificial lighting and will reduce issues of overheating.  
The level of daylight can be considered sufficient according to BR15 when the glass area is equal to at 
least 10% of the floor area in workrooms, living areas, kitchens, teaching room, and office space  if the 
glazing light transmittance is greater than 0.75. If the light transmittance is less, the glass area is increased 
accordingly. For double skin fa√ßades, the combined light transmittance through both layer of glazing must 
be evaluated. For skylights, the glass area can be multiplied by a factor of 1.4. However, skylight cannot 
be the only window type since this would compromise the visual contact to the surroundings. 
An alternative to calculating glass to floor area ratio, is to prove through calculations that the daylight 
factor is at least 2% at workspaces. The daylight requirement may imply that in some rooms use of solar 
film is impossible. The daylight factor shall be proven through calculations or tests. The requirements for 
BR15 and BR20 are stated in Table 16. 
Table 16: Requirements for daylight and glass area 
 
BR15 
BR20 
Glass to floor area ratio
10% 
15% 
Daylight Factor 
2% 
3% 
==PAGE:23==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
22 of 168 
 
The requirement for daylight factor basically predicts the possible depth of a room according to the design 
of the fa√ßade (window size). Architects tend to solve this issue by adding windows from floor to ceiling 
resulting in an increased possible room depth but also in an increased heat load from solar radiation. 
Basically, favoring daylight will increase the cooling load or worsen the thermal conditions and vice 
versa.  
It is important to realize that daylight factor and building orientation are not particular related. Just 
because a room is facing south does not mean it has a higher daylight factor. The daylight factor is 
calculated based on an overcast sky without direct sun light and therefore the orientation is of minor 
importance. In fact a south facing room will encounter huge challenges with direct sun resulting either in 
increased heat loads and increased risk of glare, or resulting in activation of solar shadings potentially 
leading to a blocked view and thereby also reduced daylight factor 
The use of daylight reduces the need for artificial lighting. Less artificial lighting translates into reduced 
energy consumption and reduced cooling loads. Daylight itself carries relatively little heat per quantity of 
visible light. Inside an office one hardly ever needs more than 500 lux, whilst direct sunlight can be as 
high as 100.000 lux (Dansk Standard 2007).  
 
2.2.9.1 
Discussion on Light 
Why does an absolute value for daylight play such an important role? For most new buildings with 
facades facing east and south solar shading is active for a significant period of the occupied time over the 
year leading to substantial drop in the daylight factor. Thereby for many hours daily the view is blocked 
or strongly reduced and the daylight factor is low.  
The alternative could be to have windows with further reduced g-value, reflective properties, and glare 
control to replace solar shading. This will lead to a continuous uninterrupted view which facilitates 
constant visual contact to the outside and thereby high visual comfort which cannot be replaced 
artificially. On the other hand the daylight factor would decrease and a more constant use of artificial 
lighting would be required. Potentially resulting in a higher average daylight factor since the windows are 
never completely blocked by external solar shading. The question is then; can windows with improved 
solar protection properties actually decrease the solar radiation in a comparable way to actual external 
solar shading? 
 
2.2.10 Room size 
The building regulation does not directly set a minimum size for an office, but a workroom should have a 
size and height so the work can be carried out in a suitable way in accordance with type of work and the 
number of occupants. However, in the corresponding guidance text a minimum room volume of 12 m3 per 
person and with at least 2.5 m room height is required (Bygningsstyrelsen 2015). If the room is ventilated 
efficiently the minimum is then 8 m3 per person is required.  
Arbejdstilsynet guidance A.1.11 (Arbejdstilsynet 2007) recommends a minimum requirement for floor 
area on 7 m2 per person in workrooms. DS/CEN/CR 1752 (Dansk Standard 2001) recommends a 
minimum area of 10 m2 per person for single offices and 10 m2 per person for open plan offices. Thereby, 
the minimum requirement in BR is rarely in effect when designing offices and workrooms in general.

2.3 Building Services 
Building services such as cooling and ventilation systems must be built in ways which prevents 
unnecessary energy consumption and allow easy access for inspections and maintenance.  
 
2.3.1 Distribution 
Cooling systems must be sized, designed, controlled and operated in accordance with DS 469 (Dansk 
Standard 1991).  
Circulation pumps must be installed in accordance with EU Regulation No 641/2009 (EU 2009). 
 
2.3.2 Ventilation 
Ventilation systems must be designed, installed, fully commissioned and handed over in accordance with 
DS 447 (Dansk Standard 2013), DS 428 (Dansk Standard 2011b) and BR15. 
2.3.2.1 
Energy 
Ventilation systems must function without consuming unnecessary levels of energy and without negative 
interference by other air consuming appliances. In BR20 air heating must not constitute as sole heating 
source. However, this does not apply to production halls and similar. 
Establishments must be made to limit the supply of fresh air during periods with reduced need for 
ventilation in the building. Provision must be made for adjusting the supply of fresh air to suit the loads in 
rooms/spaces with highly variable ventilation needs. 
Requirements for specific electricity consumption for ventilation, the SEL-value, can be seen in Table 18. 
The SEL-value must be provided by the manufacturer of the AHU based on pressure loss calculation of 
the overall ventilation system. Furthermore, it is required that all new AHU must be installed with fans 
with three step or variable speed drive (EU 2014b). 
Table 18: Requirement for maximum specific electricity consumption for non-residential ventilation systems (Bygningsstyrelsen 
2015). 
Ventilation Type 
BR15 
[J/m3] 
BR20 
[J/m3] 
CAV 
1800  
1500 
VAV 
2100 
1800 
Exhaust 
800 
800 
 
2.3.2.2 
Heat Recovery 
The general requirements for heat recovery is set out in EU Regulation No 1253/2014 (EU 2014b). 
Mechanical ventilation systems in both non-residential and residential must incorporate heat recovery 
with a dry temperature efficiency of at least 67% for BR15 and 73% for BR20. 
Liquid-coupled batteries may only be used, if separation of the air flows is essential for maintaining the 
function of the rooms. 
The requirement for heat recovery may be ignored when the surplus heat from the exhaust air cannot be 
reasonably used e.g. in cooling season. 
==PAGE:27==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
26 of 168 
 
2.3.3 Building Services 
Technical installations such as pipes, ducts, and other equipment requiring frequent maintenance, 
adjustments or inspections, must be installed in ways which enable personnel to perform the action in a 
proper and appropriate manner.  
According to BR15 segments of pipe- or duct-systems requiring frequent inspections should be installed 
in service galleries with a clear high and width of no less than 1.9m and 0.7m, in shaft with good access 
arrangements or behind removable covers (e.g. removable ceiling places). 
 
2.3.4 Thermal insulation 
Technical installations such as pipes, ducts, and other equipment must be insulated in accordance with DS 
452 (Dansk Standard 1999) to avoid condensation and to prevent unnecessary heat loss. 
Additionally, it is recommended to follow the ROCKWOOL guideline den lille tekniske (Rockwool 
2016) for insulation of all heat carrying technical installations. If the installations are insufficiently 
insulated the energy losses for low flow rates can have significant influence on the supply temperature 
and thereby decreasing the cooling capacity. The decrease can be achieved most efficiently by increasing 
the insulation thickness on the main ducts rather than the connection ducts (Bertilsson et al. 2012). 
 
 
==PAGE:28==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
27 of 168 
 
2.4 Refrigerants 
This chapter will consist of environmental effect, prohibitions, regulations, rules and more for 
refrigerants.   
The predominant refrigerants used in air condition applications today are HFCs R134a, 410A and R407C 
which replace the now prohibited HCFCs R12 and R22. It appears that there is no perfect refrigerant, for 
static AC applications, with a GWP lower than the traditional HFCs. Alternatives are either flammable 
(hydrocarbons, HFOs, R32), toxic (ammonia) or of low performance for AC application (CO2). Therefore 
alternative refrigerants are not expected to replace HFCs in the nearest future (Bitzer 2015b). 
For larger refrigeration systems the natural refrigerants ammonia and CO2 are preferred mainly due to the 
charge limits of the HFCs. 
 
2.4.1 Single or Blend 
Refrigerants can be applied either as single substances or in blends of two or more different refrigerants. 
An overview of the most common refrigerants can be found in Figure 1.   
 
Figure 1: Overview of refrigerants (Bitzer 2015b) 
 
2.4.1.1 
Zeotropic and Azeotropic 
It is important to distinguish between a Zeotropic and an Azeotropic refrigerant mixture. Azeotropic 
mixtures have a new common boiling point and act as a pure refrigerant.  
For zeotropic mixtures every component retains its own boiling point, thus the condensation and 
evaporation will occur with a gliding temperature. The temperature difference between the liquid 
saturation curve and the gaseous saturation curve is called the temperature glide. The temperature of the 
refrigerant will increase through the evaporator as more of the liquid refrigerant evaporates.  
==PAGE:29==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
28 of 168 
 
2.4.2 Environmental 
Emission of greenhouse gases from refrigeration plants contribute to the global warming effect. The 
emission occurs from leakage, service and destruction of refrigeration systems. Any refrigerant must 
therefore be categorized according to its environmental impact with the following two values:  
The Ozone Depletion Potential (ODP) of a refrigerant is compared to Freon (R11) as reference which are 
given the value 1.0 (worst). The Greenhouse Warming Potential (GWP) of a refrigerant is compared to 
CO2 as reference which is given the value 1.0. Example, 1 kg R134a is equivalent to 1430 kg CO2. ODP 
and GWP of commonly used refrigerants can be seen in Table 22.  
On basis of high GWP many synthetic refrigerants (HFC‚Äôs, PFC‚Äôs and SF6) is banned by law 
(Milj√∏styrelsen 2016c) in new refrigeration applications. However, exceptions are made on HFC‚Äôs in heat 
pumps, air conditioning and dehumidifiers with charges between 0.15 kg and 10 kg. Additionally, it is 
legal to service already existing application with HFC, but it is illegal to recharge existing applications 
with new refrigerants.  
The use of natural refrigerants (ammonia, CO2, etc.) are legal in refrigeration application at any size of 
charge as long as the safety requirements are met for the installation and the corresponding surroundings. 
It is worth to mention that the emission from refrigeration applications comes from leakage and when the 
application has to be dismantled and removed. Leakage should be frequently checked leading to very 
small emission rate and the dismantle process can be done in ways which allows the refrigerant to be 
recycled.  
Generally as long as the application is running intentionally the emission rate it outmost limited. 
However, the greatest sinner is the electricity consumption of the application during its lifetime. The 
emission rate of CO2-equivalents is 200-500g for each kWh of electricity. Focus should not only be to 
choose most environmental friendly refrigerant, but more importantly to choose refrigerants with good 
thermodynamic properties, use high efficiency compressors and associated equipment, as well as 
optimizing the overall refrigeration system.  
 
2.4.3 Prohibited Refrigerants 
The following ozone depleting substances and their isotopes, alone and in blends with more than 1 
weight-percentage of the substance are prohibited by law to use in new refrigeration systems 
(Milj√∏styrelsen 2016a): 
ÔÇ∑ 
All CFCs (fully halogenated chlorfluorcarbons): R12, etc. 
ÔÇ∑ 
All halons (fully halogenated bromflourcarbons, bromchlorcarbons and bromchlorfluorcarbons) 
ÔÇ∑ 
All HBFCs (hydrobromfluorcarbons) 
ÔÇ∑ 
All HCFCs (hydrochlorfluorcarbons): R22, R502, etc. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Methylbromid, Triclorethan and Tetraclormethan 
Alternatives to many of the prohibited refrigerants can be found in Bitzer Refrigerant Report 18 (Bitzer 
2015b). 
 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings

2.4.4 F-Gas Regulation 
The EU F-Gas Regulation 517/2014 (EU 2014c) aims at considerably reducing emission of F-Gases from 
various applications and stepwise phase-down of the use in medium to large capacity systems. The 
regulation includes training and certification of personnel, reporting on the use of F-Gasses by refrigerant 
manufacturers, and mandatory regular leakage checks from refrigerant charges of above 5 tons of CO2 
equivalent. For a wide range of applications a maximum GWP value is defined starting in 2015 for 
domestic applications, additional application will be included in 2020. Further a broad range of 
considerable restrictions are added from 2022, which will be tightened stepwise until 2030. 
The current average GWP value of all HFCs currently used in EU is approximately 2200-2300 (Bitzer 
2015a).  To meet the 2030 goal an average GWP in EU below 500 is required, but all current available 
refrigerants below this limit are in some ways flammable and/or toxic, except CO2.  
In 2020 the use of refrigerants with GWP above 2500 will be banned in stationary applications and 
commercial refrigerators and freezers. However, servicing charges below 40 ton CO2-equivalent is 
allowed and recycled refrigerants with GWP above 2500 can still be used for all servicing.  
In 2022 the use of refrigerants with GWP above 150 is banned in commercial refrigerators and freezers, 
and in multi-compressor systems with capacities above 40 kW. However, refrigerants in the primary 
circuit in cascade systems are banned with GWP above 1500.  
In 2025 split units containing less than 3 kg fluor-gasses with GWP above 750 is banned. 
 
Table 19: Summary of prohibitions extracted from EU F-Gas Regulation 517/2014 (Bitzer 2015a). Red is prohibited and Green is

2.4.5 Eco-efficiency 
The Eco-efficiency is an overall objective assessment of refrigeration plants based on the relationship 
between investment cost and the resulting environmental impact. For most companies the investment and 
operation cost is decisive whereas the resulting environmental impact is more or less neglected.  
Calculation method of the Eco-efficiency can be found in Bitzer Refrigeration Report 18 (Bitzer 2015b)  
 
==PAGE:31==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
30 of 168 
 
2.4.6 Total Equivalent Warming Impact (TEWI) 
Total Equivalent Warming Impact (TEWI) is a way of assessing the total global warming impact of a 
refrigeration system in term of CO2-equivalents by combining the direct contribution of refrigerant 
emission (leakage) with the indirect contribution of CO2 emissions from the energy consumption over the 
system operational lifetime.  
A TEWI calculation can thereby help to identify the overall most environmental friendly systems and is 
particular important in the decision making during a design process of new or retrofitted refrigeration 
systems. Designers will quickly notice that more than the 99% share of CO2 emission over a lifetime is 
due to the energy consumption for almost any refrigeration systems as illustrated in the calculation 
example in Figure 2. 
The refrigerant with the lowest GWP is thereby not the necessarily the best for the environment. If the 
thermodynamic properties are bad it could be many times worse for the environment compared to a 
refrigerant with a GWP beyond 2500 due e.g. to higher electricity use from the compressors.  
A more detailed description and the calculation method can be found in prEN 378-1 Annex B (Dansk 
Standard 2012b). Furthermore, a free available excel sheet have been developed by Videncenter for HFC-
fri k√∏ling (Videnscenter for HFC-fri k√∏ling 2008) which can directly calculate and compare the TEWI of 
two refrigeration systems based on type of refrigerant, charge size, leakage rate, expected operational life 
time, energy consumption, and yearly operation time. 
 
 
Figure 2: TEWI calculation for R1234ze and R134a with GWP on 1 and 1430, respectively, based on excel sheet from Videncenter 
for HFC-fri k√∏ling (Videnscenter for HFC-fri k√∏ling 2008). 10kg charge size, 15% leak rate, 20 year operation and 50 kW cooling. 
 
 
==PAGE:32==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
31 of 168 
 
2.4.7 Existing Refrigeration System Defined As New 
An existing refrigeration system is considered new and should thereby follow the current rules and 
regulations when the original refrigerant is tapped off and replaced by a different refrigerant. 
The process of converting a refrigeration system from an old prohibited refrigerant to a new legal 
refrigerant can be described in two ways; Drop-in and Retrofitting. 
Drop-in: The existing refrigerant is tapped of the system and a new refrigerant with similar properties, 
but with less environmental effect, is filled on the system without changing any component or oil. 
Retrofitting: The existing refrigerant is tapped of the system and a new refrigerant with different 
properties, but with less environmental effect, is filled on the system. This will most likely require change 
of oil and replacement of thermostatic expansion valve and other components.  
 
2.4.8 Regenerated Refrigerants 
A regenerated refrigerant is refrigerant which earlier has been used in a refrigeration system. The 
refrigerant has been tapped of a system and gone through a cleaning process to remove oil, water, dirt, 
and any other undesirable stuff that may decrease the quality of the refrigerant. 
Generally it is allowed to use all currently legal refrigerants as regenerated refrigerants for servicing of 
existing and new refrigeration systems.  
However, some prohibited refrigerants including R22 and R123 are still allowed to use as regenerated 
refrigerant for servicing existing systems.  
 
2.4.9 Safety Group 
Refrigerants are divided into groups based on their flammability and toxicity. The Safety Group 
classification for refrigerants from DS/EN 378 is shown in Table 20. The table has been modified to fit 
the upcoming prEN 378 were category 2 now flammable and category 2L is lower flammability.

2.4.10 Refrigerant Charge 
The charge size for natural refrigerants and hydrocarbons (HC) is theoretically and legally unlimited, but 
practical limitations such as product and machine room safety set some boundaries.  
For HFC‚Äôs as refrigerant a maximum charge limit of 10 kg per individual refrigeration cycle is required. 
Multiple refrigeration cycles with 10 kg charge may be coupled to form a system, but they have to be 
independent of each other ‚Äì meaning the maximum discharge in case of an error should only be 10 kg. 
Legislation wise HFO‚Äôs are considered as HFC‚Äôs meaning they have to follow the same rules for charge 
limit and use. It has however nothing to do with the environmental effect of these substances since GWP 
is very low, but it seems like a purely political decision. The legislation is hopefully and expectably to be 
loosened in regards of charge limit for HFO‚Äôs in the near future. 
An overview of the charge limit for selected refrigerant can be found in Table 22.  
 
2.4.10.1 Dispensation for Charge Size 
A dispensation to avoid following the charge limits can be given on individual basis. The dispensation is 
in Denmark granted by Milj√∏styrelsen and should provide documentation that the specific system with the 
intended limited refrigerant is better environmental wise than its direct alternatives.  
Usually, it may be proven by a TEWI calculation showing the overall CO2 emission is lowers than the 
alternative systems. Furthermore the safety of the refrigerant and the system should be insured and well 
documented.  
 
2.4.11 Tax 
In Denmark the use of certain synthetic refrigerants (CFC, HCF, PFC and SF6) is imposed a tax described 
in CFC-afgiftloven (Skatteministeriet 2015). The tax rate on each individual substance is determined 
based on their greenhouse effect in relation to the tax rate of CO2. However, there is an upper limit of 600 
kr. per kg. 
==PAGE:34==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
33 of 168 
 
The substances greenhouse effect is measured in GWP and is established and reviewed regularly by the 
UN climate panel IPCC. The tax rates of selected refrigerants can be seen in Table 22.  
CFC-afgiftloven does unfortunately not cover all refrigerant. If a refrigerant is not on the tax list then the 
tax will be set to 600 kr/kg regardless of its GWP. E.g. R450A have a GWP on 604 is not on the tax list 
thus the tax rate will become 5-6 times higher than it should have if it was included.  
 
2.4.12 Purchase 
The price and availability of a refrigerant is often of high importance when selecting refrigeration system. 
However, it has not been possible to collect prices for refrigerants. The price changes according to charge 
size, distributor and so forth.  
Natural refrigerants are generally cheap around 25-50 kr./kg compared to the synthetic refrigerants of 
around 400-900 kr./kg (KMO 2013).

compared in terms of their thermodynamic properties

2.5 Refrigeration Systems 
This section will consist of some of the main requirements for refrigeration plants which are relevant for 
building designers. However, this report will not go into detailed component requirements for 
refrigeration plants and heat pumps.  
The current building regulation does not provide many specific requirements for the construction or 
solutions for refrigeration systems or heat pumps. Refrigeration plants must be constructed and installed 
in ways which prevent any danger of fire, explosion, poisoning, and health hazards in the forms of noise 
and vibrations. A broad range of safety and health requirements are stated in Maskindirektivet (EU 2006) 
and Trykudstyrsdirektivet (EU 2014a).  
Special requirement and guidelines concerning installation, operation and maintenance for refrigeration 
plant and heat pumps is stated in  AT-vejledning B.4.4 (Arbejdstilsynet 2010) and should be followed.  
2.5.1 Performance 
Heat pumps must have a minimum COP of 3.6 in heating mode. COP in heating mode must be 
documented in accordance with DS/EN 14511, EU Regulation No 813/2013/EU (EU 2013) and EU 
Regulation No 206/2012/EU (EU 2012). 
Table 23: Energy requirements for air-to-air heat pumps 
 
SEER (cooling) 
SCOP (heating) 
GWP > 150 for effect < 6 kW 
4.60 
3.80 
GWP < 150 for effect < 6 kW 
4.14 
3.42 
GWP > 150 for effect 6-12 kW 
4.30 
3.80 
GWP < 150 for effect 6-12 kW 
3.87 
3.42 
 
2.5.2 Machine Room 
The room in which a refrigeration system is placed is often referred to as called a machine room. In 
general, refrigeration systems should be installed in machine rooms, so spills and risk of injuries are 
limited. There is also a desire to minimize the noise from the plants. If hazardous liquids or gases are used 
in the refrigeration system the machine room will be classified with explosion hazard, then the machine 
room must be located and constructed suitably and safely. 
The following requirements for refrigeration plants and machine rooms are most interesting for building 
designers (Arbejdstilsynet 2010): 
ÔÇ∑ 
Plants must be installed in ways that ensure safe and healthy conditions in relation to leakage, 
flammability, toxicity, vibrations and noise. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Plants with refrigerant charge less than 25 kg may be installed in machine rooms with other 
technical installations than necessary for the refrigeration plants.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Plants with refrigerant charge more than 25 kg must be installed in a separate room without other 
technical installations than necessary for the refrigeration plant. 
ÔÇ∑ 
The room must be constructed as a workroom with a free height of 2.5 meter. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Plants with refrigerant charges above 250 kg must not be installed in rooms below ground level. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Plants with refrigerant charges above 1 kg must as minimum be inspected 1 time per year.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Machine rooms must be equipped with gas detector when CO2 or a group 1 refrigerant is used. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Mechanical ventilation of the machine room is required when it is placed below ground level and 
the refrigerant is in safety class 1.
