3 Preliminary Actions  
The use of cooling should in general be avoided by all means with a proper architectural design of the 
building. All preliminary actions, mainly architectural, must be taken in order to remove/reduce the 
cooling demand. It is possible to create good architecture with increased comfort while addressing energy 
conservation reasons. This presupposes that architects early in the design process takes into account the 
form-related transactions which have implications for the energy consumption in new buildings. But even 
in cases with excellent architecture, some sort of cooling will most likely be required in given building 
type. 
This chapter will mainly focus on new building providing a brief overview to some of the main issues in 
relation to building shape, orientation, layout and material. Specific solutions are however outside the 
scope of this thesis.  
For existing buildings many of the same aspects as for new building should/must be taken into account. 
Special attention should be paid to solar shading and window properties since the orientation of the 
building is locked. Also, the possibility of moving high load equipment/functions or high performance 
functionalities to more suitable places should always be considered which can be an easy and cheap 
solution to avoid/reduce expensive and often complicated cooling solution. 
‚ÄúAs far as the thermal indoor climate is concerned, the planning of buildings and 
the choice of materials, window areas, cooling options, orientation and solar 
screening must ensure that satisfactory temperature conditions are achieved, even 
in summertime.‚Äù (Bygningsstyrelsen 2015)  
 
3.1 Building Shape and Orientation 
For new buildings attention should be paid to the orientation and shape of the building especially in terms 
of sun, natural shading conditions, and the use of passive and active solar shading. 
The shape is expressed by the fa√ßades and is the trademark of the architect. It dictates how the world will 
experience the building and how the owner/user will be judge upon the looks. Will it be remembered, 
recognized and acknowledged, looked at with pity and disgrace, or blend into the adjacent building mass? 
The shape is often a tender subject to the architect and an important subject for the design engineers. The 
shape should not only be a matter of a given design expression. It should also reflex the needs of the 
underlying premises. After all, buildings are for the users inside and not just for the spectators outside. By 
that said buildings should not just be a ‚Äúgrey mass‚Äù designed and optimized to the fullest solely by 
engineers. Buildings need to fit into the surroundings and be pleasant and interesting for the eye, but at 
the same time fulfill the energy requirements and especially the indoor comfort of the occupants. 
The orientation mainly influences the amount of solar radiation and daylight to given spaces inside the 
building. Solar radiation and daylight should be optimized in accordance to building orientation relative 
to the natural sun and shading conditions. For instance if large glazed areas are desired in an atrium it is 
inappropriate energy wise to face direct south and is better orientated directly north to reduce solar gains. 
However, it is also important to consider which other type of space(s) and fa√ßade will replace the atrium, 
since they may be more critical with an indoor environmental perspective.  
The orientation should thereby be seen as a whole to reduce the total heat load on the building but just as 
importantly to reduce high peak loads in critical parts of the building. A more uniform heat load in a 
building is generally desirable in terms of control and energy usage.  
==PAGE:37==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
36 of 168 
 
The orientation combined with the shape also influence the wind field around the building and the noise 
level from traffic ‚Äì both aspect can influence cooling solutions, especially the use of skin fa√ßades, natural 
ventilation through openable windows, and ventilation intake of AHU.  
 
3.2 Building Layout 
The building layout can be a complicated affair with multiple conflicts of interest. By all means the 
building layout is traditionally dictated to the architect in collaboration with the building owner, which 
means that the engineers just have to create solutions that fit into the given layout. Solutions can (almost) 
always be found - it is only a matter of cost and energy consumption.  
Since money is not growing on trees and building regulations forces energy consumption to a minimum, 
every possible action to optimize and improve the building design, energy consumption and cost have to 
be taken.  
Attention should be paid to the layout of functions within the building in order to place functions with 
high demands for performance or functions with high internal heat load in appropriate places e.g. facing 
north instead of south to reduce the solar radiation. 
Spaces with no requirement for daylight but with high internal heat load, e.g. meeting rooms, may be 
placed in the center of the building away from the building envelope in order to remove the external 
loads. However, meeting rooms could benefit to be placed next to atrium in order to give the impression 
of daylight and external view. 
Different aspects are important to the layout depending on the chosen cooling method for a given space or 
zone. Therefore it is a good idea to have a concept of the solution on an early stage in the design process 
and have a dialogue between architects, owner, and engineers, before it is impossible to implement the 
desired solution in a sufficient way. 
In general, spaces/functions with high internal heat loads such as meeting rooms, auditoriums, or canteens 
should be placed favorable with respect to solar radiation, as well as ventilation and cooling supply. It is 
beneficial to place these rooms/functions near an installation shaft or a technical room in order to limit the 
length and size of pipes and ducts, thus the overall costs. A nearby shaft/technical room can also be 
utilized for equipment and control of e.g. radiant floors/ceilings.  
 
3.2.1 Space Height 
The height of a space can be an important factor in the design of certain cooling systems, but also a mean 
to avoid additional cooling. The air volume of a space can be seen as a thermal capacity - relatively small 
but very useful. Tall spaces give higher capacity. The air act as a medium for transport of energy meaning 
heat is transferred from a source to the air which then either can be cooled inside the room, or exhausted 
and replaced by new colder air.  
The volume acts as a buffer delaying potential overheating. It enables cooling systems to give a more 
constant effect if considering a room volume with varying heat gains. A small volume gives a small 
buffer potentially resulting in more peak loads and shorter reaction time for the cooling system in order to 
provide a constant temperature. A larger volume gives a larger buffer resulting in a smoother load case 
and thereby a longer reaction time.  
For most buildings a day can be seen as a cycle with a given variation in load over the day. Ideally, if the 
volume has a sufficient size all the heat in the occupied time can be absorbed in the air and no cooling 
would be needed beside the thermal effect of the required air change. The accumulated heat can be 
==PAGE:38==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
37 of 168 
 
released outside occupied hours and be ready for the next cycle. In reality, such large volumes are mostly 
utopic in modern buildings and would require enormous structures. Nevertheless, a good example on a 
tall room with high thermal capacity is an atrium.  
Firstly designers have to make the best compromise between room height and ventilation rate. If the 
compromise is not sufficient to overcome the heat load and fulfil the comfort requirements then 
implementation of additional cooling system should be considered.  
Increasing the space height can be a good tool in the design for certain cooling solutions in new buildings, 
but it may very impossible/comprehensive in existing buildings. Therefore, when retrofitting buildings 
focus should instead be to avoid unnecessary reduction of height in order to reduce peak cooling demand. 
However, decreasing room height can in some cases be beneficial depending on the overheating reason 
and the desired cooling solution. 
 
3.2.1.1 
Stratification 
Stratification of the air is another important issue related to the room height. Stratification is a result of 
the buoyancy effect; Warm lighter air rises, while cold heavier air sinks. Heat sources such as humans, 
computers and sun radiation will create local plumes of warm air rising to the ceiling.  
If considering a volume with a uniform temperature and heat sources were added then the temperature 
will start rising, mostly in the top of the space where the heat cannot rise further or escape, and 
consequently layers will form with slightly different temperatures - stratification of the air has occurred. 
Constantly the warm layers will increase in height and eventually the whole volume will have too high 
temperature. The stratification will continue as long as the net gain to the space is positive resulting in 
each layer increasing in temperature. If the net gain is set back to zero the volume will slowly return to a 
uniform, but higher than initial, temperature.  
The point is that the temperature can be too high in the top of the room whilst acceptable in the occupied 
zone. The key is to keep the warm layers above the occupied zone, still supply fresh conditioned air to the 
occupants, and avoid thermal discomfort such as cold feet, draught, radiant asymmetry, etc. 
 
3.2.2 Atrium 
An atrium is traditionally a central open space fully or partly surrounded by spaces/buildings. Today, it is 
used likewise as a central but most often closed space either fully or partly surrounded by spaces. The 
function is architecturally to create openness and light into the heart of multi-story buildings. In a HVAC 
perspective it is frequently used as an exhaust zone by utilizing the natural thermal buoyancy force to 
release the exhaust air either directly to the free or extract it in the very top of the atrium and return it to 
the AHU.  
As mentioned earlier, an atrium is an excellent thermal buffer due to its large volume with no significant 
internal gains beside exhaust air from the adjacent spaces. Atria are always tailor-made and therefore 
difficult to generalize in terms of thermal capacity and cooling saving potential. If designed properly the 
heat build-up in an atrium can be controlled relatively simple and the risk of overheating within the 
atrium is insignificant. 
==PAGE:39==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
38 of 168 
 
Figure 3: Principal for an atrium utilized for displacement ventilation (Graca, Chen, and Anderson 2015) 
 
If an atrium if extensively glazed, either at one or more of the sides or at the roof top, the risk of 
overheating high which is counteracting the intentions of a thermal buffer. This can be solved by applying 
solar shading, solar protection glazing (film, electrochromic, coating, and tints), exposed thermal mass 
and lastly, but undesirable, increased ventilation. 
Additionally, atriums may experiences substantial temperature stratification affecting the magnitude and 
potentially also the direction of air flow from or to the adjacent spaces. If the atrium is designed as an 
exhaust zone, the roof should be placed significantly above the top of the highest adjacent space in 
connection to the atrium in order to avoid warm air contaminating this space. 
The most optimal atrium design in terms of daylight light and cooling load is an atrium complete 
surrounded by spaces and fully glazed roof with solar protective glazing and mechanical solar shading to 
diminish the majority of solar gains. Alternatively, a north facing sides could be extensively glazed to 
improve daylight with no significant solar gains it should however be possible to control the light 
intensity through the glazed fa√ßade. Furthermore, the atrium should in the top be connected to the 
ventilation system in order to use it as exhaust zone and also have ventilative openings for exhaust air 
when heat recovery is not required and for night cooling ventilation. Night cooling should unload heat 
build-up in the space and in the exposed thermal masses by means of free or mechanically assisted 
convection. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:40==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
39 of 168 
 
3.3 Occupancy 
Buildings are made for the users regardless of building type. Therefore it is of outmost importance in the 
design process to know how and when the occupants will use the building, since occupants are the only 
reason why technical installations are installed in the first place. No occupant equals no one to satisfy and 
no heating, no cooling, no air change, is needed.  
Regardless of new build or renovation, building owner(s) have to evaluate and decide upon potential 
occupancy schemes and the expectations for deviation of these schemes (e.g. minimum and maximum 
scenarios). The occupancy schemes should be determined as early in the design process as possible.  
The following questions should as minimum be asked/clarified at a very early stage in the design process 
in order to determine the expected occupancy behavior:  
ÔÇ∑ 
Hourly number and density of occupants in each space.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Total daily occupancy time; 8 hour, 12 hours, 18 hours, 24 hours? 
ÔÇ∑ 
Weekly days in use; 5 days, 6 days, 7 days? 
ÔÇ∑ 
Seasonal use; constant use all seasons, reduced uses during summer time, etc.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Specific use of high density space; auditoriums, canteens, meeting rooms, teaching facilities, and 
similar.  
These questions create a decent basis to design the most optimal systems for cooling, heating, ventilation, 
solar shading, etc., and it should be seen in accordance with the desired indoor environment category and 
the energy frame. 
If these questions are asked or answered too late in the design process it can be very difficult to 
implement the most optimal solution(s) which in the worst case can make it nearly impossible to comply 
with energy frame, indoor environment and investment costs.  
The sooner these questions are asked the easier it gets for the HVAC engineers to influence the structural 
system, to alter/optimize the building layout or fa√ßades, and to create a buildable solution that complies 
with the requirements. 
 
3.3.1 Diversity Factor  
Determination of the Diversity Factor or Load Factor is the assessment of probability that every space in a 
building is used simultaneously during peak load. It is a way of reducing the dimensions on HVAC 
systems in order to avoid unnecessary cost from oversized components, pipes, and ducts, and still comply 
with the requirements.  
It is unlikely that all offices, meeting rooms, and so forth in a building are in full use during an entire day 
with peak load. A person can for obvious reasons only be present at one place at the time ‚Äì that person 
cannot both be present in a meeting room and in an office at the same time. Furthermore, if an office 
building is designed for 100 persons, it is unlikely that all 100 show up every day. Maybe only 2/3 of the 
building is in use simultaneously which indicate that the dimensioning load for a system is often 
significantly lower than the total load with all areas at maximum. For schools and offices the diversity 
factor can often be set at 0.6 to 0.8, indicating the dimensioning load is only 60-80% of the maximum 
load. The Diversity Factor should be estimated/investigated based on each specific project and cannot as 
such be generalized, however rule of thumbs and previous experiences is of high importance. 
 
==PAGE:41==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
40 of 168 
 
3.4 Equipment 
Heat emitting equipment in a building is typically electronics such as computers, projectors, printers, 
servers and so forth. It can also be specialized equipment in e.g. a hospital which can have a very high 
heat emission then special cooling systems need to be installed.  
Common for all type of equipment is to know the output effect and placement in order to assess the 
correct heat load to each space. 
Functions dependent of placement such as equipment related to workstations, meeting rooms, and similar 
is hard to reduce or move, but it is still essential to know the number of components and their outputs.  
Functions semi-independent of placement such as print/copy machines should be placed in a dedicated 
space to also control the corresponding pollution load or in places with minor additional heat sources and 
away from occupants in order to reduce the total local heat and pollution load. 
Functions independent of placement such as server rooms with high heat emission should be carefully 
placed in order to optimize the heat removal e.g. shorten the distance to technical room or the heat sink 
and to reduce/remove external loads. 
 
3.5 Building Materials 
An action that requires no energy consumption but is potentially able to reduce the peak cooling demand 
is the use of materials indoors with high thermal mass and thereby high heat capacity. High heat capacity 
elements absorb and accumulate heat during the day time resulting in more even temperature changes and 
releasing the heat in e.g. the night time. However, it is depended on a cooling strategy, usually during 
non-occupied hours (e.g. night), to lower the indoor temperature air temperature such the absorbed heat is 
released and removed (e.g. by natural night ventilation).  
Another strategy to remove the heat in building materials is to install a Termo Active Building System 
(TABS) inside the elements, which continuously remove a part load of the accumulated heat. Here the 
heat can be removed over a 24 hours cycle, which enables a low by continuously effect. If the 
accumulated heat in the thermal mass is not removed then the effect will be the opposite namely 
contributing to overheating. In that case a light construction or hidden heavy construction would be 
preferable. It should also be mentioned that a high thermal mass can contribute to lower heating demand 
during winter; in this case the cooling strategy should be deactivated or the TABS should be supplied by a 
warmer fluid. 
In general, buildings consist of massive heat capacities namely in concrete elements. The problem is that 
the actual use of the heat capacity is at a minimum. Construction elements are often hidden behind layers 
of paint, gypsum board, acoustical material, and so forth, which essentially slow the heat transfer process 
due to the extra resistances between the space and the heat capacity.  
In order for the heat accumulation to be effective it needs to be (relatively) fast, which requires a very low 
resistance between the space and heat capacity. The simple solution is exposed surfaces of the heat 
absorbing materials such as exposed concrete walls or ceilings. Large exposed surfaces will provide faster 
and thus better heat accumulation and heat release. 
 
 
==PAGE:42==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
41 of 168 
 
3.6 Solar Protection 
Solar radiation through windows is probably the largest enemy (heat source) for any type of building 
when it comes to indoor thermal comfort and energy consumption for cooling. The recent year‚Äôs 
improvements of building envelopes and the increased tendency to build large glazed facades have 
resulted in high heat gains due to solar radiation, even within the traditional heating season. Special 
attention should therefore be paid to control the sun radiation and thereby reduce the cooling needs 
significantly. 
The total cooling load in the perimeter of a building is commonly ranging from 50 up to 120 W/m¬≤ 
depending on the solar radiation (Bertilsson et al. 2012). Thereby, the required cooling capacity is 
strongly dependent on the orientation, the shading strategy and the window properties.  
It should be noted that the geographical location plays a less significant role than one would think: the 
maximum cooling load is almost the identical in southern as in northern Europe if the glazing, orientation, 
and shading are the same. 
Many different actions can be taken in order to limit/control the solar radiation, but most solutions have a 
negative backside in terms of reduced daylight and reduced contact with the outdoors, which are 
important parameters for visual comfort. Also, the visual aspect of the shadings can be a torn in the eye 
for architects. 
Focus on making solar protection an integrated part of the entire building design process is vital and 
especially important in the initial design phases. Often solar shading is neglected in the architectural 
design due to the creative nature of architects and unawareness from the client perspective.  
Commonly, architects are driven predominantly by aesthetic considerations and tend to make the ‚Äúpretty‚Äù 
choices - but rarely the overall best-in-class solutions. Solar shading systems can be both attractive and 
effective if attention is paid from the initial design phases.  
Generally there are three possible positions for solar shadings; external, internal and intermediate (double 
skin fa√ßades). Furthermore, it is possible to control solar radiation by means of glazing properties, tints, 
window film and elctrochromatic glazing 
‚ÄúWith respect to light conditions, there should be an appropriate relationship between window sizes, 
room proportions and surface properties, taking the outdoor views into account. It is also important that 
the other factors which create the right visual environment should be suitable, which includes the facility 
for adjusting the lighting to suit the tasks carried out in the room.‚Äù (Bygningsstyrelsen 2015). 
  
 
Figure 4: External Solar Protection (ES-SO 2016) 
==PAGE:43==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
42 of 168 
 
3.6.1 Windows and Glazing 
Windows have to comply with strict regulations for the U-value and tightness. Other window properties 
such as g-value and light transmittance are not regulated, which leave it up to the building designers to 
select the right combination of properties.   
The selection of windows comes hand in hand with the selection of solar protection. The selection of 
window type, size, properties, etc., has impact on the requirements to the solar protection system and vice 
versa.  
Careful specification of window and glazing systems is essential to the energy efficiency and comfort of 
all buildings. Properly designed window systems can reduce the energy consumption and the investment 
cost of HVAC systems significantly. The design selecting should be evaluated holistically in 
collaboration between the designers and the building owner.  
The following aspects are worth to consider in the design process: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Heat balance 
ÔÇ∑ 
Thermal comfort requirements 
ÔÇ∑ 
Visual requirements 
ÔÇ∑ 
Daylight 
ÔÇ∑ 
Ultraviolet protection 
ÔÇ∑ 
Solar shading  
ÔÇ∑ 
Shading control  
ÔÇ∑ 
Acoustics 
ÔÇ∑ 
Energy requirements 
ÔÇ∑ 
Building orientation 
 
3.6.1.1 
Solar Heat Gain Coefficient (SHGC) 
In regards to cooling load it is important to determine the right Solar Heat Gain Coefficient (SHGC) for 
the windows, also known as the g-value in Danish contexts. The SHGC value is set to replace the Shading 
Coefficient (SC) which is defined as the ratio of solar heat gain through a given glazing compared to a 
reference glazing. 
SHGC indicates the ratio of total solar energy that is transmitted through the window. If SHGC=0 no 
energy is transmitted through the window and oppositely SHGC=1 all energy is transmitted. Therefore 
windows with low SHGC values are desirable in building with high cooling loads to help reduced the 
solar gains. Windows with high SHGC value is often desirable in buildings designed to utilize passive 
solar heating. However, it is important to notice that increasing SHGC results in decreasing light 
transmittance thus reduced daylight. 
 
3.6.1.2 
Light Transmittance  
The light transmittance (Tv or œÑv) gives the percentage of visible light that is transmitted through the glass. 
Sunlight is composed of a range of electromagnetic wavelengths as illustrated in Figure 5. Commonly it is 
divided into visible light, ultraviolet light (UV) and infrared light (IR). Visible light consists of the 
wavelengths detectable by the human eye containing about 47% of the energy in sunlight. UV consists of 
shorter wavelengths and is largely invisible to the naked eye, but it is responsible for fabric fading and 
skin damage. IR consists of longer wavelength which is invisible to the eye and contains about 46% of the 
energy in sunlight. 
==PAGE:44==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
43 of 168 
 
 
Figure 5: Spectrum of Solar Radiation (Wikipedia 2016) 
 
3.6.1.3 
Tints and Coating 
Tinting of the glass is mostly for aesthetic reasons, but some tints are able to reduce the solar radiation, 
typically by absorbing these wave lengths. Tinting can either be applied to the glass in the production or 
as colored window film afterwards.  
Coatings are most commonly a thin layer of metal oxides applied to surface of the glass. The most 
interesting coatings are the so called low-emissivity (low-e) or spectrally selective which effect on both 
the optical and the thermal properties of a glazing unit. It is able to block some or all IR wavelengths and 
thereby reduce the total radiant heat transfer to the inside.  
While the emissivity of normal glass is about 0.84, a low-e coating may have an emissivity of as low as 
0.03 (Babiak et al. 2013). The transmittance of visible light is about the same while the infrared is 
reduced relative to the uncoated glazing due to increased reflectance in the infrared wavelengths. 
Additionally, low-e coatings can also lower the U-value of the window. The coasting exists in various 
configurations with altered optical and emissive properties.  
For both tints and coatings attention should be paid to selecting the right types, as their application can 
radically change the heat loss and heat gain of a window. Wrong selection can result in the exact opposite 
of the desired performance. However, from the perspective of a consultant, specifying the windows U-
value, SHGC and light transmittance means there is no need to specify any specific tints and coatings. 
 
3.6.1.4 
Window film 
Window film is designed to reduce the amount of solar heat through the windows by increasing the 
reflection (the increase in reflection is not always visible), and reduce the amount of solar radiation 
passing through the glass. 
The classic colored film (and tints) types works by absorbing solar radiation. The color absorbs the solar 
energy at the glass, thus reducing the direct transmission into the space. These window films are not as 
effective as a reflective film to reduce heating (3M 2011). 
Traditionally, reflective films contain metals, such as aluminum and are designed to reflect certain 
wavelength of the solar radiation.  
==PAGE:45==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
44 of 168 
 
The state-of-art in window film utilizes non-metalized nano-technology to create reflectivity even lower 
than glass so limited sunlight is reflected off the building. The nano-film is spectrally-selective rejecting 
up to 97% of IR and 99.9% of UV. The total solar energy rejected is up to 61%, while it reduces the 
visible light transmission down to 63% (3M 2011). 
Quality, price, and properties variate greatly ‚Äì with many combinations of transmitted visible light, total 
rejected solar energy, external reflection, and glare reduction. It is therefore of outmost importance to 
evaluate the exact needs and consequences at every specific case in order to apply the best solution.  
Films can be a simple but great contributor to decrease the cooling load in many building types at a 
relatively low cost compared to external solar shading or double-skin fa√ßades. Attention should be paid to 
retaining a sufficient daylight factor due to the sometimes significant reduction in light transmittance 
through the windows.  
Window films are highly suitable for spaces, with large window areas and without requirements for a 
specific daylight factor, like atriums, auditoriums, showrooms, etc. One very beneficial effect of the film 
is that it does not compromise the view to the outside in comparison to most solar shading devices. 
It should be stated that window film cannot completely replace solar shading, but it should be seen as a 
supplement or as an alternative in places where solar shading is less attractive or comprehensive to install. 
In the future improvements in intelligent nano-films may be possible which adapt the heat rejection and 
light transmittance according total solar load. This will allow even less energy to enter at peak load and 
generally compromise the daylight less. This could reduce the cooling need significantly without 
installing solar shading and without issues with the daylight factor.  
 
3.6.1.5 
Electrochromic Glazing 
Electrochromic glazing is a dynamic glazing able to change opacity in response to voltage and thereby 
controlling the amount of light and heat passing through. Generally it offers the potential to modulate 
solar radiation through the glazing while maintaining a full view to the outdoors.  
Pilot projects have demonstrated lighting savings up to 60%, cooling load reduction up to 20%, and peak 
electricity reduction up to 26% (IEA 2013). More research and development is required are needed to 
improve electrochromic glazing and to make it more cost-effective.  
 
3.6.2 External Solar Shading 
External Solar Shading is the most effective method to significantly decrease direct and indirect solar 
radiation into the building. The majority of solar radiation is absorbed and reflected by the shading device 
and then the heat is transfers to the ambient air by convection. A small percentage is transferred to the 
window by long wave radiation. Furthermore, solar shading may also improve the performance of solar 
control glazing as well as reducing glare.  
It is essential to design any external shading device in correspondence to the glazing. Studies illustrated in 
Figure 6 have proven that external shadings (venetian blinds) can reduce the cooling load by up to 70% 
with double glazing (g=0.75), up to 50% with using low-e glazing (g=0.64) and up to 30% with solar 
control glazing (g=0.32) (Bertilsson et al. 2012). Significant optimization of the solar shading can be 
achieved by applying automatically controlled shadings. 
==PAGE:46==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
45 of 168 
 
 
Figure 6: Left: Cooling load for a simulated office in Madrid depending on window orientation. Solid lines are without shading 
and broken lines are with venetian blinds. Right: Reduction in primary energy usage for heating cooling and lighting with 
automatically controlled shading compared to no shading. Red is double glazing, Yellow  is low-e glazing and Blue solar control 
glazing (Bertilsson et al. 2012) 
 
When deciding upon external solar shading it is important to consider the climatic and especially the wind 
conditions (coastal/rural/urban). In relation to wind conditions the height of a building plays a significant 
role - tall buildings enhance the wind load on the fa√ßade creating extra difficult conditions for external 
solar shading. Buildings taller than roughly 15 stories most likely will not be able to have external 
shading due to wind load. Here solar control glazing, internal shading or double skin fa√ßade may be the 
possible alternatives.  
Additionally, external solar shading has to be coordinated with the strength of the structural components 
and the possibility to actually install it in a proper manner. After all the structure has to support and 
distribute the load from the shading device itself and more importantly also the wind, rain and snow 
loads.  
Various types of external solar shading are available - static and dynamic - each with its own advantages 
and disadvantages. The selection mostly depends on architectural preferences and building shape. A long 
list of options for external solar shading can be found in (Beck et al. 2010). 
 
3.6.2.1 
Static Shading 
Static solar shadings, also know have architectural shadings, are by nature not able to adapt to changing 
conditions. They are often seen as horizontal lamellas above the windows blocking the direct solar 
radiation. It is therefore not particularly effective when it comes to blocking indirect radiation and diffuse 
light.  
Furthermore, horizontal static shadings are proving little shading during winter time, at sun rise and sun 
set, due to the low position of the sun in the sky. A combination with solar control glazing and/or internal 
shadings could however be effective. 
 
3.6.2.2 
Dynamic Shading 
Dynamic or mechanical solar shadings are able to rapidly adapting to changes in solar radiation and 
weather conditions, enabling it to reduce the heat load and/or controlling the illuminance level when 
necessary. Dynamic shading exists in many forms covering all angels from vertical to horizontal.  
==PAGE:47==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
46 of 168 
 
Dynamic shadings are commonly sensitive to wind, rain, snow and frost ‚Äì vertical shadings are generally 
least sensitive. The construction is incapable of handling especially high wind pressures leaving the 
building either completely unprotected or completely closed in case of a blue sky with heavy wind 
conditions.  Therefore the majority of dynamic shadings are vertical systems able to operate in a wider 
range of external conditions. However, even vertical external shadings tend to struggle against the 
elements.  
 
3.6.2.3 
Challenges with External Solar Shading 
One of the major downsides of solar shading, especially dynamic vertical shadings, is that they very often 
fully or partly blocks the view to the outside and simultaneously reduces the incoming daylight to a 
minimum leading to poor view to the outside and extra energy for artificial lighting.  
Careful attention should also be paid to avoid unwanted reflections from solar shading system to the 
surrounding. Generally, solar shading in any form should be installed in ways that does not create worse 
conditions for the surrounds than that of the existing window reflections. The existing windows should 
neither provide significant reflections to the surroundings.  
Mechanical shadings need to be robust and able to resist various external weather conditions. 
Consequently, they are liable to frequent damage leading to high maintenance and/or replacement costs. 
In case of renovation architectural regulations from the local authority may prohibit the application of 
exterior shading on for instance historical buildings. However, if allowed solar shading may be the best 
solution for achieving better indoor comfort and reduce the heat load without installing expensive cooling 
applications. The payback in renovation projects can only come from improved comfort and health, and 
increased productivity. 
 
3.6.3 Internal Solar Shading 
Internal Solar Shading is the least effective method against solar radiation. If internal shading is installed 
as the sole mean against the sun one will most certainly face an overheating problem or large energy 
consumption from cooling applications. The incoming solar radiation, especially the high energy short 
wave, will mainly be absorbed by the blinds and the heat will be released by convection and long wave 
radiation to the space. 
Internal shading devices should therefore only be used to control glare and light intensity from diffuse and 
reflective lights. Both glare and high light intensity is undesirable and causes visual discomfort for the 
occupants due to high luminance ratio within the field of view.  
Internal curtains and blinds can be controlled automatically and/or manually which usually satisfies the 
occupants in terms of perceived control of the indoor environment. Additionally, internal shading devices 
are relatively cheap and very efficient at controlling glare compared to external shading. 
For north facing facades no significant solar radiation is present and therefore only internal shading for 
glare protection and for adjustment of light intensity may be sufficient.  
 
==PAGE:48==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
47 of 168 
 
3.6.4 Intermediate Solar Shading (Double-skin Fa√ßade) 
Intermediate Solar Shading, also known as a Double Skin 
Fa√ßade (DSF), is somewhat a combination of external and 
internal solar shading. It is not the most common fa√ßade type 
and it is mostly seen in taller modern buildings with all glazed 
facades. 
Architects are usually appreciating DSF for allowing creative 
three-dimensional expression on the exterior while keeping the 
load-bearing structure and building fairly simple. 
DSF consists of an external glazing acting as a barrier against 
the element, a ventilated cavity and an internal glazing acting 
as the primary thermal barrier.  
A mechanical solar shading, either lamella or fabric, is 
installed in the cavity and is thereby protected from any 
external weather conditions by the external fa√ßade. The solar 
radiation is then absorbed by the shading system in the cavity 
and the heat is therefore removed by ventilation ‚Äì giving it 
basically the same heat rejecting properties as external 
shading.  
This construction will result in increased SHGC, reduced light 
transmittance to the space, higher U-value and better sound 
insulation of the fa√ßade, thus decreased heat load to the space.  
The cavity may become very hot and thereby increase indirect heat gains. Therefore, it is important to 
reduce the heat build-up in the cavity by proper ventilation and ensuring heat does not enter the building 
unintendedly. In winter the build-up may contribute to a reduction in heating demand, while in summer 
the heat build-up should be removed to prevent an increased cooling demand. 
The cavity is either ventilated naturally or mechanically to remove the absorbed heat as illustrated in 
Figure 7: 
Naturally ventilated DSF (interactive fa√ßade) typically consist of a single layer external glazing and a 
double internal glazing. The cavity is naturally ventilated with outdoor air supplied at the base and 
extracted at the top of the entire fa√ßade or sections of the fa√ßade. 
Mechanically ventilated DSF (active fa√ßades) consist typically of a double glazing external and a single 
layer glazing internal. The cavity is ventilated e.g. with air extracted from the space supplied at the base 
and returned to the AHU or exhausted to the outdoor at the top depending on the situation. 
Studies indicates that naturally ventilated DSF with single glazing external and double glazing internally 
is superior to mechanically ventilated in terms of yearly cooling load (Beck et al. 2010). However, the 
study also indicated that a standard fa√ßade with double glazing and external shading is performing slightly 
better than the naturally ventilated DSF.  
The overall performance of a DSF system is depended on various design options including type of 
glazing, type of blind, color of the blind, cavity geometry, airflow rate in the cavity and so forth. For 
instance darker blinds absorb more heat while lighter blinds reflect more radiation, both influencing the 
temperature of the blind and the temperature build-up inside the cavity creating different air flows. 
 
 
Figure 7: Ventilation flow of a double skin 
fa√ßade with natural ventilation (left) and 
mechanical ventilation (right). Exterior is left 
and internal is right (Graca, Chen, and Anderson 
2015) 
==PAGE:49==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
48 of 168 
 
3.6.4.1 
Challenges with Double Skin Fa√ßades 
DSF may seem like the best solar shading solution but several issues have to be addressed. However, it 
may be the most optimal solution for tall buildings due to issues with high wind load on external 
shadings. Designers and architects have to keep in mind that the light transmittance is permanently 
decreased due to the extra layer of glazing making it difficult to achieve a sufficient daylight levels. 
The price of DSF compared to a traditional fa√ßade may increase by 200 to 700 EUR/m¬≤ or 30 to 100% 
plus raising the maintenance-costs (Graca, Chen, and Anderson 2015). Developments in fa√ßades made of 
transparent ETFE-foils weighting only about 1% of glass offering improvements in fire protection, noise 
reflection, load optimization and consequently a significant cost reduction (Graca, Chen, and Anderson 
2015).  
The higher initial investment cost of DSF due to the extra layer of fa√ßade without achieving significant 
better results compared to traditional external shading must be held up against the protection and 
occasionally limited operation of standard external shadings. 
A significant issue with DSF is its obvious maintenance problems. Cleaning of the cavity including the 
glazing facing the cavity can be extremely difficult and special means for this have to be implemented 
which further increases the investment cost. The internal glazing may be openable for cleaning and 
maintenance purposes which also may allow for natural ventilation of the indoor environment. However, 
if aiming for natural ventilation problems with high air velocity, high supply temperature and noise may 
be a reality.  
If the DSF is no separated for each floor practical issues as such noise reflection, fire protection and 
thermal stratification inside the cavity poses considerable limitations to the design; especially if natural 
ventilation is intended by window opening. 
Overall there are still some issues with DSF and a broad range of possible improvements in terms of 
functionality and cost effectiveness.  
 
==PAGE:50==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
49 of 168 
 
3.6.5 Sensors and control 
To optimize the indoor environment, the cooling loads and the energy consumption, it is essential to 
integrate sensors into a Building Management System (BMS) in order to control both external and 
internal solar shading.  
Basically internal, intermediate and external solar shadings could be manually controlled by the 
occupants. It might be a good thing for some occupants to provide a perceived control of the environment 
but far from optimal in an energy and maintenance perspective.  
Automatically controlled shadings assisted by various sensors and with limited user interactions are the 
optimal solution for a long range of buildings. Automatic systems are able to react fast and precise to 
changing conditions and adjust when no occupants are present. Firstly, this should greatly assist providing 
a better indoor environment with less glare and improved illuminance level. Secondly, it should decrease 
the solar gains in the most optimal way in order to reduce overheating and realize energy savings for 
cooling. Thirdly, it will secure the shadings against severe external conditions and thereby reduce 
maintenance by limiting damage caused by wind, rain and snow. 
In order to track the external conditions the system needs a range of different sensors. Traditionally, only 
a solar sensor and a wind sensor was installed to inform the external shadings if solar radiations is present 
or if the wind load is too high and then retract. It can be a simple stand-alone installation working without 
a BMS. However, it does not always react in the most optimal way in regard to energy efficiency and 
indoor environment. Therefore, today a long range of parameters may be taken into account via a BMS to 
control the interaction between solar shading, lighting, glare, heating, ventilating, and cooling. It can be a 
complex task to connect everything and mostly only certain parameters which are important to the 
building or the building owner are taken into account.  
The control of dynamic shadings is alpha and omega. One has to keep in mind that it is rarely civil 
engineers programming the actual BMS but ordinary programmers who do not necessary know anything 
about building energy or indoor comfort they just connect the parameter given but the building designers. 
If the building designers are not careful in their system descriptions it may lead to very simple controls 
resulting in unnecessary energy consumption and discomfort for the occupants. 
Appreciatively, integrated intelligent smart-solutions in every building application are in a rapid 
development allowing much easier programming, more plug-and-play and most likely better control of 
the often very complex mechanisms.  
The following will consist of the basic but nevertheless most important information regarding various 
sensors types: 
Solar Sensor: Measure incoming actual solar radiation on a surface to determine the light intensity and 
heat effect. Multiple sensors may be needed on each fa√ßade depending on design and size of the fa√ßade.  
Wind Sensor (anemometer): Measure wind speed to determine the actual wind load on external 
shadings in order to protect of the shading device by telling which positions is possible (open, close, 
intermediate) to avoid damages. Multiple sensors may be needed depending on design and size of the 
building.  
Wind Direction Sensor: Measure average wind direction to determine which fa√ßades are affected by 
wind and thereby whether the shading should be active or retract due to wind load. Multiple sensors may 
be needed depending on design and size of the building.  
Rain Sensor: Measuring rain or no rain. Some shading devices are sensitive to rain and should retract due 
to intensive rain. Multiple sensors may be needed depending on design and size of the building.  
==PAGE:51==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
50 of 168 
 
Temperature Sensor: Measure outdoor and indoor temperatures. Shadings can be programmed to only 
work at certain indoor or outdoor temperatures, or in a combination between indoor and outdoor 
temperatures. Furthermore, some shadings do not work or can be damaged in frost weather.  
Occupancy detector: Measure the presence of human (activity) inside a space to enable external 
shadings to be active in cooling mode even if no occupants are present in order to save energy or inactive 
in heating mode if sufficient solar radiation is available.   
Illuminance sensor: Measure the illuminance level inside a space to determine whether to 
activate/deactivate internal blinds, external shadings and/or artificial lighting.  
 
3.6.5.1 
User Interaction 
User interaction on the shading control is a good way to satisfy the occupants in terms of perceived 
control on the indoor environment. The interaction could allow the occupants to control both internal and 
external shadings by pushing a button to override the BMS. However, it is often a very personal opinion 
of one or a few occupants and may work best in single offices.  
User interaction has many side effects; for instance it could oppose the effect of a cooling system if the 
user interaction and the cooling system are not connected thus supplying extensive amount of cooling the 
space. 
To the untrained eye it is not always possible to see or guess why the shadings are on or off. With more 
sophisticated control strategies follows therefore more confused occupants due to lack of knowledge on 
solar shading which in the end may lead to dissatisfaction. Therefore, it can be a very good idea to make 
relevant information on the controls and function easily accessible to the occupants and educate then in 
using the system properly.  
A way to enable user interaction without risking the energy consumption or the indoor environment is by 
utilizing the above mentioned sensors to control the shadings during predefined and acute peak load 
conditions and let the users be able to override the settings during mild conditions e.g. no direct sun or 
low indoor temperature. The system should however be able to override the user interaction when 
conditions are changing to the worse. The mix between system control and user interaction can variate 
greatly according to building type, location, user pattern, and so forth. Therefore, each case should be 
handled individually by evaluating pros and cons. No solution is completely perfect, but every solution 
can be optimized. 
 
 
==PAGE:52==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
51 of 168 
 
3.7 Recommendations 
This section will summarize the described preliminary actions and highlight the recommended aspects.   
Cooling of buildings starts with the preliminary actions. The architectural building design has a 
significant influence of the cooling load and the efficiency of the cooling systems.  
The overall target for the architectural design process is to control the solar radiation since it usually 
contributes with the highest heat load. Geographical location does not play a significant role in regards to 
solar heat load and therefore it is a relevant subject everywhere. The orientation, shape and layout of a 
building should be seen as a holistic process with increased focus on energy requirements, indoor 
environment, and solar shading. Therefore, the engineers have to be involved in the process at an early 
stage to guide the architecture to an energy efficient design. 
Number one rule of thumb when designing buildings and especially cooling systems is to keep the solar 
radiation as far from the indoor environment as possible while allowing sufficient visual light into the 
building; i.e. use external shadings.  
Dynamic external solar shading is the most effective method for reduction of cooling, occupant comfort 
and energy savings by modulating the amount of incoming solar radiation.  
Internal shadings should not be used as solar shading, but it is the best choice to adjust glare and 
illuminance level in the space. A combination of internal and external shading may be the best overall 
solution.   
Nano-film for windows is an interesting alternative or supplement to external solar shading to reduce the 
solar load without compromising the view to the outside or the looks of the fa√ßade. It is especially 
attractive to buildings with large glazed fa√ßades with reductions of the solar energy on more than 60% 
may be realistic.  
Solar control glazing should reject as much heat as possible, while transmitting sufficient levels of visible 
light. Combining solar control glazing and exterior architectural shading offers an improved solution. 
Double skin fa√ßades have a promising future for almost any types of buildings. However, it still has some 
issues which have to be resolved to make it more mainstream and cost effective.  
One of the largest challenges with solar shading is often the partly or completely interrupted view to the 
outside which compromises the visual comfort of the occupants for longer periods each day.  
Currently, there is no optimal way to satisfy the cooling demand, while obtaining a good indoor 
environment with low glare, constant illuminance level, high daylight levels and achieve an 
uncompromised view to the outside.  
Additional recommendations for windows and glazing in relation to cooling load 
ÔÇ∑ 
High light transmittance (>70%) is desirable for increased daylight. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Low SHGC values (<0.40) is desirable to decreased solar heat load in buildings with large glazed 
facades thus significant reduced heat loads.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Spectrally selective glazing has a relatively high visible transmittance and a relatively low SHGC 
which benefits the heat load but is constrained by its cost and less transparent nature. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Low SHGC glazing should be considered for east and west fa√ßades in combination with internal 
shading as a means of controlling solar heat gain and increasing occupant comfort.  
ÔÇ∑ 
North-facing windows are not critical in most northern countries and internal shading may be 
sufficient. 
ÔÇ∑ 
South-facing windows will most likely benefit most from external solar shading in combination 
with internal shadings. 
==PAGE:53==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
52 of 168 
 
4 Supply of Cooling 
This chapter focuses on the concepts and the devices utilized for supplying cooling to a space. Also, 
consequences for architecture, constructions and associated installations are evaluated. The focus will be 
on sustainable building utilizing high temperature cooling. 
Different cooling strategies can have remarkably different effects on thermal comfort. Depending on the 
use of a space, criteria such as draft, noise, radiant asymmetry, vertical air temperature difference, etc. 
may become limiting factors in the selection of a supply strategy.  
At lower heat loads the sensible heat load in a space is typically handled by basic air supply necessary to 
maintain a sufficient IAQ. At higher loads a balance between a local thermal unit and the basic air supply 
will often be more adequate in terms of thermal comfort, energy efficiency and spatial requirements. 
For cooling it is desirable to achieve high supply temperatures close to the indoor temperature in order to 
reduce the risk of thermal discomfort and to reduce the energy used to cool the supply media. However, a 
high supply temperature results in a lower cooling capacity, therefore more air or water have to be 
supplied to the space and potentially the supply area has to be increased. Thereby the energy saved in 
producing a less cold medium has to be weighed against the extra auxiliary energy consumption and the 
potentially increase in investment cost for the installation.  
Lastly, it is important to consider control possibilities and dynamic behavior of the supply devices. Some 
systems try to obtain a specific temperature in the space and have fast reaction time (e.g. ventilation 
system), other try to obtain temperature sliding inside a range but with slow reaction time (e.g. TABS).  
The selection of supply devices in the space may be the most important, but it is far from the only part in 
designing the most optimal overall cooling system solution. Equally important to evaluate are the 
transportation and auxiliary energy consumption associated with the supply devices from fans, pumps, 
valves, sensors, dampers, and so forth. A wrong or incorrect design in the distribution system could lead 
to massive unnecessary energy consumption.  
Also important is the availability of energy sources and sinks; is it possible to utilize natural resources 
like sea water, ground water, soil and wind, is district cooling available,  or is refrigeration systems the 
best option? Based on the available sources it is possible to decide upon the cooling supply temperature 
set for the building or vice visa.  
On basis of the possible temperature set the range of options for selecting supply devices will be limited 
due to certain operational constraints for each device. Additionally, the temperature set will indicate the 
potential sizes of supply devices, pipes, ducts, etc. to be used in the overall building design process.  
In this section the cooling capacities of the supply systems will be evaluated based on a W/m2 value and 
is typically based on the performance in spaces with normal room height 2.5-3.5 meter.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:54==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
53 of 168 
 
4.1 Background 
The HVAC industry in Denmark tends to use the same few solutions for cooling over and over because 
these solutions are well tested and everyone have adequate experience with designing, dimensioning, 
constructing and maintaining them. Occasionally, the solutions get improved by the manufactures due to 
technological advancements. Roughly speaking, if a solution has proven to work in one case then why 
change it to the next especially if this solution keeps improving ‚Äì If it ain‚Äôt broke, don‚Äôt fix it! 
Interestingly, similar tendencies can be found in every region of the world. The difference is the type of 
systems which variate mostly depending on the climate but just as much on the local regulations and 
coincidences combined with the skills and knowledge of local manufacture‚Äôs and entrepreneurs.  
Clearly new alternative solutions emerge once in a while and are incorporated in the industry. However, 
the actual number of suitable alternatives offered by manufactures is much broader than what is 
incorporated in the building mass. If also including the alternatives on the drawing broad which are 
theoretically and experimentally tested by various universities the range of alternative could expand even 
more. 
 
4.2 Media 
Heat energy is transferred as a result of temperature differences passing from warm to cold. The greater 
temperature difference the greater heat transfer. Therefore, in order to extract heat from a space a colder 
medium/source has to be available.  
Overall there are two solutions for supply of cooling; by air and by fluids ‚Äì primarily water but also 
brines. Generally air is massively outperformed by fluids as heat carrier. Water is a much more efficient 
heat carrier than air with a specific heat capacity of water 4.2 times higher than that of air. Therefore, 
higher airflow rate and consequently large duct works are needed to provide the same cooling effect as 
water.  
Table 24: Thermodynamic properties at 20 oC 
 
Density
Specific heat capacity
[kg/m3]
[kJ/kgK]
Water 
1000 
4.200 
Air 
1.2 
1.005 
 
Air can be applied directly to a space whereas fluids have to be applied indirectly through heat 
exchangers to air or materials in contact to the space. Additionally, the air mostly has to be cooled by a 
colder fluid before supplied to a space which necessitates installation of a refrigeration system. 
 
 
==PAGE:55==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
54 of 168 
 
4.3 Cooling Strategy 
Ventilation should be present in any building regardless of type or cooling demand to ensure a satisfying, 
healthy and comfortable indoor environment in the occupied spaces. The ventilation system should as a 
minimum be designed to supply enough air to a space to maintain a sufficient IAQ, to maintain a 
satisfying CO2 concentration and to dilute and remove the generated pollution according to design criteria 
for use of the space, number of occupants, equipment, furnishing and so forth. This minimum air change 
rate will from here be referred to as the basic air change rate. 
The supply air is medium carrying energy affecting the condition in a direction depending on its 
temperature. If the sole aim is to provide fresh air without any risk of thermal discomfort the supply 
temperature should be equal to or slightly colder than the room temperature.  
In northern Europe the outdoor air temperature is rarely above the indoor comfort range (~22-26oC) and 
thereby unconditioned air will mostly have a cooling effect on the indoor temperature. Therefore, the 
basic air change rate should always be seen as the initial part of the cooling strategy! 
In many cases the basic air change rate supplied with unconditioned air is rarely sufficient to maintain 
indoor temperatures within the comfort range at all times - especially during warm periods with outdoor 
temperatures within or even above the comfort range. However, the time thermal comfort conditions are 
outside the comfort range has to be evaluated for each case before applying additional means of cooling.  
The following four options are available to increase the cooling capacity to maintain temperatures in a 
space: 
1. Increase air change rate 
2. Decrease air supply temperature 
3. Allow extended exceedance of indoor temperature outside the comfort range 
4. Install additional cooling applications 
 
4.3.1 Design Process 
In the design process for new buildings, the designers need to make early estimates of whether the basic 
air change rate is sufficient or insufficient to obtain satisfying indoor temperatures and IAQ.  
If sufficient, the designers can start to feed the architects and the other designers with sizes of ducts, 
shafts, technical rooms, required space above suspended ceiling, etc. and thereby go into more detailed 
calculations and design, as well as planning and coordination in between the subjects. 
If insufficient, the designer needs to evaluate mainly in collaboration with the architect and the client how 
to approach this issue with the above mentioned options.  
An increase in air flow rate will require larger (or extra) ducts, shafts, AHUs and air terminals in order to 
maintain acceptable energy consumption. All these parameters have a clear influence on the architecture 
and the building layout. Depending on the increase in size or numbers it may not always be seen lightly 
upon from the point of view of the architect and the owner.  
A decrease in supply temperature requires installation of cooling coils in the AHU and/or decentralized in 
connection to the spaces. The cooling coils require a chilled water circuit connected to a cooling unit; e.g. 
heat pump or refrigeration plant. The cooling unit is typically placed in a separate technical room and its 
corresponding dry cooler or cooling tower is placed outdoors (e.g. on the roof). If only decreasing the 
supply temperature the sizes of ducts, shafts and partly the AHU remain unchanged compared to the basic 
air change rate, but extra space for installations such as pipes, chillers, cooling towers, dry coolers, heat 
pumps, etc. is required.  
==PAGE:56==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
55 of 168 
 
A combination of decreased supply temperature and increased air flow rate is the most common way to 
increase the cooling capacity. However, at a certain point when further increasing the cooling capacity 
this combination will require too much space, in terms of construction height, shaft space and size of 
AHU to enable proper integration into most buildings. This naturally depends on a long range of 
parameters and should be investigated for each specific case. 
To further increase the cooling capacity without challenging the construction and the architecture, it is 
recommended to integrate fluid based local thermal units in a space to assist the selected ventilation 
strategy.  
 
4.3.2 Minimum Thermal Resistance Principle 
A useful tool in order to predict and optimize the overall effectiveness of a cooling system is to apply the 
minimum thermal resistance principle. It evaluates the total equivalent thermal resistance from heat 
sources to heat sink by adding the resistances for all heat transfer processes in the supply chain. 
Therefore, for a given set of constraints the heat transfer process is optimized when the thermal resistance 
is minimized. 
‹¥‡Øò‡Ø§,‡Øß‡Ø¢‡Øß= ‡∑ç‹¥‡¨µ+ ‹¥‡¨∂+ ‹¥‡¨∑+. . . +‹¥‡Øú 
Simplifying the heat transfer process from source to sink by reducing the number of heat transfers and/or 
optimizing each heat transfer process is best the ways to reduce thermal resistance. A decreased resistance 
results in either increased cooling capacity in the condition of identical temperature difference or 
increased chilled water temperature in the condition of identical cooling capacity.  
As illustrated in Figure 8, the equivalent thermal resistance of a radiant floor system is lower than that of 
a ventilation system. In this case it allows higher chilled water temperatures in the radiant system which 
essentially will reduce energy consumption for the refrigeration system.  
 
 
Figure 8: T-Q diagram of equivalent thermal resistance for a ventilation system(left) and a floor radiant system(right) (EBC 2015) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:57==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
56 of 168 
 
4.3.2.1 
Ventilation system 
The equivalent thermal resistance of a ventilation system consists of three resistances. R1 is the resistance 
caused by convective heat transfer from heat source to indoor air. R2 is the resistance caused by mixing 
supply air and indoor air. R3 is the resistance caused by heat transfer between supply air and chilled water 
through a cooling coil in the AHU. The resistances can be calculated as following (EBC 2015): 
 
 
From this, three parametric variations are potentially possible; indoor temperature, flow rate and size of 
cooling coil (UA). The indoor temperature is bound to a thermal comfort range and an increase will 
reduce R1. The flow rate is related to the energy consumption of fans and the size of ducts and other 
components and an increase in flow rate will reduce R2. The size of cooling coils is a matter of available 
space at the AHU and initial cost and an increase in size will reduce R3. And vice versa! 
 
4.3.2.2 
Radiant system 
The equivalent thermal resistance of radiant system consists of two resistances. R1 is the resistance caused 
by radiant heat transfer to the surface and convection between the surface and the indoor air. R2 is the 
resistance caused by the conduction from chilled water to the surface. The resistances can be calculated as 
following (EBC 2015): 
 
 
The equivalent thermal resistance for radiant floor system can be considerably reduced if the floor is 
directly exposed to solar radiation and thereby there is only one step from source to sink

4.4 Compatibility  
Various combinations of thermal units and ventilation strategies are feasible. Some combinations of 
systems have obvious synergy effects while other systems should not be combined due to e.g. 
counteracting air flows or risk of condensation.  
The overall compatibility between the systems included in this report can be seen in Table 25 with more 
detailed description under the specific section for each system type. The table includes a primary system 
supplemented by one or more secondary systems. Here four levels of compatibility have been identified: 
ÔÇ∑

ÔÇ∑

free interaction with the primary system. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Limited (yellow) systems can be integrated in some cases with certain precautions and 
limitations. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Potential (*) systems are identified as potentially recommended solutions but specific examples 
could not be found in the literature or directly at any manufacture.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Not Recommended (red) the secondary system is either counteracting the primary system, 
problematic in terms of condensation risk, or not possible to integrate simultaneously.

4.5 Ventilative Cooling 
Ventilative Cooling refers to the use of natural or mechanical ventilation strategies to cool indoor spaces 
and it is the most widely spread form of cooling strategy due to its undeniable presence in all buildings to 
at least ensure sufficient IAQ.  
This chapter will contain guidelines and design criteria for a broad range of ventilative cooling strategies 
such as mixing, natural, hybrid, displacement, piston flow, personalized, fan coil, chilled beam, diffuse 
ceilings, cool ceiling and textile based ventilation.  
The main method of heat emission and removal for ventilative systems is by convection. Depending on 
the supply air temperature the systems act as a cooling or heating system.  
The effect can be adjusted according to varying loads by adjusting the air flow rate and/or the supply 
temperature. The often colder outdoor air can be effectively utilized to maintain indoor temperatures 
without presence of additional mechanical cooling. Especially night ventilation strategy utilizing the free 
cooling potential of the outdoor air can be beneficial for the energy consumption of a building.  
Ventilative cooling can be applied to a wide range of spaces. But in practice, it can be difficult for the 
designers to select the most effective and efficient ventilation strategy. The selection of ventilative 
cooling systems depend on dimension of the space, heat gains, pollutants, possible air flow rate, air 
distribution pattern, thermal capacity of the constructions, heat transfer ability between air and 
construction, use of space and requirements for the thermal comfort. A combination of ventilative cooling 
strategies may have a better performance than utilizing just one strategy. 
The following ventilative strategies are outside the scope of this thesis; Stratum Ventilation, Protective 
Occupied zone Ventilation, Impinging Jets, Confluent Jets, and Local Exhaust Ventilation. 
 
4.5.1 Climatic Cooling Potential 
The current climate and the future climate changes play a significant role in the selection of cooling 
strategy. The effectiveness of ventilative cooling systems greatly relies on the difference between indoor 
and outdoor temperatures and for some system also the strength of the natural driving forces.  
The cooling effectiveness is high during cold periods but with increased risk of draught and temperature 
asymmetry. Therefore the supply solution should be able to handle this issue with care. During warm 
periods the ventilative cooling may be completely unavailable in daytime and may therefore be limited to 
night-time supply where the accumulated heat gains during the daytime can be removed. However, a 
warmer climate will eventually result in an increased demand for artificial cooling since the reliability of 
free cooling will be reduced.  
The most interesting and widely usable is the night-time cooling potential. Currently, free cooling 
potential for ventilative systems are great during the majority of the year in Northern Europe. The 
Climatic Cooling Potential (CCP) for night time throughout Europe during July is displayed in Figure 9 
indicating a very significant CCP for night ventilation in Northern Europe and therefore night-time 
ventilation strategies seems to be applicable in most cases. 
==PAGE:60==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
59 of 168 
 
The daily Climatic Cooling Potential CCPd for a space is based on the degree-hours method and is 
describing the thermal build-up over a period of time (Graca, Chen, and Anderson 2015):  
‹•‹•‹≤‡Øó=
‹≥‚àô›ê
‹∏‚àô›ä‚àô‹£‹•‹™‚àôﬂ©‚àô‹ø‡Ø£
 [‹≠‚Ñé] 
Where; ‹≥‡Øó is heat gain [W/m2K], ›ä is temperature efficiency (›ä=
‡Øç‡≥ö‡≥†‡≥ü‡¨ø‡Øç‡≥ê
‡Øç‡≥ç‡¨ø‡Øç‡≥ê), ACH is air change rate [h-1], t 
is occupancy time [h], V is volume of the space [m3], ﬂ© is air density (1.2 kg/m3), and ‹ø‡Ø£ is air heat 
capacity (1000 J/kgK). 
If the CCPd is smaller or equal to the CCP it is theoretically possible to remove all the heat generated 
during the day only by an effective night cooling strategy. Otherwise additional means of cooling should 
be incorporated.  
It is important to note that even though the global warming is increasing the average temperatures and 
thereby decreasing the effectiveness of ventilative cooling, night cooling strategies still remains an 
attractive solution for northern countries either alone or in hybrid systems. For mid and southern countries 
the ventilative cooling potential is already very low during summer and will most likely keep decreasing 
in the future. Therefore ventilative cooling strategies are less attractive since it will only be possible in 
parts of the transition season and in the winter.  
The CCP and the cooling demands are opposing each other - rising average temperatures results in lower 
CCP and higher cooling demands, and vice versa. Global warming may increase the peak cooling load 
and the number of peaks and also extend the cooling season further into both spring and autumn, which 
thereby further limits the use and effectiveness of ventilative cooling. 
It should be mentioned that the conditions of the future climate holds large uncertainties which leads to 
extreme difficulties when estimating if ventilative cooling is the wrong choice over the lifetime of a 
building. One thing is for sure, the possible changes needs to be taken into account when selecting and 
designing ventilative cooling solutions by making sensitivity analysis between the outdoor temperature, 
the energy consumption and the cooling capacity. If the system design relies too much on the current free 
cooling potential it may provide insufficient cooling or higher energy consumption in the future.

4.5.2 Total Volume Ventilation 
Total Volume Ventilation (TVV) is the overall description of all mixing and displacement air distribution 
strategies and it indicates that the entire space is ventilated. As displayed in Figure 10 the distribution can 
either be driven by buoyancy or momentum flow, and various subcategories can be identified.  
Selection of ventilation strategy should evaluate the comprehensive conditions in the space and include 
various design parameters as such internal loads, external loads, thermal plumes, air flow rates, 
temperatures, ventilation efficiency, removal of contaminants, potential energy saving and so forth. 
 
 
Figure 10: Overview of total volume air distribution (Bolashikov 2015) 
 
4.5.2.1 
Air Supply 
The supply temperature and the flow rate should be able to varies according to changing loads in order to 
obtain the most optimal indoor environment in an energy efficient way. Intake air is cooled or heated 
depending on external condition to a point neutral or slightly cooler than the indoor comfort temperature 
typically to between 16 and 24oC, depending on internal heat loads and system type, before it is supplied 
to the occupied space. 
The initial air velocity varies greatly depending on the system type whether it is MV, DV or DIFCV. 
Common for all is they are restricted in the occupied zone to maximum of 0.15-0.20 m/s. Additionally it 
is important to ensure a sufficient low draught rating. Therefore, the location and type of air supply 
openings or air supply diffusers play a major role for air distribution in spaces. 
==PAGE:62==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
61 of 168 
 
 
 
Figure 11: Dynamic interaction between air flow and heat sources (Melikov 2015) 
Furthermore, as illustrated in Figure 11 the dynamic interactions between air flow distribution, action of 
the occupants and solar gains are underestimated, e.g. the movement of occupants and the presence of 
heat sources at different conditions (Cao et al. 2014). Consequently, the thermal plumes may cause larger 
local temperature gradient and higher local air velocity, which can influence the air distribution and 
potentially cause local thermal discomfort. Therefore, the design of air flow distribution should take into 
account both air velocity and the interaction with thermal plumes. 
 
4.5.2.2 
Cooling Effect 
The cooling effect from a given air flow or vice visa can be calculated as following:
