Where; ﬂù‡Øß is heat removal efficiency, ›ç‡Ø© is air flow,  ﬂ©= is density, ‹ø‡Ø£ is specific heat capacity, ‹∂‡Øò‡Ø´ is 
exhaust temperature, and ‹∂‡Ø¶ is supply temperature. 
 
4.5.2.3 
Heat Removal Efficiency 
An index to refers the temperature distribution which is defined in terms of temperature within the space 
and called ventilation effectiveness for heat removal (Awbi & Gan 1993). The ventilation efficiency for 
the removal of heat is defined as: 
ﬂù‡Øß= ‹∂‡Øò‡Ø´‚àí‹∂‡Ø¶
‹∂‡Ø•‚àí‹∂‡Ø¶
 
Where; Tex is temperature of the exhaust air, Ts the temperature in the supply air, and Tr the temperature in 
the occupied zone. 
ﬂù‡Øß is used to describe how efficient a ventilation system is to remove heat from a space and how well the 
air is mixed. ﬂù‡Øß on 100% indicates that the extracted air has the same temperature as the occupied space 
which typically indicates a very good mixing of the air or zero heat gains in the space. Oppositely, ﬂù‡Øß on 
200% indicates very high temperature differences within the space and thereby incomplete mixing of the 
air or high level of stratification.  
In general, displacement ventilation has a ﬂù‡Øß> 100% and mixing ventilation has a ﬂù‡Øß‚â§100%(Lindab 
2008). For well-designed ventilative cooling systems in spaces with normal room height (2.5-3.5 m) ﬂù‡Øß 
should, depending on the system configuration and load case, be in the range of 100-150% at heat loads 
from 0 to 160 W/m2 (Nickel 2015).  
==PAGE:63==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
62 of 168 
 
For spaces with exhaust placed in the upper part ﬂù‡Øß will typically increase with increased room height 
when the load is constant due to possibility of more heat accumulation in the upper part of the space and 
better stratification. 
 
4.5.2.4 
Coanda Effect  
The Coanda effect has a great influence on ventilative cooling and the air distribution. When air is 
supplied along a surface, e.g. ceiling, a vacuum will be established between the jet and the ceiling making 
the jet stick to the ceiling as illustrated in Figure 12. The Coanda effect increases the throw length 
resulting in decreased risk of cold drop. In order to achieve the greatest possible Coanda effect air should 
be supplied in smaller quantities with as large a spread as possible on the ceiling, preferably with a 360Àö 
pattern, and with the greatest possible velocity. If the diffuser is placed too far from the ceiling the 
Coanda effect will be absent. 
 
Figure 12: Coanda effect (Lindab 2008) 
4.5.2.5 
Design chart  
An example of a design chart for ventilative cooling is displayed in Figure 13. The chart is based on the 
minimum and maximum allowable flow rate to a space. As indicated it is necessary to have a minimum 
flow rate of fresh air to obtain a sufficient air quality. Furthermore, it illustrates a maximum temperature 
difference between return and supply to avoid draught and/or too large temperature differences.  
 
Figure 13: Design chart of ventilative systems where ‚àÜTo is temperature difference between exhaust and supply air and q is 
supply air flow rate 
 
 
==PAGE:64==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
63 of 168 
 
4.5.3 Natural Ventilation (NV) 
Natural ventilation (NV) is the oldest principle for supplying fresh air to a space. The very basic version 
of NV consists of manually openable windows or louvres. The most interesting and modern version is to 
apply mechanical actuators to control the indoor environment according to internal and external 
parameters such as temperature, CO2 concentration and wind load.  
NV can be an effective method for passive cooling with outdoor air ‚Äì almost energy neutral. In buildings 
with surplus heat during the occupied time it can be beneficial for the energy consumption to use NV 
during all period of the year whenever peak loads occurs and to the extent where the indoor thermal 
comfort is still acceptable. Especially during summer, NV can be the sole source of fresh air supply since 
the outdoor temperature is mostly above the acceptable temperature for supply air.  
NV is mostly applicable as a supplement to mechanical ventilation to enhance the energy performance of 
buildings with high requirements for the indoor environment (See Hybrid Ventilation).  
Furthermore, night cooling of the building by NV can be an effective measure to reduce/remove the 
accumulated surplus heat from the building mass and thereby contribute to reducing the active cooling 
measures during the day time or simply avoiding active cooling measures and still keep the temperature 
within the comfort range. Night-time ventilation strategies are enhanced by the exposure of heavy 
building parts (e.g. concrete walls) combined with a relatively high air change rate 4-6 h-1 (Dansk 
Standard 2013). 
 
Figure 14: Natural Ventilation (Bolashikov 2015) 
4.5.3.1 
Potential and Limitation of Natural Ventilation 
With an energy perspective NV is an excellent choice for large periods of the year to utilize the climatic 
cooling potential of the outdoor air.  
Well-designed NV systems operate typically with pressure drops of 1 Pa and 2 Pa.  A pressure drop of 
only 1 Pa may drive an air flow through a 1 m¬≤ clear opening of an air flow rate magnitude up to 2.800 
m¬≥/h (Graca et al. 2015). 
The building shape and orientation combined with the size, shape and placement of openings have 
significantly influence on the operation of natural ventilation. Building with NV should be relatively 
narrow compared to mechanically ventilated buildings due to difficulties in adequately distributing fresh 
air in wide spaces. In general, pure NV is most suitable for spaces with low requirements to the indoor 
environment where the occupants have individual control of the openings. 
For buildings with NV as the sole source of air supply and cooling, studies (Graca et al. 2015) indicates 
that it is mostly applicable for single offices with the possibility of manual control. It is however required 
to have very effective solar shadings, which essentially can interrupt the functioning of openable 
windows. The study on a multi-story office building in Belgium with single sided NV indicated an energy 
==PAGE:65==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
64 of 168 
 
saving for cooling of about 30% and 40% with additional night cooling with air change rate for day and 
night on respectively 4 h-1 and 8 h-1. However, the study did not indicate anything on thermal comfort. 
Cooling during daytime reduces the efficiency of night-time ventilative cooling as it reduces room and 
construction temperatures. Thereby the combined effect will be less than sum of the two‚Äôs effects 
individually, but still significantly larger than each of the individual effects.  
In general, NV has low running cost, low energy consumption, less hygiene problems with ducts, filters 
etc., low maintenance, and often lower initial costs compared to a standard mechanical ventilation system.  
On the other hand, NV has many constraints mainly concerning the effect on the indoor environment: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Use is limited by external conditions; air quality, noise, temperature, wind pressure 
ÔÇ∑ 
Openings have to be relatively large and unhindered to obtain sufficient air flow at all times. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Internal blinds and external solar shadings can obstruct the use of NV. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Dependent on space and fa√ßade layout. Distance to the fa√ßade and placement of opening 
determines the size of the occupied zone.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Difficult control; often sophisticated and costly controls are required to avoid comfort problems. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Large openings at ground level possess a security risk. 
All the constraints can vary which decreases the capability to provide a constant indoor environment 
regardless of the external conditions. 
Lastly, an important consideration is whether a real effect on energy consumption for cooling and 
improvement of thermal comfort can be realized in the future by only utilizing NV in large non-
residential buildings?  
 
4.5.3.2 
Impact of Urban Environment on Natural Ventilation  
An urban environment poses certain challenges, limitations and constraints to ventilative cooling 
compared to a rural environment. In larger urban areas compared to its surroundings the outdoor 
temperatures are generally higher due to greater thermal mass, the average wind speed is typically lower 
but the amplitude may be higher due to tall buildings, and pollution and noise levels are significantly 
higher mainly due to traffic.  
These factors have especially implications to possibility of utilizing NV due to noise and pollution in 
urban areas. Furthermore, they reduce the natural cooling effect of the air (higher temperatures). 
In urban areas the noise level typically rises with increased traffic density. The noise level is reduced with 
height above the street and width of the street ‚Äì attenuation increase with the distance to the source. The 
outdoor noise level restricts the possibility to apply NV by opening windows in the occupied time. NV at 
night time or outside occupancy hours may however be possible however the affection of incoming 
pollution on the indoor environment should be evaluated.  
 
4.5.3.3 
Night Cooling 
Night time cooling can be used to reduce the need for mechanical cooling during normal operation time 
by reducing the indoor temperature outside working hours by taking advantage of the free cooling effect 
of the colder nights. For mechanical systems the fans may run but with no need for cooling energy or 
ventilative opening such as window may be opened to limit the energy consumption even more by 
utilizing the natural forces.  
Figure 15 illustrates the operation of night cooling strategy under summer conditions. The temperature at 
the end of the day may be close to the upper limit for thermal comfort and with night cooling the 
==PAGE:66==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
65 of 168 
 
temperature at the start of the day will be at the lower limit. Attention should be paid to not exceeding the 
requirements for temperature drift on maximum 4oC over a day.   
Night cooling strategies is however highly dependent on the climatic cooling potential (CCP) of the 
ambient air as mentioned in section 4.5.1. In most of northern Europe the CCP is high and should be 
considered in the building design to optimize the energy consumption.  
 
Figure 15: Night cooling during summer (Climaconsult 2013) 
4.5.3.4 
Control 
Under constant conditions it is relatively simple to control and adjust NV but in reality, under dynamic 
conditions, it can be very difficult to control especially in terms of flow rate, flow pattern and 
temperature. Often sophisticated and costly controls are required to avoid comfort issues and to create a 
stable indoor environment. It can be a good idea for the users to be able to override automatic window 
openings.  
 
4.5.3.5 
Ventilative Openings 
Types of openings can be seen in Figure 16 and each type has its own advantages and limitations which 
should be evaluated for each specific case but is not further described in this report. Common for all is a 
large variety of sizes and the possibility to install vertically, horizontally and sloped. 
Strategies for ventilative openings can be divided into the following: single-sided, cross, buoyancy-driven 
or a combination. 
 
Figure 16: Types of openings (Graca, Chen, and Anderson 2015) 
Assessment criteria for the ventilative openings could however be as following(Graca et al. 2015): 
Ventilation Capacity: Depending on the effective opening area. 
==PAGE:67==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
66 of 168 
 
Controllability: Manual and/or mechanical controllability. Qualities may include robustness against 
wind pressure, proportionality of opening angle and effective opening. 
Comfort: Operability, occupants‚Äô control, draught risk, pollution and noise. Manual controlled low level 
openings and automatic controlled high level openings could be a solution to avoid draught. 
Security: Openings at low levels may impose a security risk. Limiting the openable area may be 
sufficient. 
Integration: Conflict between external solar shading, internal blinds, windows and actuators must be 
taken into account. 
Interaction: Counteraction between heating systems and ventilative openings must be handled especially 
with manually controlled openings.  
 
4.5.3.6 
Slot Vents, Louvre, Grilles and Dampers 
Slot vents, louvres, grilles and dampers are alternative options for utilizing ventilative openings and are 
available in large variety of sizes, air flow properties and fields of application. All types are able to 
provide a minimum air change rate for fresh air in smaller spaces but have limited capabilities in larger 
spaces and for cooling. They work passively (non-motorized) with pressure differences, local pressure 
drop and natural driven forces. The effect can be enhanced by a mechanical exhaust fan.  
The four options are displayed in Figure 17 and the basic functionalities are as following: 
Slot Vents: Stationary or dynamical, window or wall mounted. Airflows at 2 Pa reach values in the range 
of 50 to 150 m¬≥/h per meter of extension (Graca et al. 2015). 
Louvre: Adjustable wall mounted opening.  
Grilles: Stationary wall mounted installation, offering good protection against weather, animals, burglary 
and partly noise.  
Dampers: Adjustable, motorized or manual, either separate or in connection to grilles or louvres to 
regulate the air flow. 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 17: Slot Vent (left), Louvre (middle left), Grilles (middle right) and Dampers (right) (Graca et al. 2015) 
The common disadvantages are the uncontrollable varying air flow, partly allowing noise, difficulties to 
reduce pollution, and not shutting completely tight which can cause undesired infiltration, draught risk 
and additional heat loss especially during cold periods.  
Draught risk can partly be solved by installation in the upper part of the spaces and directing the intake air 
upward. Acoustical issues can be solved by fillings providing additional noise protection, but reducing the 
effectiveness of air intake.  
 
==PAGE:68==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
67 of 168 
 
4.5.4 Mixing ventilation (MV) 
Mixing Ventilation (MV) is based on mechanical air supply with conditioned air. It aims to remove 
polluted warm air and replace it with clean air and thereby achieve a uniform temperature and 
contaminant distribution in the occupied zone. 
The air is supplied outside of the occupied zone from an air terminal placed in the upper part of the space 
to ensure total mixing of the air and to avoid large velocities in the occupied zone. The supply is based on 
momentum flow with high initial velocity in order to overcome the buoyancy force from heat plumes to 
ensure full mixing in the space. The air is mixed by turbulence between the ambient air and the supply air.  
MV is often described as mechanical or forced ventilation because it utilizes a mechanical system or 
equipment to transport, condition, and supply air into the buildings. It typically either operates with 
filtered conditioned outdoor air or filtered conditioned outdoor air mixed with recirculated room air. 
The placement of the supply air terminal is critical in order to ensure sufficient fresh air to the desired 
places without creating draught and thermal discomfort. The type of supply device should be selected 
according to the shape and use of the space. Countless devices exist with various flow patterns and six of 
the most typical supply flow patterns for MV are displayed in Figure 18. 
 
Figure 18: Type of air distribution with mixing ventilation in large spaces (Kandzia et al. 2013) 
Other supply solutions such as ceiling mounted large area diffusers and diffuse ceiling do also create 
mixing flow due to buoyancy effect. Diffuse ceiling will be discussed in another section of this report. 
The polluted air is removed by an exhaust device usually placed in the top of the space, either on the wall 
in the ceiling or above the ceiling if having perforated ceiling, in order to extract the warmest air. The 
shape or type of the exhaust device is less important; however it should be able to extract the desired 
amount without creating noise and with a low pressure loss. 
The placement of the exhaust air terminal is less critical, some even says unimportant, but even though 
opinions are divided the placement of the exhaust can influence the heat balance in the space. Placing the 
exhaust above the place in a space where heat is expected to accumulate, e.g. above certain equipment or 
in front of windows, the warmest air has a shorter way and is not mixed as much with the occupied zone. 
==PAGE:69==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
68 of 168 
 
The exhaust placement should still be in accordance with the supply placement in order to avoid short 
circuiting. 
Modern MV systems are required to always be able to provide exactly the right amount of fresh air to 
every occupant in a space. This means that flow rate should be able to variate over time according to 
changing needs (VAV-system) and the air distribution systems may possibly be designed to allocate air to 
other spaces where occupants are present. In this way air distribution systems can fully adapt to the 
strength and location of heat and pollution load, which potentially lead to energy saving (Kandzia et al. 
2013). 
The control is primarily automatic by various sensors connected to a BMS controlling temperature and 
CO2 concentration. Manual control is unsuitable for large spaces and is generally limited to small spaces 
such as single offices.  Manual control could consist of adjusting air flow and targeted air temperature. 
MV usually operates with air change rates between 1 and 8 h-1 (Petersen 2011) under comfort conditions. 
The challenges are especially to obtain total mixing of the air at small air change rates and to avoid 
draught at high air change rates. Therefore, designers have to make a careful strategy regarding the 
placement of supply and exhaust terminals according to the arrangement and use of the space to obtain an 
optimal indoor environment for the occupants. Different issues could be draught if placing diffusers too 
close to the occupants and too close to the wall. Additionally, air supplied with too high flow rates or 
velocities resulting in very turbulent air streams creating draught and noise issues from the diffuser. 
MV is able to provide both heating and cooling to a space, non-simultaneously. Typical supply air 
temperature range for heating is up to 34¬∞C and down to 14¬∞C for cooling (Cao et al. 2014). In some 
countries, such as Denmark, the highest permissible supply air temperature is limited to 35¬∞C by 
regulation, DS 469 (Dansk Standard 1991). For cooling it is not recommended to have higher temperature 
difference than 10¬∞C between the supply air and space air to achieve proper mixing and to avoid cold 
downdraught (Kandzia et al. 2013).  
The temperature of the supply air is most commonly adjusted by a cooling and heating coil in the AHU 
but decentral heat exchangers can be installed at building sections or at room level in case of individual 
needs. However, decentralized heat exchangers impose larger investment cost with increased 
maintenance. The optimal way to adjust the effect to a space is by adjusting the flow rates, at room level, 
and provides an overall suitable temperature the AHU.  
The obtained heating and cooling depend on the ventilation rate and according to Taschenbuch f√ºr 
Heizung und Klimatechnik (Schramek 2007) a specific cooling load in the range of 90 W/m2 can be 
handled with MV systems. 
MV enables easier refurbishment and high flexibility within a space, since the air terminals are mounted 
in the top of the space. Thereby a space can be arranged and rearranged to some extent without having to 
change the installations or disturbing the flow patterns. If the space is constructed with suspended ceiling 
then the ceiling plates are most often very easy to mount and dismount making it relatively easy to change 
the placement or the number of terminals. 
MV is suitable as ventilation solution for almost any type of space when selecting supply devices with 
appropriate flow patterns according to the space dimensions. However, for very tall spaces or spaces with 
high heat loads other cooling and ventilation supply approaches are superior such as DV and radiant 
systems. 
As cooling solution all air systems such as MV is optimal at low loads which can be handled with the 
basic air flow. When going way beyond the cooling capacities of the basic air flow, MV is rarely the most 
energy efficient or cost effective system. Neither is it the best in terms of affections on the indoor 
environment due to increased draught risks. At high loads a combination between MV and a radiant 
system may be a better solution or alternatively replaced by chilled beams. 
==PAGE:70==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
69 of 168 
 
4.5.4.1 
Jet Ventilation 
Jet ventilation is a MV strategy for very large spaces and it can be a ventilative cooling solution in very 
tall spaces where it is difficult to implement other mixing ventilation units.  
The air is supplied through jet nozzles at very high initial velocity in order to overcome heat plumes and 
the large distances to the occupied zone. As illustrated in Figure 19 the supply nozzles are installed in the 
lower part of the space but still relatively high above the occupied zone. 
Jet ventilation is challenged in maintaining a uniform thermal environment and can especially be difficult 
to control in terms of draught with variations in heat plumes and solar load.  
In many cases, radiant floor cooling combined with displacement ventilation is superior both in regards of 
energy and comfort, and if possible it should be considered instead of jet ventilation (EBC 2015). 
 
 
Figure 19: Jet Ventilation (EBC 2015) 
 
 
==PAGE:71==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
70 of 168 
 
4.5.5 Hybrid Ventilation (HV) 
Hybrid Ventilation is a term used when both mechanical and natural ventilation are used in the entire or 
sections of a building. It can either be in simultaneous or separate use e.g. NV for night cooling and 
mechanical ventilation for comfort in daytime or mechanical ventilation in winter time to ensure 
sufficient supply temperatures with heat recovery and NV in summer for energy neutral ventilation and 
cooling.  
A hybrid system should be able to automatically change between the two systems in order to optimize the 
indoor environment and minimize the energy consumption by utilizing the strengths of each system. 
HV is typically applied when NV alone is not able to maintain appropriate thermal comfort in summer 
and winter conditions. 
Operation strategy typically include a winter strategy for which the air quality is the dimensioning factor 
for the air flow rate and a summer strategy for which the thermal comfort could be the dimensioning 
factor for the air flow rate. 
Typically four different types of Hybrid Ventilation (Dansk Standard 2013): 
ÔÇ∑ 
Fan supported Natural Ventilation. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Natural and Mechanical Ventilation. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Natural Ventilation supported by Mechanical Ventilation.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Mechanical Ventilation supported with cooling by Natural Ventilation 
 
4.5.5.1 
Fan supported Natural Ventilation 
Fan supported Natural Ventilation is a duct based solution without openable windows. The system is 
based on low driving forces (low velocities) and it greatly relies on an effective building shape which 
enhances thermal buoyancy effect and minimum pressure loss of the system. Furthermore, the cooling 
effect of this system in relatively small and it will require effective solar shading to avoid overheating. 
Ideally, the system can operate purely by natural forces, but in practice the natural forces variates too 
much to maintain a stable indoor environment. Therefore, fans are installed to ensure the capacity of the 
system regardless of presence and size of the natural driving forces and should be able to continuously 
regulate in line with changing natural forces and ventilation demand in order to supply a constant pressure 
in the system. The fan can be either a supply or exhaust fan depending on the system design, and in some 
cases both fans are required. Therefore, the fan design depends on the pressure losses within the system 
and the variations in natural driving forces. 
Night cooling by night ventilation is energetically very cheap in this system type, as most of the volume 
of air is moved by natural driving forces.  
As displayed in Figure 20, in some cases with central air intake this solution allows installation of filters, 
preheater, cooling coil and effective heat recovery through liquid connected battery to prolong the usage 
time of natural ventilation, improve the indoor environment and improve the energy usages ‚Äì making it 
closer to an actual mechanical ventilation system than a NV system. 
Air intake should be placed in terrain but this can have some limitations especially in urban environments 
where pollution and noise can be problematic for the system. Additionally, supply ducts in the ground 
could be uninsulated in order to allow heat exchange with the soil - potentially heating air in the winter 
and cooling air in the summer. An investigation (Bergs√∏e 2012) suggests that it is a risky idea in relation 
to mold and fungus, therefore precautions have to be made for this solution to work properly. 
 
==PAGE:72==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
71 of 168 
 
 
Figure 20: Fan assisted natural ventilation (Hviid & Svendsen 2007) 
Air is supplied to the space through simple air terminals or diffuse ceiling. The challenge is to ensure 
proper mixing of the air at the low supply velocities without causing cold drop.  
The air exhaust should be placed on the roof in order to maximize the benefit of the buoyance force, in 
this case exhaust is created through open connections from the occupied spaces to an atrium to minimize 
the flow resistances.  
A very low pressure loss is generally required for every part/component in the system in order for the 
system design to be a valid supplement to traditional mechanical ventilation. Both the heat recovery coils 
and the ductwork occupies considerable amount of space due to the low pressure loss requirement, 
therefore the both should be incorporated in the building from the initial design phase.  
A liquid connected battery is necessary to have heat recovery and intake in the bottom and exhaust in the 
top of the building, and thereby optimize the thermal buoyancy effect. It should be possible to achieve 
effectiveness in the range of 70-80% (Hviid & Svendsen 2007). Liquid connected heat exchangers are 
generally more expensive and have higher running costs, due to a circulation pump between the two 
ducts, compared to traditional heat exchangers. However, it could be possible to couple the heat recovery 
coil in the supply to a refrigeration plant in order to provide better cooling during the warm periods. 
 
4.5.5.2 
Natural and Mechanical Ventilation  
The most common hybrid systems are, probably, a combination of separate natural and mechanical 
ventilation systems in a building which can be an option to increase the overall energy efficiency for 
cooling, heating and ventilation. 
The two systems can either work separately or simultaneously depending on the system design. Most 
commonly the two systems work separately. Which system is active at a given time depends on multiple 
factors; natural driving forces, outdoor climate, and cooling need. The system with the lowest energy 
consumption and at the same time able to fulfill the indoor environmental requirements will be chosen as 
the active system. A hybrid system could potentially switch between mechanical and natural modes on a 
==PAGE:73==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
72 of 168 
 
daily basis to further reducing the overall energy consumption. However, such control strategy can be 
very difficult to simulate and to realize in practice. 
For this type of HV the year can basically be divided into three periods; natural ventilation, cooling, and 
heating. The natural ventilation period is mainly the two transition seasons where the outdoor is relatively 
close to the desired indoor temperature. In this case the mechanical system does not operate and thereby 
no energy is consumed. The cooling period is mainly the summer months June, July and August. Here the 
mechanical system is responsible for the air conditioning during the occupied time and is supplemented 
by NV for night cooling. The heating season is mainly the winter months from November to April. Here 
the mechanical system is sole responsible for the air conditioning due to the need of heat recovery and too 
cold outdoor temperatures. 
Traditionally in Denmark, the mechanical system will be active in the cold periods where heat recovery is 
beneficial and preheating is necessary. It can also be active in cases with high outdoor temperatures, 
where the supply temperature with natural ventilation would become too high to cool the space, or when 
outdoor noise and pollution levels are extending the limits.  
Natural ventilation in the occupied time on the other hand will mostly be used in the warmer parts of the 
year and partly in the transition periods when the outdoor climate allows it in terms of passive cooling 
potential and indoor climate requirements. Furthermore, it can be used for night cooling outside occupied 
hours regardless of the season.  
 
4.5.5.3 
Natural Ventilation supported by Mechanical Ventilation 
This HV principle is based on a duct system with very low pressure loss and a support fan, and 
constructed in a way that intake and exhaust are strongly enhanced by the natural forces. This enables the 
ventilation fan to be adjusted according to the available forces and in some periods turn off.  
 
4.5.5.4 
Mechanical Ventilation supported with cooling by Natural Ventilation 
This HV principle is based on a mechanical ventilation system designed to meet the air quality 
requirements in the heating season and then supported by natural ventilation for cooling during summer 
time. This could give savings in relation to forced fan operation to increase the air change rate in the 
building and it could remove or significantly reduce the need for mechanical cooling.  
 
 
==PAGE:74==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
73 of 168 
 
4.5.6 Displacement Ventilation (DV) 
The main principle of Displacement Ventilation (DV) is to force polluted warm air upwards by suppling 
colder air at or close to the floor. The air rising due to a combination of momentum of the supply air and 
buoyancy forces of different heat sources. Hereby, stratification in the space occurs dividing it into two 
parts; the occupied zone with clean air and the polluted zone in the top of the space. The stratification 
ensures a high IAQ and comfortable temperatures in the occupied zone while the polluted warm air forced 
by convection to top of the space where it can be extracted. Consequently, DV is only well suited for 
taller spaces with ceiling heights above 3 meters to obtain a satisfying height of the clean air layer 
(Hamilton et al. 2004). 
DV is primarily used for cooling with a supply temperature neutral or slightly colder than the room air 
temperature down to 18¬∞C to reduce risk of thermal discomfort (Cao et al. 2014). It is recommended to 
supply with ‚àÜ‹∂ in the range of 2-4oC with a maximum temperature gradient in the occupied zone of ~2 
K/m (Lindab 2008). The air is supplied at relatively low initial velocity in the range of 0.25-0.35 m/s 
(Awbi 2003) since the air flow does not have to overcome any countering forces such as heat plumes in 
opposition to mixing ventilation. The low velocity enables reduced noise, draught risk and fan power, but 
draught can still be an issue near the air terminal unit. 
The maximum cooling capacity of a DV system is in the range of 30-35 W/m2 (Awbi 2003) and maybe 
even up to 50 W/m2 (Schramek 2007). However, great variations exist depending on the specific system 
type. Due to the limited cooling capacity DV is not recommended in spaces where surplus heat is the 
main problem. However, less cooling is needed to obtain a given temperature in the occupied zone and 
thereby large heat removal efficiency can be expected.  
DV can also be used for heating but it is not recommended due to short-circuiting of supply air. 
 
Figure 21: Basic principle of displacement ventilation (Hamilton et al. 2004) 
 
DV can operate with higher air change rates compared to mixing ventilation. However, higher flow rates 
require substantial supply areas in order to reduce the air velocity. The air can be supplied as an example 
through a permeable floor, diffusers mounted in the floor, diffusers placed in the bottom of the wall or as 
free standing diffusers. If not integrated into the floor or wall, DV occupy significant parts of floor 
typically along the walls making it both very visible and very space consuming.  
==PAGE:75==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
74 of 168 
 
For permeable floor the air is supplied via the whole floor area. The upwards flow is mainly driven by 
buoyancy forces. However, a major challenge is the maintenance for these floors - in most cases the 
carpet have to be extremely clean to avoid polluting the supply air.   
Exhaust should always be placed as high in the space as possible to extract air from the warmest layer of 
air and thereby improve the heat removal efficiency.  
It is important to be aware of that the air flow pattern in the space is not significantly disturbed by the 
momentum provided by the supply openings and thermal plumes from heat sources control the air 
movement in the room. Thereby, DV is not sensitive to varying loads and heat plumes making the system 
perform equally under any load condition.  
Studies indicates that both auxiliary and cooling energy can be saved and still achieve same thermal 
comfort and air quality with less air compared to MV (Hovorka et al. 2012). 
In general, DV has low flexibility in term of retrofitting and refurbishing, since it is very dependent on the 
position of the occupants within the space and the system is only designed for one type of setup.  
DV is most suitable for auditoriums, theatres, and similar where the seating is constant and the room 
height is sufficient to create good stratification. Furthermore, very large spaces such as halls, atriums, 
conference rooms, and shopping centers, where stratification is beneficial and other ventilative strategies 
have issues with the supply distance from the air terminal to the occupied zone.  
On the other hand, DV has very little use in offices spaces and similar due to insufficient room height to 
achieve stratification. However, it may work for spaces with normal room height in open connection to an 
atrium. 
 
4.5.6.1 
Piston Flow Ventilation (PiV) 
Piston Flow Ventilation (PiV) is DV where air is supplied by a constant, approximately laminar flow 
through the entire surface area of the floor, ceiling or wall as illustrated in Figure 22. The supply air 
pushes the room air towards the opposite surface where the air is extracted through the entire surface area. 
PiV is primarily used in spaces with requirements for extreme high air change rates up to 50-100 h-1 
(Kandzia et al. 2013) e.g. in clean rooms and in the manufacturing industry to protect products and 
personal from pollution, dust, etc. Very high ventilation effectiveness can be achieved with PiV with no 
risk of draught since the air flow is constantly even. Due to the high air change rates the supply 
temperature is equal to the required room temperature. In general, PiV is unsuitable for non-industry 
premises. 
 
 
Figure 22: Basic principle of Piston Flow Ventilation (Bellia et al. 2010) 
 
==PAGE:76==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
75 of 168 
 
4.5.6.2 
Underfloor Ventilation 
Underfloor Ventilation is a combination of DV and MV. It utilizes the space beneath a raised floor either 
as a plenum box or for supply ducts/pipes to distribute air directly to the occupied zone through diffusers 
placed in the floor. The diffusers are either active or passive swirls placed outside the occupied zone 
creating a mixing flow keeping the mixing zone near the floor.  
Underfloor ventilation can be used for both heating and cooling due to the mixing of air and a higher 
initial velocity and often higher supply temperatures is used compared to DV or MV to avoid thermal 
discomfort. However, underfloor ventilation systems pose a large risk of excessive draught due to its 
often close placement to the occupancies making it an unattractive solution for most cases. Like most DV 
systems it also has limited flexibility without changing the whole floor or obstructing the flow with 
furniture. One of the main issues is that it suffers from increased transportation of particles, dirt and 
pollutants from floor to the space due to higher velocities near the floor which essentially decrease the 
IAQ in the occupied space. 
 
 
Figure 23: Underfloor ventilation (EBC 2015) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:77==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
76 of 168 
 
4.5.7 Diffuse Ceiling Ventilation (DIFCV) 
Diffuse Ceiling Ventilation (DIFCV) is essentially low impulse displacement ventilation with air supplied 
vertically downwards at slight under temperature compared to the space. When the supply air meets the 
heat plumes it creates a mixing flow in the space.  
A diffuse ceiling is a suspended ceiling system with diffusive properties and low pressure drop. As 
displayed in Figure 24 the cavity above the suspended ceiling acts as a large plenum box, with a 
minimum height of roughly 300 mm (Troldtekt 2008). Air is supplied to the cavity through one or more 
supply ducts, depending on the size of the space. The supply could come from an AHU or fan assisted 
natural ventilation. A small pressure difference between the plenum and the occupied space results in air 
penetrates evenly through the entire ceiling into the occupied zone with a low level of momentum flow.  
If designed and installed correctly DIFCV has low vulnerability to an uneven air supply in the plenum. 
However, designers have to take into account reductions in the diffusive area due to other installations, 
such as light fixtures, exhaust terminals, sensors, etc. which can reduce the air supply and thereby the 
delivered cooling effect. The exhaust is through traditional ceiling or wall mounted diffuser which should 
be placed to create minimum pressure loss for the system and if possible be places strategically according 
to heat sources. 
Design parameters such as shape, distribution of air passages, porosity of the ceiling, intentional cracks 
etc. has a significant influence on performance of a DIFCV system.  
Another important parameter for DIFCV systems is the ability to acts as a sound absorber. Acoustical 
panels from e.g. Troldtekt can be used as diffusive ceiling plates. If perforated metal plates are used sound 
absorbing materials can be either integrated in the plates or inside the plenum. 
 
Figure 24: Diffuse ceiling ventilation (Troldtekt 2008) 
Compared to traditional air supply terminals DIFCV reduces the pressure drop of the inlet mixing device 
from typically 30 Pa to 2-6 Pa. The system is only balanced by a damper in the supply duct. Moreover, a 
reduction in pressure loss due reduced amount of distribution ducts. The low pressure loss in DIFCV 
enables the possibility to utilize natural ventilation where cool air is drawn in through a louvre damper 
arrangement in the fa√ßade.  
DIFCV is primary used for cooling and ventilative purposes with supply air temperatures neutral or 
colder than the room temperature. A very high ‚àÜT is theoretically possible between the plenum and the 
space without draught problems or cold drop due to the large supply area. The possible ‚àÜT is depended on 
the specific type of ceiling system. However, attention should be paid to the temperature of the ceiling 
plates which have to be above the dew-point temperature of the space to avoid condensation. In practice a 
==PAGE:78==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
77 of 168 
 
‚àÜT between 1 and 8oC can be expected with air changes rates up to 8 h-1 (Troldtekt 2008) corresponding 
to cooling load up to 60 W/m2. Heating is possible but very limited and should generally be avoided or 
supplemented by convectors.  
DIFCV has generally lower investment cost than conventional MV solutions with less ductwork and no 
air terminals as such. Additionally due to the ‚Äúlack‚Äù of terminal unit in the ceiling it enables earlier 
balancing of the system in the construction phase with fewer conflicts to other subjects.  
In general, DIFCV is most suitable for spaces with normal room height (2.5-3.0 m) in order to obtain 
fresh air in the occupied zone and sufficient mixing of the air. DIFCV is superior to most other ventilative 
cooling strategies at creating draught free environment within the comfort range even at relatively high 
loads.  
 
4.5.8 Textile Based Ventilation (TV) 
Textile Based Ventilation (TV) is essentially a DV system and somehow similar to diffuse ceilings. 
Traditionally, a cylindrical textile bag with a stiff skeleton is mounted in the top of the room connected to 
an air supply duct in either the top or the end of the bag. The textile is often replaced by a perforated 
metal cylinder mainly due to hygienically issues with the textile. The bag with good diffusive properties 
distributes air evenly into the space at low velocities due to its large supply area. It can however be very 
difficult to balance the supplied air flow and get an even distribution over the entire bag diffuser 
especially when varying the air flow. 
The air distribution in the space is controlled by buoyancy forces and is therefore sensitive to the 
positioning of heat sources in the space.  
 
Figure 25: Performance of textile based ventilation with varying supply temperature (Bolashikov 2015). ‚àÜT=troom-tsupply; A: ‚àÜT>0, 
B: ‚àÜT=0, C: ‚àÜT<0, and D: ‚àÜT<<0. 
As displayed in Figure 25, the supply air temperature has to be neutral or slightly colder than the room air 
in order to achieve proper mixing and avoid draught and cold drop. Furthermore, heating is not possible 
due to the low initial velocity, resulting in insufficient mixing of the air. 
In general, textile based ventilation is not suitable for cooling as such and should mainly be used in spaces 
where very large constant air change rates are required simultaneously with occupancy e.g. in laboratories 
due to chemical fume hoods with high exhaust rate. 
==PAGE:79==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
78 of 168 
 
4.5.9 Personalized Ventilation (PV) 
Personalized Ventilation (PV) or localized ventilation is based on supply of clean conditioned air directly 
into the breathing zone at each workstation before mixing with the surroundings air. Thereby PV achieves 
very high ventilation efficiencies and high IAQ in the micro environment at around each occupant.  
Individual control of the air supply allows each occupant to adjust the thermal comfort to their personal 
preferences and thereby potentially give a higher satisfaction with the indoor environment compared to all 
other ventilation principles. Typically, it is possible to individually adjust air flow rate, air velocity and 
flow pattern. It can also be possible to adjust the supply temperature for each occupant, but this can be 
difficult to control and expensive to install extra components required and it is therefore often left out. 
The supply temperature of PV is often in the range of 20 to 28oC, but may be supplied colder or warmer if 
under individual control (Cao et al. 2014). Spaces with PV has the possibility to allow increased indoor 
temperature in cooling situation as the occupants can improve the thermal comfort by adjusting the air 
supply (Hovorka et al. 2012).  
Regardless of the achieved air quality and thermal sensation of each occupant the minimum air change 
rate for occupied spaces still applies and additional ventilation may be necessary in form of background 
TVV system.  
 
 
 
Figure 26: Personalized Ventilation (Hovorka et al. 2012; Melikov 2015) 
 
As displayed in Figure 26, PV can be architecturally challenging to implement in most spaces. Air supply 
to workstations is difficult to integrate with ductwork either below the floor, on the floor or going down 
from the ceiling to the workstations. Consequently, PV has a low flexibility in the space in terms of 
rearranging. Additionally, PV is expensive to implement in a space due to a high number of air terminal, 
controls and ducts.  
PV is most suitable for certain healthcare functions, workstations with very high performance 
requirements, and polluted industrial working environments. It has limited use  
In general, PV is not appropriate for cooling due to its low air flow rates with little thermal capacity. PV 
should be installed in combination with a radiative cooling system, chilled beams or a TVV system.
==PAGE:80==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
79 of 168 
 
4.5.10 Cool Ceiling (CC) 
Cool Ceiling (CC) is a patented product developed by Danish JS Ventilation, documented by Teknologisk 
Insitut, and is designed combination with ceiling systems from Austrian Fural. CC is an air based ceiling 
ventilation system connection by ducts to an AHU and it can be used non-simultaneously for cooling and 
heating. In many ways it is comparable to a mixing ventilation solution, but at the same time have similar 
properties of a diffuse ceiling. 
The system relies on an air tight cavity above a suspended ceiling with integrated air diffusers. The 
expected leakage of the ceiling construction is 0.36-0.99 l/s/m2 at 10-50 Pa overpressure in cavity. 
Therefore equipment installed in the ceiling and openings to adjacent room should be sufficiently sealed.  
The minimum required construction height above the ceiling is 300 mm, the air diffuser is typically 200 
mm high and 100 mm free space above air diffusers is required in order for the system to run optimal. 
Increased height inside the cavity is possible; however the influence of extremely high cavities should be 
investigated further.  
 
 
Figure 27: Cool Ceiling (Cool Ceiling 2015) 
 
The low construction height can reduce the total story height or increased room height (aesthetics, thermal 
buffer), compared to traditional ventilation systems. Depending on preference this can lead to a reduction 
of the total building height which essentially can saves substantial construction.   
The system offers high flexibility with various air diffuser sizes, variable plate sizes, and it is easy to 
install light fixtures, various sensors, and other equipment. 
No supply ducts are required above the room which leaves more space for other installations and give less 
troubles in both planning and construction phase. Since the amount of technical components is limited, 
compared to standard mixing ventilation systems, a short construction phase can be expected. The system 
is almost completely finished and balanced when the short supply duct branch with damper is installed 
into the room, leaving only ceiling plate and diffusers left to mount ‚Äì resulting in less trouble with other 
subject in the construction phase. 
The exhaust system can be connected in multiple different ways. One option is a classic ceiling mounted 
air terminal connected with a duct. This of course limits some of the benefit with reduced ceiling height 
and limited interactions with other installation. Another option could be to have a wall mounted air 
terminal below the ceiling preferable in connection to a room with reduced ceiling height (e.g. hall way) 
in order to create a hided installation.  
The heat transfer process between the cool ceiling and the room is approximately a 15/85 combination of 
radiation and air mixing. Chilled air from the ventilation system enters the cavity through one or several 
==PAGE:81==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
80 of 168 
 
duct(s), depending on the room size, with a VAV damper regulating 25-100 % and muffler, filling the 
cavity with air and keeping a constant overpressure in the cavity. The chilled air falls towards the warmer 
ceiling plate, heat up a little, rise up and pushed over the edge of the air diffuser and into the room. The 
now chilled ceiling plate then acts as a radiative cooling panel. An illustrated process can be seen in 
Figure 27.  
The ceiling plates are fabricated of thin perforated metal ensuring a fast heat transfer between the chilled 
air above, the plate and the room. Furthermore, the plates have integrated a thin layer of sound absorbing 
ensuring good acoustical properties. 
The temperature of a ceiling plate should always be higher than the dew-point temperature. The specific 
temperature is project depended and should be assessed each time. However, above 17 degree is 
recommended, corresponding to a minimum inlet temperature of 13 degree.  
It is possible to supply with these low temperatures without creating draught or cold drop because the 
ceiling plates drag out some of the energy in the air before it is supplied to the room. Furthermore, the air 
diffusers are optimized to increase the Coanda-effect to avoid cold drop. Traditional ventilative cooling 
solutions supply all the energy directly into the air resulting in potential local discomfort. The CC solution 
can potentially decrease the required air volume by up to 20 % compared to traditional ventilative cooling 
solutions, due to the possibility of a lower supply temperature.  
Experiments  have shown that due to the mix of radiation and air mixing the CC can provide a cooling 
effect up to 50 W/m2  while providing a class A indoor environment (S. Madsen, Guan & Kronby 2014). 
However, it is proven possible to provide a maximum cooling effect of 100 W/m2 and still provide a class 
B indoor environment. The supplied effect is controlled as a combination of the air flow rate and the 
supply temperature. The exact expectable effect depends on the property and size of each specific room. 
The CC system is well tested in rooms with normal room height. Limitations may exist in high room 
above 4 meters due to its relatively low initial air velocity. When increasing the room height the system 
will most likely still be able to handle the cooling demand, but it will eventually make it impossible to 
perform full mixing of the air and thereby making the system unable to comply with the requirements of 
the atmospheric environment. 
If the available air flow to a room is only enough to comply with the IAQ but not enough to comply with 
the cooling demand (e.g. in renovation cases) then a FCU or a CB can be installed. The FCU or the CB 
can be installed above the ceiling in the cavity or in connection to the cavity extracting warm air directly 
from the room and supply the chilled air to the cavity. This eliminates the classic draught problems of 
especially the FCU and enhances the cooling effect of the ceiling. Alternatively, radiant panels or TABS 
could be integrated in the top of the cavity to improve the cooling capacity. 
Experiments with utilization of the CC for heating shows that it is possible to supply air even at high 
temperatures ~30oC and still achieve adequate mixing in the room, without creating comfort problems due 
to poor mixing and/or high air velocities (S. Madsen, Guan, Hoen, et al. 2014). The experiments achieved 
35 W/m2 for heating and still provide a category A indoor environment. Typical heat loss in new low 
energy buildings span from 10 to 20 W/m2. Further experiments revealed even higher possible effects 
when increasing the supply temperature, but this reduce the indoor environment category to B. 
Calculations should be carried out in any specific case in order to determine the exact effect.  
 
 
==PAGE:82==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
81 of 168 
 
4.5.11 Chilled Beam (CB) 
Chilled Beams (CB) aim to provide high temperature cooling/low temperature heating into space by water 
heat exchanger mounted in the ceiling. As displayed in Figure 28, this section includes the two main types 
of CB, passive and active, as well as active CB with radiant panels. Additionally, active CB with diffuse 
ceilings is included.  
The main cooling principle is to remove sensible loads by natural (passive) or forced (active) convection. 
A minor part of the heat exchange is by radiation; however this part depends on the temperatures, the 
shape and the type of CB.  
              
 
Figure 28: Options for chilled beams (Bolashikov 2015) 
CB should operate in dry-mode to avoid condensation. The surface temperature of the heat exchanger 
should always have higher temperature than the dew-point temperature of the space. Therefore, size, 
shape and temperatures should be designed according to the space temperature and humidity. Drainage 
can be integrated allowing extended cooling capacity, but it should be avoided due to health risks from 
e.g. mold and bacteria growth.  
When applying CB it is important to limit additional sources of humidity, e.g. infiltration and natural 
ventilation. If windows are openable the BMS should be connected to these window and be able to adjust 
the supply water temperature to the CB. 
Outside the scope is so call multiservice chilled beams, where other building services such as light, 
sprinklers and sensors, can be integrated optimizing the use of ceiling space.  
4.5.11.1 Passive Chilled Beams 
Passive Chilled Beam is a simple standalone heat exchangers connected to a chilled water circuit. They 
are mounted in the top of the space mostly fully exposed free hanging to obtain the best natural 
convective flow. As displayed in Figure 29, the warm air rises to the ceiling, passes through the chilled 
beam where it is cooled and flow downwards back into the space causing a constant flow.  
 
Figure 29: Passive chilled Beam convective flow(left) and recommended clearance(right) (Butler 2004) 
==PAGE:83==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
82 of 168 
 
The passive CB can also be recessed into a suspended ceiling or placed above a perforated suspended 
ceiling. In both cases it is important to ensure proper air flow to the beam in order for the system to work 
adequately. However, the most effective is fully exposed free hanging CB. In all three cases it is 
important to keep a minimum clearance above the beam in order to obtain an adequate return flow (Butler 
2004). Nevertheless, it is important to avoid placing a passive CB directly above fixed work stations due 
to risk of strong cold drop.  
It is recommended to supply high temperature chilled water to the CB in the range of 14-18oC  to reduce 
condensation risks and enable more low quality cooling sources, free cooling options and smaller 
refrigeration systems (Hovorka et al. 2012). 
Passive CB is most suitable for spaces with a sensible cooling load in the range of 40 and 80 W/m2 when 
good thermal comfort is required (Butler 2004). The cooling capacity is typically around 150 W per meter 
beam. Higher loads can be handled where thermal comfort is less important with cooling loads up to 120 
W/m2 and 250 W/m beam. Furthermore, passive CB is very rarely used for heating and should generally 
be avoided due to poor mixing of the air. A separate heating system is therefore typically required. 
A passive CB is only able to circulate air and therefore primary air has to be supplied to the space using a 
separate system. It is important to avoid interferences between the supply air and the natural convection 
around the CB in order to achieve the best operation. Attention should especially be paid to ceiling 
mounted air terminals supplying air horizontally along the ceiling; if not carefully designed and placed 
the air jet obstructs the natural convection around the beam. Furthermore, the exhaust air terminal should 
be placed strategically away from the CB to avoid disturb of the natural flow and extraction of cold air. 
In general, passive CBs are not very suitable for spaces with high ventilation rates, such as meeting rooms 
and classrooms, due disruption of the natural convection flow. Here active CB or all air systems would be 
a better choice. An exception could be in spaces where displacement ventilation is an option, such as 
auditoriums. Here warm polluted air at low velocity rises towards the ceiling creating stratification, 
thereby the warmest air in the space meets the CB which cools the air and returns it downwards creating a 
good mixing. Additionally, tall spaces with downward facing air jet supply may work. 
 
4.5.11.2 Active Chilled Beams 
Active Chilled Beam is, likewise passive CB, heat exchangers connected to a water circuit, but instead of 
natural convection they rely on forced convection meaning they are also connected to a ventilation system 
to provide fresh air. If not connected to fresh air supply the CB can be installed with a fan for enhanced 
cooling and recirculation of the air ‚Äì also known as a Fan Coil. The forced convection makes both cooling 
and heating possible depending on a combination of water temperature and air temperature.  
As illustrated in Figure 30 the fresh air supply moves through venturi nozzles, creating a low-pressure 
zone within an active beam, room air is induced upward where it makes contact with the cooling coil. The 
two air flows are then mixed inside the CB and supply to the space. In cooling mode the fresh air supply 
is typically 20-30% and the rest is inducted recirculated air. The air is supplied to the space along its 
length in an angle through a linear slot diffuser forcing the cold air along the ceiling utilizing the Coanda 
effect to increase the throw length and to minimize the risk of cold downdraught. Primary air must be 
supplied at a flow rate large enough to handle the latent loads and sensory pollution in the space. 
It is recommended to supply high temperature chilled water to the heat exchanger in the range of 14-20oC  
to reduce condensation risks and enable more low quality cooling sources, free cooling options and 
smaller refrigeration systems (Butler 2004; Hovorka et al. 2012). 
Active CB are most suitable for spaces with a sensible cooling load up to between 60 and 80 W/m2 when 
good thermal comfort is required (Butler 2004). The cooling capacity is typically around 250 W per meter 
==PAGE:84==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
83 of 168 
 
beam. Higher loads can be handled where thermal comfort is less important with cooling loads up to 120 
W/m2 and 350 W/m beam. The heating capacity is typically 150 W/m corresponding to 25-35 W/m2. 
  
  
Figure 30: Active chilled beams (Butler 2004; Bolashikov 2015) 
 
Active CBs benefit from being both a ventilation unit and a cooling unit making it less sensitive to air 
movements and local heat plumes. The cooling effect and space temperature is typically controlled by the 
chilled water flow rate in the heat exchanger. The flow rate and velocity of the chilled water greatly 
depends on the specific product. Most CBs are also able to adjust the flow rate of both fresh air and 
recirculated air in order to optimize the performance according to changing needs 
Active CBs have very wide range of use in offices, meeting rooms, etc. It is however not recommended in 
tall spaces where full mixing of the air can be difficult to obtain. It is neither recommended for spaces 
where the latent load and/or sensory pollution are the dominating loads. In both cases all air systems is 
preferred. 
The most optimal placement of active CB is directly above fixed work stations, where the air velocity is 
lowest and thereby the risk of draught is minimized. Nevertheless, this might be difficult especially in 
open plan offices with multiple rows of workstations potentially leading to some row experiencing no 
cold air drop and while other sit just below the drop. Therefore, the furnishing of a space should take into 
account placement of CBs and vice versa.  
Additionally, it is important to consider the throw length of the supply air and whether the CB supplies 
the air two-sided or one-sided. CBs placed too close to each other will increase the air drop where the air 
jets meet resulting in air drop. Likewise, CBs placed too close to a wall will force the air downwards and 
a one-sided supply should be considered. However, a well-designed and proper controlled CB should not 
poses any significant risk of downdraught.  
Overall, a key advantage of CBs is that they require relatively little space in the ceiling and combined 
with a low installation height of around 300 mm (Hovorka et al. 2012). The potentially largest advantage 
is the high energy carrying capacity of water provided via pipes consuming a minimum of space for it 
distribution around the building compared to all air systems which are significantly less efficient because 
of the low heat capacity of air, which necessitates large ductwork. 
 
 
==PAGE:85==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
84 of 168 
 
4.5.11.3 Active Chilled Beam with Radiant Panel 
Active chilled beam in combination with radiant panels (radiant passive chilled beam) can be a solution 
for rooms with very high sensible heat loads ‚Äì above what is possible to handle with the two systems 
individually. The radiant panels are placed facing the room either below or on the sides of the CB (Figure 
31). The chilled water supply to the CB is also circulated in the radiant panel. Hereby, the two systems 
work a one unit in a very simple design increasing the conductance per unit which can increase the 
cooling capacity significantly without disrupting the thermal comfort.  
 
 
Figure 31: Active chilled beam with radiant panel (Bolashikov 2015) 
 
4.5.11.4 Active Chilled Beams with Diffuse Ceiling 
The active chilled beam is connected to a primary air supply and to a space for recirculation. Instead of 
supplying the air directly into the room all or the largest part of the air is directed to a cavity above the 
suspended ceiling as illustrated in Figure 32. The cold air is then inducted to the space by natural 
convection through a diffuse ceiling or by a Cool Ceiling.  
This setup will minimize the risk of local down draught and the cooling will be more evenly distributed in 
the space. It is mostly favorable in spaces with relatively low room height. 
 
 
Figure 32: Chilled beam in connection to  diffuse ceiling (Bolashikov 2015) 
 
 
==PAGE:86==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
85 of 168 
 
4.5.12 Fan Coil Unit (FCU) 
Fan Coil Unit (FCU) is a heat exchanger connected to a chilled water circuit or refrigerant circuit, with a 
fan to recirculate air. FCU are available in a broad range of variations and are installed in the upper part 
of the space either recessed into a suspended ceiling, free hanging, or mounted on the wall, but also free 
standing versions can be found. 
As displayed in Figure 33, warm air is extracted from the space, pass through a heat exchanger cooling 
the air and resupplies to the space. FCU are only recirculating air and should only be seen as a cooling 
addition to the primary ventilation system.  
 
 
Figure 33: Fan coil unit (Bolashikov 2015) 
 
Typically, FCU cool the air 8-10oC from 26oC and resupply to the space at 14-16oC (EBC 2015). The 
chilled water typically with a design temperature difference of 5-7oC with temperature sets 
(supply/return) in the range of 7/12oC or 7/14oC (EBC 2015). The large temperature difference and low 
water temperatures will eventually create condensation at the cooling coil and therefore drainage is 
required. FCU can also be used for heating with supply water temperatures around 50oC and return 
around 40oC (EBC 2015).  
The cooling capacity is theoretically unlimited depending on the size of the coil, the fan speed and the 
water flow rate. However, for comfort purposes the cooling capacity is very similar to active CBs ranging 
from  
Combining the low thermal inertia with a high initial air velocity the FCU is good for fast conditioning of 
spaces with high cooling demands or large heat load variations. FCU is a relatively cheap installation and 
well known technique. However, it has a high risk of thermal discomfort, high noise levels from fans, 
potential health issues with bacteria in the condensate and dust in filters, and it is relatively expensive in 
energy consumption and maintenance compared to e.g. active CB. 
FCU is most suitable for spaces with high equipment loads, low humidity, low thermal comfort 
requirements, and in spaces with temporary occupancy. In general, FCU are not recommended to use in 
spaces where good thermal comfort is required. 
 
 
==PAGE:87==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
86 of 168 
 
4.6 Radiative Cooling  
Radiative Cooling refers to hydronic radiant cooling systems with a fluid, often water, as heat carrier 
which is not in direct contact with the environment where the heat exchange to a space relies on long-
wave radiation and partly by convection along a chilled surface facing the space. Common for all 
radiative systems is that they are able to cool and heat a space, non-simultaneously, and thereby 
potentially also remove the need for a separate heating system.  
Radiant cooling systems require installation of a separate ventilation system to cover at least the basic air 
change rate in order to obtain a satisfying IAQ and address the latent load to decrease risk of surface 
condensation. Radiant systems should therefore operate in dry cooling mode which necessitates the water 
temperature to be controlled relative to the room conditions generally by utilizing high temperature 
chilled water resulting in relatively high surface temperatures.  
The radiant and the ventilation system must be carefully coordinated to avoid counteracting. The 
combination enables lower air flow rates to the space compared to all-air systems where the entire load is 
handled by the ventilation system. 
Investigations of the dew-point temperature should be made at each case, since it could become the 
dimensioning factor. The typical design ensures that the dew-point temperature of the room is above the 
surface temperature of the radiant system. However, the most bulletproof design is to keep it below the 
chilled water temperature which reduces any worries about air leakage to the supply pipes in the radiant 
system. 
Radiant cooling can be divided into three categories; radiant panels, radiant surface systems, and 
Thermally Active Building Systems (TABS). The majority of radiant cooling systems are chilled ceiling 
installations such as radiant panel and pipes in the slabs.  
In general, radiant cooling systems are quiet with very low draught risks. However, slight noise issues can 
potentially occur in ceiling and wall panels due to uneven water flow or air bubbles. The large heat 
transfer surfaces allow fast reaction times for changing load. However, the reaction time to changing 
conditions is slower compared to air systems. 
Radiant system is not very common and fairly untested in Danish contexts. However, the trend is that 
more buildings are installed with radiant system to increase the cooling capacity of a space without 
causing increased draught risks and to reduce the increasing spatial demands of ventilation system. 
Moving cooling from ducts to pipes radiant system can potentially reduce the required story height of a 
building, reduce the size of the AHU and reduce the size of technical shafts due to reduced duct sizes. 
Additionally radiant systems can be well-integrated in almost any surfaces within a space and can be 
almost invisible to ensure the aesthetical aspect which most certainly satisfies the architects.  
Additional construction types of radiant systems can be found in REHVA guidebook 07 (Babiak et al. 
2013).  
 
 
==PAGE:88==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
87 of 168 
 
4.6.1 Capacity 
Based on the total heat transfer coefficients, acceptable surface temperature in terms of condensation and 
comfort and room temperature within the comfort range 20-26oC the maximum cooling capacities can be 
calculated and is displayed in Table 26. The difference in the total heat transfer coefficients is a 
combination of radiant and convective heat transfer. The radiant heat transfer coefficient is approximately 
5.5 W/m2K while the variation in the total coefficients origins from the natural convection coefficient.  
Floor cooling capacities within the occupied zone 42 W/m2 and in cases with direct sun radiation the 
cooling capacity can potentially exceed 100 W/m2. Wall cooling capacities is 72 W/m2 
Ceiling cooling capacities is up to 100 W/m2 for one-way heat exchange and up to 130 W/m2 for two-way 
heat exchange ‚Äì toward the space and toward a large cavity above which is actively used with proper air 
exchange.
