In practice the cooling capacity for a radiant system depends on spacing between pipes, thickness 
above/below pipes, material properties, surface material, water temperature, and water flow rate.  
According to DS 15377 less than 5% is dissatisfied when the radiant temperature asymmetry is less than 
14oC for a chilled ceiling and 10oC for chilled vertical surfaces. However, the dew-point temperature will 
become the limiting factor before the radiant asymmetry for almost every cooling case.  
The cooling capacity can with benefit be enhanced by combining radiant systems with chilled beams 
(CB), as also mentioned in section 5.5.10, since cooling pipes and air supply is already present when 
using radiant systems. However, attention should be paid to the supply temperature from the CB in order 
to avoid cold drop due to the extra cooling of the air from especially chilled ceilings.  
 
4.6.2 Temperature 
The large heat transfer areas in radiant systems enable the fluid temperature to be very close to the room 
temperature. For correctly designed radiant cooling systems the fluid temperature is slightly below the 
desired surface temperature.  
Typical fluid supply temperatures for high temperature cooling is in the range of 14-20oC with a ‚àÜT of 
approximately 2-4oC (Hovorka et al. 2012). The dimension of piping influences the flow rate and thereby 
the temperature and the heat exchange. 
It is important to ensure a close to uniform temperature distribution in the hydraulic system to obtain an 
even cooling supply to the space. This may result in multiple loops in the system to decrease the pipe 
==PAGE:89==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
88 of 168 
 
length and thereby decrease the temperature reduction in the fluid. Furthermore, supply pipes, valves, 
manifolds etc. should therefore be appropriately insulated to avoid surface condensation.  
It is possible to utilize the same radiant system for heating and cooling depending on the load situation. It 
will however require a set of heating pipes, a set of cooling pipes and a three-way valve as displayed in 
Figure 34.  
In some buildings with no simultaneous heating and cooling demands it may be possible to have only one 
common set of ‚Äúcomfort‚Äù pipes where the supply water temperature in the system variates  according to 
load situation and seasonal changes. By removing the need of a separate heating system and maybe also a 
set of heating pipes will result in large economic savings in both investment and maintenance cost. 
 
 
Figure 34: Principe of combined heating(left) and cooling(right) radiant system with three-way valve (Rasmussen 2016) 
 
 
==PAGE:90==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
89 of 168 
 
4.6.3 Ceiling Cooling 
Radiative ceiling cooling systems, also known as chilled ceilings, can be considered as radiant panels and 
are typically constructed as a suspended ceiling or embedded into a fixed ceiling. The system should aim 
to cover as large ceiling area as possible to provide the most uniform distribution in the space and 
therefore models with integrated sound absorption material is recommended to improve the acoustical 
performance in the space. However, it is important to account for the correct exchanging surface area as 
lights, sensors and air diffusers reduce the area.  
The theoretical cooling capacity is 100 W/m2 but in practice when the effective area is taken into account 
in the range in more likely 50-80 W/m2 (EBC 2015; Hovorka et al. 2012). Versions of suspended radiant 
ceilings panels exists where heat is exchanged both upward and downwards resulting in a higher capacity 
than the typical downward facing panels with cooling capacity in the range of 120-130 W/m2 (EBC 2015). 
This requires an open ceiling system with a decent height above the plates where to warm air can move 
freely or forced. Essentially this can be said to be a large passive chilled beam.   
 
 
Figure 35: Boundary temperature conditions for radiant ceiling panels is radiant asymmetry for heating is (left) and dew-point 
temperature for cooling(right) (Rasmussen 2016) 
 
The boundary temperature condition for radiant ceiling systems are as illustrated in Figure 35 the dew-
point temperature in the space for cooling whereas for heating it is the radiant asymmetry. Radiant ceiling 
systems are typically open for diffusion meaning that air can potentially get in contact with the piping 
which means that the dew-point temperature in the space should at best be below the chilled water 
temperature.  
The construction of different types of radiant ceilings is very similar consisting of pipes in close 
connection to a material with high conductivity which exchanges energy between the chilled pipe and the 
space. A chilled ceiling can be integrated in very shallow ceiling constructions with as little as 60-70 mm 
available. 
Most often the ceiling is divided into multiple sections where a main distribution pipe runs above the 
ceiling with a (flexible) connection to each ceiling plates section in order to achieve a low ‚àÜT and an 
uniform effect in each circuit.   
Various versions exist and some are displayed in Figure 36. One solution is small plastic pipes with short 
distances in between can be embedded into gypsum board (or similar) to create a uniform temperature 
distribution in the ceiling. Alternatively, large metal pipes with longer distances in between can mounted 
==PAGE:91==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
90 of 168 
 
onto a metal plate ceiling to even the heat exchange. Either types of ceiling can be constructed 
dismountable or fixed plates. 
Regardless of the ceiling type certain precautions has to be taken. Firstly, unplanned holes and mounting 
of things should are not be allowed in radiant ceilings.  The significant extra weight from the panels has to 
be taken into account when designing the bearings for the ceiling system.  
Special attention should be paid to the interfaces with other subjects in the execution phase because the 
system has to be installed in steps in between the installation of electricity and ventilation. A good advice 
is to mount the main pipe into the space and close it with a cut-off valve in order to balance the whole 
system in the building.  
Radiant ceiling panels are most suitable for spaces with normal room height, 3-4 meter, such as offices, 
meeting rooms, and similar, but can basically be applicable anywhere. For very tall spaces and spaces 
dominated by sensory pollution other cooling solutions may be more efficient.  
 
 
Figure 36: Construction of ceiling cooling panels with plastic pipes embedded in gypsum board (bottom left and right), metal 
pipes (top right) and system ceiling (top left) (Rasmussen 2016) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:92==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
91 of 168 
 
4.6.4 Floor Cooling 
Floor cooling system can be considered as a radiant surface system. It consists of pipes embedded in 
either a concrete floor or a thin wooden floor with metallic plates connected to the pipes to enhance the 
heat distribution and increase the area of exchange. It is recommended to install the pipes in close contact 
with the surface to the space in order to achieve optimal heat transfer and faster reaction time for 
changing conditions. 
The floor surface temperature for cooling is recommended by standard (Dansk Standard 2008) to be 
limited due to thermal comfort issue, such as the risk of cold feet, to minimum 19-20¬∞C in spaces with 
sedentary occupant and 18 ¬∞C in spaces with higher activity level or spaces outside the occupied zone. 
The water temperature should therefore be in the range of 16-20¬∞C resulting in maximum cooling 
capacities around 42 W/m2.  
Floor cooling is especially attractive in large tall spaces with temporary occupancy and direct solar 
radiation. In cases of low heat loads, for offices and similar, floor cooling systems can with benefit be 
utilized for both cooling and heating. However, it is important to take into account the influence of 
furniture which can interrupt both convention flow and radiation from the surface. 
 
 
Figure 37: Floor cooling (Hovorka et al. 2012) 
 
4.6.5 Vertical Cooling 
Vertical radiant cooling systems can be considered as radiant panels and are typically placed within the 
occupied zone to improve local thermal comfort.  
For most spaces the available vertical spaces is often very limited due to doors, windows, furniture and so 
forth, making it difficult to integrate evenly throughout the space. Consequently, there is a large risk of 
creating a non-uniform environment within the occupied zone.  
Vertical cooling panels is not further included is this report due to limited range of application.  
 
 
==PAGE:93==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
92 of 168 
 
4.6.6 Thermally Active Building Systems (TABS) 
Thermally Active Building System (TABS) is pipes embedded into concrete structures, such as walls and 
decks. Also, Air TABS known as TermoDeck is possible, where air is supplied into hollow concrete 
decks. However, the most energy efficient design is to use water as heat carrier with decks as the TABS 
for largest surface and the most uniform uninterrupted heat distribution/absorbing. 
Pipes are embedded into the neutral zone of structural elements, as displayed in Figure 38, usually DN 
20-25 pipes with 150-300 mm spacing. This however makes it difficult to change or alter the system after 
installation. The building structure is then used actively as an energy storage interacting with the indoor 
thermal environment by absorbing or releasing heat, by natural convection and radiation, depending on 
the need. 
It is recommended to have exposed surfaces of the TABS to achieve a sufficient heat exchange with the 
space. Also utilizing surfaces with high conductivity or suspended ceiling with good air transfer ability 
are possible. Either way attention should be paid to ensuring proper acoustical performance in the space 
due to the large hard surfaces and the lack of installation space especially for ceiling TABS. 
 
 
Figure 38: Construction of TABS. Source: Uponor 
 
The aim is to enable an efficient use of the energy and level out the peak loads thus allowing installation 
of smaller chillers and heat pumps.  This also level out the energy production and thereby avoid most of 
the peak load production capacity potentially resulting in smaller production and distribution systems. 
Studies indicate a possible peak load reduction capacity up to 50% (Lehmann et al. 2007). 
In general, TABS are a base load system letting the room temperature drift around an equilibrium. It is 
self-regulating with constant mass flow and supply temperature control mostly adapted according to 
predefined schedule. Water supply temperature should generally be close to the design indoor comfort 
temperature in order to keep the whole structure at this level and avoid excessive heat transfer to/from the 
space.  
The water temperature should be in the range of 17-20¬∞C for cooling and 23-26¬∞C for heating (Stephan et 
al. 2011). Therefore, like other radiative cooling systems, investigations of the dew-point temperature 
should be made at each task, since it could become the dimensioning factor.  
The temperature sets results in cooling and heating capacities in the range of 25-39 W/m¬≤ which is 
average over an 24 hour period  when allowing a temperature drift over the occupied time of 4¬∞C e.g. 
from 21¬∞C in the morning and 25¬∞C in the evening (Stephan et al. 2011; Lehmann et al. 2007). However, 
==PAGE:94==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
93 of 168 
 
other guidelines indicate cooling capacities in the range of 40-50 W/m2 (Hovorka et al. 2012). As with 
floor cooling the cooling capacity of TABS may be significantly increased when affected by direct solar 
radiation. 
TABS have a long reaction time to changing conditions in the magnitude of 10 to 15 hours  due to the 
large thermal capacity of the structures (Stephan et al. 2011). This leads to difficulties in controlling the 
thermal environment without allowing excessive temperature drifting. TABS are typically not capable of 
removing all the heat gain during the occupied time. Therefore it relies on active charging and de-
charging during off-load periods such as night time and unoccupied time.  
The limited capacity and sensitivity to peak load results in the need of a high quality building envelope 
with effective solar shading and very limited heat loads. 
Consequently, it is recommended to use TABS to deal with the background thermal load and in 
combination with another thermal system with a much faster reaction time to deal with the varying loads, 
e.g. the already necessary ventilation system. 
TABS are most suitable for very low energy buildings with less restrictive requirements for the thermal 
environment and it may be a good solution for residential building. TABS have limited use in commercial 
building, offices, hospitals, etc. with varying conditions and should in these cases be combined with a 
second thermal system. Lastly, the low temperatures for heating and the high temperatures for cooling are 
especially attractive in combination with heat pumps, ground heat exchange and other low-
quality/renewable heat sources. 
 
 
==PAGE:95==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
94 of 168 
 
4.6.7 Phase Change Material (PCM) 
Phase Change Material (PCM) can be seen as a way to improve a TABS system and should therefore only 
be seen as an enhancer to another primary cooling system. PCM are materials with large specific latent 
heat capacity which can be used to store and release latent heat in the solid-liquid (melting/solidifying) or 
liquid-gas phase change of the material where the heat is absorbed and released almost isothermally. 
Typical PCMs can store five times the energy as a concrete slab (Olesen & Kazanci 2016). Examples of 
PCMs are paraffin and eutectic salts which is typically embedded in microcapsules and mixed into 
plaster, gypsum board or other surface covering materials. As illustrated in Figure 39, it is important to 
notice that only ~10-30% of the system is actual PCM. 
 
 
Figure 39: Microencapsulated Phase Changing Material (PCM) in gypsum board (Pavlov 2014) 
 
The efficiency, storage capacity and cooling capacity of a PCM system primarily depends on the phase 
change temperature of the PCM. This temperature is typically given as e.g. P23 meaning the change will 
occur within an interval of 23¬±2oC and thereby when the indoor temperature is within this range the PCM 
will start absorbing energy.  
For cooling, heat gains in a space are absorbed by the PCM during the occupied time and release outside 
occupied hours, during the night, or within given intervals. The heat can be released by either cold air in 
contact with the surface or embedded water pipes inside the PCM. The latter is the most effective.  
PCM may also be integrated in DIFCV systems. Here the low velocity air flow allows for the PCM to 
change phases, cooling or heating the supply air. 
PCM can be used to reduce the energy consumed by conventional heating and cooling systems by 
reducing peak loads by shifting the loads. Studies (Olesen & Kazanci 2016) indicates that at potential 
reduction in the peak load by up to 50% can be found.  
The technology is still new and fairly untested within the building industry and it still requires some 
further research and development before entering the marked as a proper alternative for cooling. 
Nevertheless, PCM currently shows a promising potential for energy savings and load shifting which can 
be beneficial for almost any type of space.  
 
 
==PAGE:96==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
95 of 168 
 
4.7 Evaporative Cooling (EC) 
Evaporative Cooling (EC) utilizes the adiabatic cooling effect to reduce the air temperature. EC systems 
should be seen as a cooling addition to ventilative systems ‚Äì and cannot stand alone. EC occurs when 
atomized water is sprayed into the air and evaporates. The evaporation process requires energy and the 
energy is taken from the surrounding air resulting in a decrease in the air temperature while increasing the 
relative humidity.  
EC systems should always be coupled with a ventilation system to ensure the IAQ and to avoid excessive 
humidity levels. Careful attention should therefore be paid to control the level of relative humidity in a 
space. Too high levels may lead to a dissatisfying indoor environment and undesirable mold/fungus 
growth. Challenges occur in case of hot humid air. Here the additional humidity needs to be handled in 
the AHU by dehumidification. 
Water treatment should be installed in connection to EC to prevent Legionella bacteria in both direct and 
indirect systems. 
EC can be done in three ways; directly into the space, directly into the inlet air, or indirectly into the 
exhaust air by utilizing the heat recovery unit in the AHU (Graca et al. 2015). 
4.7.1 Direct Evaporative Cooling 
Direct EC systems, as illustrated in Figure 40, typically consists of a spray nozzle in connection to a 
compressor unit pressurizing the water. The spray may very well be assisted by a small fan in order to 
improve the mixing with the air. The supply nozzle should be placed in the top of the space away from air 
terminals, where the temperature is highest and the spray is uninterrupted. The water is pressurized before 
entering the nozzle where it vaporizes into the air. The power consumption for pressurizing and suppling 
is in the magnitude of 0.01-0.10 kWh/kg of water depending on the type (Energiwiki 2013).  
It has not been possible to find any guideline values of the cooling capacity for direct EC. However, the 
maximum achievable temperature reduction, ‚àÜ‹∂‡Ææ‡Æº, from the direct EC in the exhaust can be calculated as 
following (Wescor HVAC 2015): 
‚àÜ‹∂‡Ææ‡Æº= (‹∂‡Øó‡Ø•‡Ø¨‚àí‹∂‡Ø™‡Øò‡Øß) ‚àôﬂù  
Where; ‹∂‡Øó‡Ø•‡Ø¨ is dry bulb temperature, ‹∂‡Ø™‡Øò‡Øß is wet bulb temperature and ﬂù is the efficiency of the media 
(typically ~90%). 
  
Figure 40:  Direct evaporative cooling in a space (Condair 2016) 
Direct EC is broadly utilized in all types of buildings located in regions with dry warm climates, where 
the rise in humidity also significantly benefits the indoor environment. It still has limited spread in the 
Danish marked, most likely due to unawareness and caution regarding adding humidity to the indoor 
environment.  
==PAGE:97==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
96 of 168 
 
Direct EC is most suitable for industrial premises such as data centers and production facilities where the 
increased humidity providing additional benefits to the production, such as reduced dust and static 
electricity. However, it can also be efficient in large tall space with high heat loads and/or high ventilation 
rates, such as atriums, auditoriums and cantinas. It may also be applied in large spaces such as open plan 
offices with large volumes. 
Direct EC should generally not be coupled with radiative cooling systems, chilled beams, or fan coils due 
to increased dew-point temperature of the air leading to increased risk of condensation. If EC is necessary 
during winter season attention should be paid to cold bridges and cold window surfaces which may lead 
to condensation.  
Direct EC is not suitable for spaces with normal room height and small volume in relation to heat load, 
such as single offices and meeting rooms, due to difficulties in proper mixing with the air without 
affecting the occupants and the fact that the investment cost in relation to the effect is rather high.  
4.7.2 Indirect Evaporative Cooling 
Indirect EC systems are often integrated in the AH, as illustrated in Figure 41, where the exhaust air from 
the building is cooled by direct EC which then utilize the heat recovery to indirectly cool the intake air. 
Thereby the supply air obtains none or a minimum amount of moisture depending on the type of heat 
exchanger, resulting in no significant increase in the indoor humidity compared to direct EC. The EC unit 
is similar size as a cooling coil with an installation length around 700-800 mm and since it is installed in 
the exhaust duct he AHU does not necessarily increase in size.   
It has not been possible to find any guideline values of the cooling capacity for indirect EC. The capacity 
is very depended on the external and internal conditions and therefore a dynamic simulation required 
revealing the exact capacity. According to Condair (Condair 2016) properly designed indirect EC systems 
in high quality buildings with standard Danish climate can reduce the ventilation rates in summer by up to 
40% without exceeding a relative humidity level of 55% under the assumption that the ambient air is 
18oC with 75% relative humidity and the indoor temperature is 26oC. The corresponding winter saving is 
up to 70%. The result will vary depending on the heat loads and external conditions. Therefore each case 
should investigate the specific capacities and potential savings. 
 
Figure 41: Indirect evaporative cooling (Hovorka et al. 2012) 
Indirect EC is suitable for a very broad range of building types with mechanical ventilation. However, 
attentions should be paid to buildings and spaces with high latent loads, such as kitchens, fitness rooms, 
and bathrooms, which can significantly reduce the effect of indirect EC due to high relative humidity in 
the exhaust air. Generally, indirect EC is not suitable for high humidity climates.  
In many cases EC may significantly reduce or completely remove the need of a separate cooling coil in 
the AHU and thereby also reduce or remove the need of a refrigeration system. The system is generally 
cheap in investment cost and has a small operating cost in water and electricity. However, regular 
maintenance and cleaning is required which increase the overall operation cost. 
 
==PAGE:98==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
97 of 168 
 
4.8 Recommendations 
This part will summarize the described cooling supply systems and highlight the recommended aspects.   
The selection of sustainable cooling supply solutions requires the following parameters: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Clear definitions of occupancy, use of space and requirements to the indoor environment. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Sufficient control of the heat loads with well-designed effective solar shading. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Building layout, space dimensions, and space available for installations. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Availability of supply sources: NV, district cooling, ground/sea heat exchange, etc.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Cooling effect of the basic air change rate.  
ÔÇ∑ 
The specific achievable cooling capacity for any cooling supply system will vary depending on 
the space configurations and the conditions within the space. Therefore, the cooling capacity 
should be investigated for each case. 
The cooling demand for most office spaces in Denmark is roughly in the range of 35-70 W/m2, but for 
spaces with high solar radiation or high occupancy such as atriums and auditoriums the cooling demand 
may be much higher. 
A list of the most relevant cooling supply system included in this report can be seen in Table 27 with their 
corresponding limitations in terms of cooling capacity, supply medium and applicable type of space. 
Common for all values are that they possess relatively large uncertainties and should only be seen as 
guideline values.  
Most cooling supply systems are available in a broad range of versions altered to fit into specific setups 
and are likely to be found in improved versions with higher cooling capacity than what is presented in this 
report. However, the basic functionalities, challenges and limitation of each system type remain almost 
the identical.  
It is recommended to utilize high temperature media for cooling in order to reduce risks of discomfort and 
condensation, reduce energy losses in the distribution system and facilitate the use of low-quality heat 
sinks to reduce the production cost of cooling.  
Water supply temperatures to radiant systems, TABS, and chilled beams should preferably be in the range 
of 14-20oC in cooling mode with a ‚àÜT of 2-4oC. For cases with low heat load the supply temperatures 
could be even closer to the room temperature. To cover the same load with higher supply temperatures 
larger supply flow rates are required leading to increased energy consumption for the pumps and 
potentially larger pipes. These two aspects have to be carefully weighed against each other. 
Air supply temperatures for ventilative systems should generally not exceed a ‚àÜT of more than 8-10oC in 
relation the space to avoid cold drop. The supply temperature in cooling mode should be in the range of 
14-18oC depending on the system type. Utilizing CC and DIFCV enables lower supply temperatures 
while maintaining thermal comfort even at high loads compared to MV, DV, HV and NV. 
The use of all air systems for cooling should, if possible, be reduced to the capacity of the basic air 
change rate or slightly above. Higher loads will result in large ducts and AHUs. Transporting energy 
through water is more efficient and requires significantly less routing space for pipes compared to air. 
Therefore it is recommended to integrate more water based systems such as radiative ceiling and CBs. In 
this context it is worth to keep an eye on PCM in the future, which shows potential to improve the cooling 
capacity and reduce the peak cooling loads by shifting the loads.  
Additionally, it can be beneficial to select a cooling system which can also be used to cover the heating 
demand in order to remove the need of a separate heating system. Typically the heat loss in new low 
energy buildings spans from 10 to 20 W/m2. Most ventilative and radiative systems are capable of 
covering this relatively low heat loads in an efficient way ‚Äì however air heating must not be the sole 
==PAGE:99==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
98 of 168 
 
heating source in buildings following BR20 and should be accompanied by convector or radiators which 
lead to an extra benefit of implementing radiant cooling systems capable of heating over all air cooling 
systems. 
This report has shown that there are clear indications of the possibilities and limitations for each system in 
terms of cooling capacity, source temperatures, supply temperatures, compatibility with other systems and 
integration options. On this basis it is possible to rule out the majority of supply options for a given case 
and perform more detailed analysis of the few options left. But there is no clear way to unambiguously 
recommend one supply system over another. 
More systems may be equally good for a given case in terms of comfort, flexibility, and integration. The 
selection will typically depend on either an estimate of the construction costs, often due to restrictions in 
the clients current funding, or an evaluation of the architectural and esthetical aspects of the system. 
The way to select a system based on cost is by performing a Life Circle Cost (LCC) analysis. It includes 
predictions of the cost of construction, maintenance, and energy consumption over the life time of the 
system.  
Lastly, it is advisable to design simple cooling systems without too many interactions with other systems 
and limit the numbers of heat transfers from the production to the space in line with the minimum thermal 
resistance principle to reduce energy waste. Simplicity enables easier design, construction, balancing and 
maintenance of the system. Complex solutions may work more efficiently in theory and design. But it is 
important to remember that the craftsmen and facility managers do not necessarily have the expertise to 
fully understand the system, potentially leading to errors in the construction, balancing and operation.

5 Distribution Systems 
This chapter will describe best practice design approaches for the distribution part of air and water based 
cooling systems. It will include sizing of ducts and pipes, balancing, dampers, pumps, fans, and other 
related topics to the distribution of cooling. 
The overall design approach of the distribution system is to transfer precise amounts of the cold media 
from one place to another. This should be done with a minimum amount of energy losses, both thermal 
and pressure. 
The cooling capacity in terms of the flow rates and temperatures can be controlled as a function of the 
outdoor and indoor temperature, and further refined systems may include more parameters such as solar 
radiation and humidity. Loads on hourly or daily basis require the ability to control and adjust flow rates 
and temperatures in the distribution systems to maintain a constant indoor environment in every space and 
at any time.  
 
5.1 Air System 
The air distribution system in a cooling system could look as illustrated in Figure 42. This section will 
include duct design, control strategies, balancing, and the parts in AHU.  
 
 
Figure 42: Air distribution system (Exhausto 2016) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:102==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
101 of 168 
 
5.1.1 Ducts 
It is recommended to design ventilations duct systems for pressure loss of ~1 Pa/m (Petersen 2011). 
Larger ducts allow higher flow rate than small ducts resulting in similar pressure loss per meter. For VAV 
systems the maximum air velocity in distribution ducts should be ~4 m/s to avoid noise in the dampers. 
Table 28: Recommended design velocities for ventilation ducts (Petersen 2011) 
Type of duct 
Maximum Air velocity 
[m/s] 
Main duct 
4-8 
Distribution duct 
2-4 
Connection duct 
2-3 
 
In general, ventilation ducts should be designed to create a minimum pressure loss to the system. 
Attentions should be paid to the routing of the ducts. The network should consist of as many straight 
sections as possible without too many corrections and bends.  
Rectangular duct bends should be fitted with rounded corners which may result in reduced pressure drop 
compared to sharp corners without additional costs or increased spatial requirements. Also, round duct 
bends should be installed with smooth longer curves and avoid short sharp 90 degree bends to decrease 
the pressure losses. 
 
5.1.2 Control Strategy 
The selection of a control strategy for ventilation systems is important to ensure the most optimal indoor 
environment is achieved in an energy efficient way. The best control strategy depends on the use and 
requirement to the individual space, to the building section (zone), or to the whole building.  
Three main control strategies are possible for mechanical ventilation systems as illustrated in Figure 43: 
Constant Air Volume (CAV), Variable Air Volume (VAV), and Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) 
(Exhausto 2016).  
Some of the main control parameters are indoor and outdoor temperatures, presence of occupants, CO2 
concentration, humidity level, pressure differences, supply temperature, and flow rates. 
Figure 43: Control strategies for mechanical ventilation (Exhausto 2016) 
Additionally, the system control can be divided into three (Graca et al. 2015): 
Fully automatic control lets the system operate according to the chosen control parameters to ensure the 
most efficient performance and it has proven to be significantly more effective than personal control in 
terms of energy and comfort.  
Personal control and/or manual override of automatic control are very important as it affects user 
acceptance of the indoor climate positively. Many studies and practical experiences reveal a high 
appreciation of personal control. 
==PAGE:103==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
102 of 168 
 
Automated user-feedback when operating windows, fans, solar shading, etc. is left to personal control 
but the BMS provides active feedback and specific advices of how to optimally use the specific 
components. However, this is a promising field of development, but its use is being heavily challenged by 
human habits like laziness and familiarization. 
 
5.1.2.1 
Constant Air Volume (CAV) 
Constant Air Volume (CAV) control strategy allows the fan to maintain a constant air volume flow in the 
system. The system to be either on or off at given pre-set time intervals or based on manual switches or 
movement sensors.  
The supply volume flow rate is constant at any time and the cooling capacity is adjusted by the supply 
temperature. CAV systems can benefit of having frequency driven fans to maintain a constant flow. 
Otherwise dampers should increase the pressure loss in the system or the extra air should be led directly 
to the exhaust ‚Äì both wasting energy.  
CAV systems should be designed for low velocity and low pressure drop. It is recommended to install 
motorized constant air flow controllers to maintain a constant flow and to facilitate commissioning 
balancing) procedure. 
CAV is applicable for single spaces with constant load with small fluctuations or in combination with a 
secondary thermal system or zone-cooling to accommodate multiple spaces with different or varying 
loads. Post-cooling (or heating) is however relatively expensive in investment cost. 
 
5.1.2.2 
Variable Air Volume (VAV) 
Variable Air Volume (VAV) control strategy allows the fans and dampers to maintain a constant pressure 
within the system. The system adjusts the flow rate depending on the presence of occupants either 
predefined or with sensors. The cooling capacity can thereby be adjusted by a combination of the flow 
rate and the temperature to achieve considerable more constant indoor environments compared to CAV.  
VAV systems should be designed for an index pressure drop with ducts designed for low air velocity and 
low pressure drop. As a rule of thumb the air terminal devices should be designed for pressure drop of 
~100 Pa, even though certain types of terminals can operate with much lower pressure drop (Lindab 
2008). 
Ventilation systems with VAV consist of mechanical flow controllers (dampers). The dampers are 
installed in connection to a space, a zone, or directly into the air terminal to provide the correct flow rate 
independently on use of adjacent spaces or zones. The dampers are either adjustable as on/off or two/three 
step control.  
In cases of large ventilation network it is often necessary to install dampers in the beginning of all 
branches to avoid excessive differential pressure on some individual VAV dampers, which may lead to 
noise and control issues when the flow is varying.  
Additionally, it is important to ensure a certain minimum available pressure over the damper in order to 
have accurate control of airflow ‚Äì also known as authority. However, emerging newly developed flow 
control devices are able to detect and adjust to very small air flows.   
To fully benefit of the VAV system the AHU must be installed with a variable speed (frequency 
modulated) fan to optimize the pressure in the system and to avoid on/off control which lead to uneven 
flow in the system especially at low air flow rate. 
==PAGE:104==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
103 of 168 
 
Based on an economical consideration VAV systems are more expensive to install due to the motorized 
dampers and more advanced to implement and balance compared to CAV.  
Based on an economical consideration the total air volume through a VAV system can be halved 
compared to CAV systems leading to a smaller energy consumption by the fans and less load on all the 
system components resulting in a longer lifetime (Petersen 2011). The extra investment cost can thereby 
be repaid by the reduced operation and maintenance costs. 
VAV is applicable in spaces with varying occupancy but relatively small fluctuations in the heat load, 
such as north facing offices where there are significant differences between maximum and minimum 
required flow rates. VAV can be used in combination with MV, DV, DIFCV and CC. 
 
5.1.2.3 
Demand Controlled Ventilation (DCV) 
Demand Control Ventilation (DCV) is an improved VAV system which allows the system to 
continuously step-less adjust and balance the flow rate to fit the specific need for each space 
independently based on sensor or user feedback from the spaces. DCV is typically controlled based on the 
indoor temperature and/or CO2 concentration, but can be combined with various other parameters such as 
solar radiation, solar shading control, window opening, humidity level, and interactions of the occupants. 
The dampers and air terminals used for DCV are similar to those in VAV systems, but not necessarily the 
same. They are often more advanced in order to fine tune the air flow and avoid draught, they often 
include more intelligent individual adjustments, and they require more sensors with a precise integration 
in a BMS. Therefore, DCV has higher investment costs compared to CAV and VAV. The additional cost 
should be repaid in terms of energy savings due to more precise and correct air supply, and the avoidance 
of unnecessary cooling or heating of extra supply air. Also the improvements in the indoor environment 
and IAQ should be appreciated.  
The position of each damper is continuously monitored and a control algorithm adjusts the static pressure 
to the minimum set point required to keep at least one damper ‚Äì typically on the critical path -  fully open 
at any given time - such system is called a Static Pressure Reset (SPR) (Bertilsson et al. 2012). This 
control/design strategy is optimal for energy saving by minimizing the static pressure throughout the 
system and also benefitting the precision of control by minimizing the differential pressure over each 
damper giving the dampers significantly larger working areas to adjust upon. 
DCV is applicable to spaces with high requirements to the indoor environment and with large variation in 
occupancy and heat load such as south facing spaces, meeting rooms, open plan offices, and auditoria. 
DCV can be used in combination with MV, DV, DIFCV, CC, CB, PV, and radiant systems. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:105==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
104 of 168 
 
5.1.2.4 
Temperature and Humidity Independent Control (THIC) 
Temperature and Humidity Independent Control (THIC) is proposed to realize the separate control of 
indoor temperature and humidity which basically divides the space conditioning in two separate systems 
with separate controls. The operating principle of THIC is illustrated in Figure 44. 
 
 
Figure 44: Operating principle of THIC (EBC 2015) 
 
The humidity control system is a ventilation system assuring sufficient outdoor air to meet the IAQ 
requirements and dry enough air to control the moisture load assuring sufficient indoor humidity levels. 
This system should be controlled as a DCV system. 
The indoor temperature control system is a thermal system such as radiant systems, CB, and FCU, 
assuring control with the varying heat loads.  
THIC systems avoid the entransy dissipations of coupled treatment of sensible and latent loads that 
negatively affect conventional all air systems. Studies indicate that when comparing conventional systems 
to a THIC system with radiant panels and heat pump driven liquid desiccant the energy efficiency can be 
improved by ~38% (EBC 2015). 
In general THIC systems can adjust to changing conditions of indoor temperature and humidity better 
than conventional all air systems, and they show significant potential for energy conservation (EBC 
2015).  
THIC is suitable for high temperature cooling applications in a broad range of building types were the 
ventilation system is responsible latent load and the IAQ, while the thermal system is responsible for the 
indoor temperature. 
 
==PAGE:106==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
105 of 168 
 
5.1.3 Balancing 
Balancing of the air distribution is important to provide the correct air flow rate and the correct cooling 
capacity to every space.  
All branches in the distribution system should have similar if not identical pressure. As a general rule, the 
pressure loss in the distribution air duct from the first branch to the last branch should not exceed ~40% 
of the static pressure (Lindab 2013b). 
Traditional ductwork and air terminals do not have self-balancing properties and should be assisted by 
additional dampers to balance the flow. More intelligent components are emerging on the market. 
Especially air terminals with self-balancing effects can significantly reduce the number of dampers and 
improve the overall balance and efficiency of the system.  
Poorly balanced systems result in higher energy cost usually due to unnecessary high pressure losses in 
the system, and consequently too high or too low supply temperatures and flow rates leading to 
discomfort and unhealthy indoor environments. 
The normal tolerance for the total air flow rate should be ¬±5-10% and ¬± 10-20% for individual air flows 
or rooms (Bertilsson et al. 2012). The air temperatures should also be within specified limits. 
Although ductwork balancing is the responsibility of the contractor, it is the responsibility of the design 
engineer to design robust ductwork which is easy to balance during the commissioning and 
recommissioning processes (Bertilsson et al. 2012). 
 
5.1.3.1 
Dampers 
Selecting the correct damper is important to ensure the most precise supply to each terminal independent 
on changing conditions in the total system.  
As a rule of thumb dampers and flow controllers should be selected for low velocity and thus low noise. 
Additionally, most dampers should have a minimum pressure drop of 50 Pa across the unit to maintain 
authority.  
Additionally, many types of dampers and measuring devices need a piece of straight duct before (~3x√ò) 
and after (~1.5x√ò) in order to obtain a laminar flow after e.g. a bend which enable proper accuracy of the 
flow control (Lindab 2013a).  
In general, the control can be done in three ways; air flow regulation, pressure regulation, or a 
combination.  
Air flow regulation aims to keep a constant air flow in the system at varying pressure levels. The damper 
is set to operate within a certain preset max/min interval for the airflow at a minimum preset pressure 
level. The damper then regulates independent on pressure changes. Most air flow dampers and measuring 
devices require a minimum flow velocity ~0.7 m/s to assure accurate measures (Lindab 2013b). Air flow 
regulation is mostly applicable for zone-regulation where the air terminal has individual dampers. 
Pressure regulation aims to keep a constant static pressure in the system at varying air flows. To maintain 
a constant static pressure in the system it is necessary to install pressure sensors in the ducts. Typically, 
diffusers and dampers are set and calibrated for a certain air flow variation at a given pressure. Correct 
pressures and pressure loss over dampers and diffusers results in a decreased risk of noise issues. Pressure 
regulation is mostly applicable in spaces or zones where the air terminal has individual motorized 
dampers (Lindab 2013b). 
==PAGE:107==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
106 of 168 
 
Optimally, dampers should be placed both in the supply and exhaust duct(s) to a space in order to ensure a 
precise balance between supply and exhaust flow. The two dampers must be connected directly or 
through a BMS ‚Äì a so called Master/Slave principle (Lindab 2013b). 
Dampers can be either manual, for one time balancing, or motorized for CAV or VAV/DCV purposes. 
For ventilative cooling it is generally recommended to use motorized dampers with step-less regulation to 
continuously improve and optimize the air flow or pressure level in the entire system resulting in energy 
savings when connected to fans with likewise step-less variable speed drive.  
Attention should however be paid to avoid excessive energy consumption by adjusting dampers position 
and controlling fans speeds. Too many and too rapid adjustments may result in energy consumptions 
which surpass the potential energy savings.  
State-of-the-art in damper technology is a so called venturi damper using a droplet shaped element to 
control the flow as illustrated in Figure 45 (LeanVent 2016). The air flow is controlled by pressing against 
the inlet by means of a linear actuator. The spherical shape of the damper ensures a sufficient free cross-
sectional area allowing the air to pass turbulence free around the closing element which also enables air 
flow monitoring over the damper itself. Consequently, the venturi damper provides a better and more 
precise regulation of the air flow compared to traditional dampers.  
It can be installed with and without motorized regulation suitable both CAV and VAV/DCV systems. 
Furthermore, the venturi damper is in the same price-range as conventional motorized dampers, while 
also saving the need for a separate air flow or pressure controller. 
 
 
Figure 45: Venturi damper from LeanVent (LeanVent 2016) 
 
 
==PAGE:108==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
107 of 168 
 
5.1.4 Air Handling Unit (AHU) 
The Air Handling Unit (AHU) is a vital part of any building with mechanical ventilation ensuring control 
of temperature, relative humidity and IAQ.  This section will include the most relevant parts of the AHU 
for cooling such as cooling coil, heat recovery, desiccant cooling, bypass, fans, and placement of intake 
and exhaust.  
Furthermore, the specifications of the AHU should be selected based on a range of parameters: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Requirements to the indoor environment 
ÔÇ∑ 
All air cooling system or assisted by secondary thermal system 
ÔÇ∑ 
Possibilities and limitations in the building design 
ÔÇ∑ 
Centralized or de-centralized system 
ÔÇ∑ 
The surroundings; urban/rural, traffic, pollution, etc.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Investment, operation and maintenance costs 
 
5.1.4.1 
Cooling Coil 
Cooling coils are essential for ventilative cooling strategies in order to supply air with the right 
temperature to the space. The most common cooling coil configuration is a counter-cross flow water 
circuit as illustrated in Figure 46 (Climaconsult 2013). As a rule of thumb cooling coils operate ~800 
hours in Danish climate conditions when the supply air temperature at the AHU is set to 17oC 
(Climaconsult 2013). 
The cooling capacity of the coil is determined based on a number of fixed parameters related to the 
geometry of the coil such as surface area and shape. The heat absorbed by the chilled water can be 
determined as (Vakiloroaya 2013): 
‹≥‡Øñ‡Øñ= ‹®‡Ø¶‚àô‹£‡Øî‚àô‹∞‡Ø•‚àô‹∑‚àô‚àÜ‹∂‡Øü‡Ø†

of rows in the coil, U is the overall heat transfer coefficient, and ‚àÜ‹∂‡Øü‡Ø† is the log mean temperature 
difference.  
 
 
 
 
Figure 46: Counter-cross flow Cooling Coil (Climaconsult 2013) 
 
For a counter flow coil the log mean temperature difference can be determined as:

==PAGE:109==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
108 of 168 
 
The only parameters which can be variated for a given coil are the water temperatures and the water flow 
rates, since everything else is fixed or uncontrollable, like the outdoor air temperature (‹∂‡Øî‡Øú‡Ø•,‡Øú‡Ø°). 
The air temperature after the cooling coil should be slightly lower, ~0.5-1.0oC, than the desired supply 
temperature to the spaces due to heat gains from the fan and the distribution system.  
The air velocities through the coiling coil should be above 1.5 m/s to encourage even air distribution and 
below 2.5 m/s to ensure condensed water is not carried off the coil (Climaconsult 2013). 
The water velocity should be above 0.2-0.3 m/s to ensure turbulent flow and below 1.5-2.0 m/s depending 
on the pipe material to avoid erosion. The water supply temperature is typically ~7oC with a ‚àÜT of 5-6oC 
(EBC 2015). However, these chilled water temperatures are conflicting with the general desire to utilize 
high temperature cooling in building applications and complying only with all air systems and fan coils. 
Other water based cooling supply systems require temperature above 14oC as stated in the previous 
chapter.  
A simple calculation reveals that if the supply water temperature is raised from 7oC to 14oC, then ‚àÜ‹∂‡Øü‡Ø† 
will be reduced by more than 50% resulting in a significant reduction of the cooling capacity of the coil. 
Consequently, the coils should be more than twice as big to achieve the same capacity and the water flow 
rate should potentially be increased. A larger coil leads to increased pressure drop on the air side and 
thereby affects the SEL value of the ventilation system.  
A higher water supply temperature will facilitate one common chilled water circuit for the whole building 
which also ensures a high evaporative temperature for the chiller leading to a higher efficiency.  
The additional cost of a larger cooling coil and increased operation cost of fans and pumps should be held 
up against the savings from a more efficient chiller and potentially reduce the need of a separate low-
temperature chiller with separate water circuit.  
The best way to increase the capacity of a coil without changing the water temperatures is to increase the 
number of water connections to the coil. Further, capacity increase can be done by increasing the number 
of rows of pipes. The number of rows in the coil should only be increased if the maximum permissible 
pressure loss on the water side is exceeded or if the capacity is insufficient. The pressure loss on the water 
side and the difference in water temperature increases with an increasing number of water connections. 
Also, longer circuit increases the pressure drop on the water side. 
 
5.1.4.2 
Change-over Coil 
Heating and cooling coils are traditionally separated, but they can also be shared coils, so called change-
over coils, switching between heating and cooling depending on the demand.  
A change-over coil is basically an ordinary water-to-air heat exchanger but it is connected to both heating 
system and cooling system enabling it to rapidly switch between the two temperature sets according to the 
needs. The supply connection to the coil can either be direct as displayed in Figure 47 or indirect through 
a heat exchanger in case of glycol is needed to avoid freezing. Indirect will however loss some efficiency 
through the extra heat exchange. 
Increased heat exchange area enhances the possibility to use water temperatures closer to the air 
temperature. Multiple change-over coils are therefore especially useful for high temperature chilled water 
and low temperature heating water.  
 
==PAGE:110==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
109 of 168 
 
 
Figure 47: Example of a change-over coil (left) (Sarrade 2011) 
 
5.1.4.3 
Bypass 
A bypass allows air to be lead around the heat recovery unit. It is utilized when the outdoor air 
temperature is close to or higher than the desired supply air temperature, typically above 16-18 ¬∞C, but 
still colder than the exhaust air. If the intake air is warmer than the exhaust air the heat recovery unit will 
transfer heat from the intake air to the exhaust air and thereby cool the intake air.  
Most heat recovery units, besides rotary, cannot modulate the amount of heat recovered. Therefore, a 
bypass is essential to reduce undesired heat gains to the supply air and thereby save energy on cooling. A 
bypass can be installed in combination with all types of heat recovery units. 
A bypass could also be constructed in a way which leads the intake air around heating coils, cooling coils 
and dehumidification units to reduce the pressure loss in the system. This allows the AHU to supply 
unconditioned cold air to the building at very low pressure loss enabling more energy efficient night 
cooling strategies.  
 
5.1.4.4 
Heat Recovery 
The largest component in the AHU is normally the heat recovery unit. For the main part of the year in 
northern climates it is beneficial to recover heat from the exhaust air to the supply air in order to save 
energy for heating the air to the desired supply temperature. The heat recovery unit must be able to 
provide dry temperature efficiency above 67% to fulfill BR15 and 73% to fulfill BR20 for non-residential 
buildings.  
In low-energy buildings very high recovery rates are unnecessary as the supply air temperature usually 
rises too high and thereby the indoor temperature is too high. The recovery rates should then be 
decreased. Scaling-down takes place in rotating heat exchangers by decreasing the wheel speed and in the 
counter flow by a portion of the supply air is lead around via a bypass. 
Having an indoor temperature of 23 ¬∞C in winter and outdoor temperature of -5¬∞C (during the day), the 
supply air temperature at 75% recovery is 16 ¬∞C, i.e. the fresh air is blown in with an under-temperature 
of 7 ¬∞C, which is normally permissible. However, the cooling load does not necessarily correspond to 
such a low supply temperature and further heating of the air is necessary to avoid thermal discomfort in 
the building. 
==PAGE:111==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
110 of 168 
 
At the same time the transmission loss is relatively small when considering new buildings that the internal 
heat contribution to a large extent should be removed by ventilating with cool outside air.  
In general, four types of heat recovery units are available; counter flow, rotary (thermal wheel), cross 
flow and coupled - see Table 29 for details.  
Heat exchangers have relatively high pressure losses compared to the whole system resulting in increased 
fan power. Studies indicate that is possible to reduce the pressure loss in heat exchangers from 200 to 80 
Pa by achieving more undisturbed air flow through the heat exchangers resulting in a reduced energy 
consumption of ~15% (DTU 2011).  
 
Table 29: Heat recovery units (Petersen 2011; Hovorka et al. 2012) 
 
Counter flow 
Rotary 
Cross flow 
Fluid coupled 
Illustration 
 
 
Efficiency 
~80-95 %

5.1.4.5 
Heat Recovery for Cooling 
When the indoor temperature is lower than the outdoor temperature it is possible to use the heat recovery 
unit to cool the intake air with the exhaust air. In practice this have very limited use in northern climates 
since the outdoor temperature rarely exceeds the allowed maximum indoor temperature in summer of 
26¬∞C.  
The possibilities are much higher in dry and warm climates where the average outdoor temperature is 
above the desired indoor temperature for longer periods.  
In very humid climates (tropical) the use of recirculation of air is however dominating to avoid extensive 
dehumidification.  
 
5.1.4.6 
Desiccant Cooling  
Desiccant cooling systems are based on the principle of evaporative cooling where water is used as 
refrigerant in direct contact with the air. The process can be driven by a thermal wheel (rotary heat 
exchanger) constructed with good sorption properties, i.e. a hydroscopic material, to exchange humidity 
between the intake and exhaust air (Solair 2009).  
Several desiccant cooling processes exists. One process of a solid desiccant cooling system is illustrated 
in Figure 48 and a comprehensive description of the process can be found in (Solair 2009). In short, 
humid intake air passes the desiccant wheel and moisture is absorbed by the hydroscopic material. The 
absorbed moisture is then rejected by the exhaust air which is heated before passing the desiccant wheel 
enabling it to obtain more water. After the intake air is dried by the desiccant wheel it passes a humidifier 
which cools the air by direct evaporative cooling.  
 
Figure 48: Desiccant Cooling (Hovorka et al. 2012) 
Careful attention must be paid to the amount of water applied to the intake air. The humidity should not 
exceed the indoor requirement, nor should it be so high that the cooling coil will hit off the water again. 
Desiccant cooling may reduce the required peak capacity of the cooling coil by up to 50%, while ensuring 
a more precise temperature and humidity control (Hovorka et al. 2012). Furthermore, this can improve the 
efficiency of the chillers by enabling higher supply water temperature to the cooling coil. 
The savings on the cooling coil and potentially the chiller should be set up against the cost of the 
additional equipment, the consumption of water and heating, and the increased construction length of the 
AHU. It is indicated in the literature that an overall savings on electricity and operation costs can be 
expected with desiccant cooling compared to traditional systems (Hovorka et al. 2012; Solair 2009). 
==PAGE:113==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
112 of 168 
 
5.1.4.7 
Fan 
Fans are used to transport air through the duct system and the type of fan has a large influence on the 
overall efficiency of a HVAC system.  
An important consideration of fans in relation to cooling is the increase in temperature caused by friction 
between the blades and the air, and the heat released from the motor if placed inside the air stream. 
Roughly, the temperature raise from friction can be expressed as a function of the raise in total pressure 
‚àÜ›å‡Øô‡Øî‡Ø° (Petersen 2011): 
‚àÜ‹∂‡Øô‡Øî‡Ø°=
‚àÜ›å‡Øô‡Øî‡Ø°
1000 ‹≤‹Ω/‹≠ 
The specific temperature raise depends on type and quality of fan. 
The choice of fan depends on a combination of the available static pressure and required air flow rate. 
The three most common fans are all centrifugal fans; forward-curved blades, backward-curved blades, 
and axial. Backward-curved blade and axial fans have efficiencies in the range of 80-85% while forward-
curved blade fans are around 70% (Petersen 2011).  
It is important to dimension the fan for the most common air flow in the system to ensure the highest 
efficiency is at this level resulting in the most optimal solution energy wise. However, the fan should be 
also able to supply higher and lower air flow, if required, at acceptable efficiencies. 
The air flow can be adjusted in multiple ways: Change the fan characteristics by changing the angle of the 
blades, add extra pressure loss to the system by a damper, or control the rotation speed of the fan by e.g. 
an EC motor or a frequency modulated AC motor. EC motors are preferable in terms of energy efficiency 
and maintenance.  
Fans in VAV systems must be equipped with variable speed drive in order to adjust the air flow in fine 
intervals to ensure constant pressure and optimal efficiency at all times.  
Fans in CAV systems should at least be equipped with three step regulation to adjust the air flow between 
high, medium and low, but preferably with variable speed drive to ensure constant volume flow at any 
given condition. 
Simple on/off control should always be avoided due the waste energy by starting and stopping the fan and 
thus also providing a more unstable air supply. 
5.1.4.8 
Intake and Exhaust 
There are several options to get air in and out of the ventilation system depending on the placement of the 
AHU. It is advisable to place the AHU together with intake and exhaust on the roof or one of the top 
floors in larger buildings ‚Äì especially in urban areas. This ensures good access to fresh clean intake air 
(away from traffic) and exhaust air will not disturb nearby buildings or pedestrians at ground level. 
Additionally, a short and simple connection between intake/exhaust and AHU reduce pressure loss and 
improves the energy efficiency of the ventilation system. 
The placement of intake and exhaust can furthermore benefit from the wind direction. Orientation in 
accordance to the most common wind direction can naturally assist the efficiency of fans in the AHU by 
pushing/pulling air in/out of the system. 
Intake and exhaust must be installed in ways which ensures protection of the system against various 
weather conditions, humans, animals, leaf, etc. which can destroy, interrupt, or decrease the functioning 
of the system. Additionally it must be installed in ways which avoid unnecessary pressure loss. The 
pressure loss for correctly sized and shaped intake and exhaust should be ~10 Pa (Lindab 2008). 
==PAGE:114==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
113 of 168 
 
5.2 Water System 
The water distribution system in a cooling system could look as illustrated in Figure 49. This section will 
include pipe design, hydraulic efficiency, balancing, pump selection, buffer tank, and the importance of 
return water temperature.  
Figure 49: Overview of a hydraulic distribution system (Bertilsson et al. 2012) 
 
5.2.1 Pipes 
It is recommended to design cooling pipes for maximum pressure losses of 100-250 Pa/m depending on 
size and operational time (Christensen 2003).  
Some recommended values for different steel pipe sizes and maximum pressure losses are shown in Table 
30. Attention should be paid to the operation time. Longer operation time results in larger energy 
consumptions, thus the additional cost of larger pipes may be a better investment than for shorter 
operational time.  
In practice, it is energy efficient to design pipes for a pressure loss between 50-100 Pa/m, but it is a trade-
off with the additional costs of larger pipes, fitting, valves, etc. 
Changing the pipe dimensions can have significant effect on the total pipe pressure loss, as the pressure 
drop increases with the velocity squared. The energy savings by increasing the pipe size must always be 
weighed against the extra cost associated with the use of larger pipe diameters. Also, the cost of fixtures 
increases for larger pipes. If using stainless steel pipes and fittings the additional costs from increasing the 
pipe size can be significant and should be considered carefully. 
 
Table 30: Design recommendations for cooling pipes of steel (Christensen 2003)
