5.2.2 Hydraulic Efficiency 
The hydraulic efficiency of fluid based systems is important to maintain. Corrosion and dirt depositions in 
hydraulic systems may increase the surface resistance in the pipes by between 15 and 70% the first year 
of operation which may increase to over 150% after 20 years leading to increased pump energy 
consumption by up to 35% only by the first year of operation (TA Hydronics 2015).  
Furthermore, air built up in terminal units takes up space for the water and thereby decreases the cooling 
capacity of the units. Air may also affect the energy consumption of pumps by moving air around the 
system instead of water. Also, dirt built up in valves can reduce the control precision and the flow 
capacity. 
Deposition of dirt typically occurs due to bad water quality and too low velocities in parts of the system. 
This can be solved with a water treatment plant and adequate balancing of the system.  
Corrosion is typically related to incorrect pressurization in the system leading to ingress of oxygen. This 
can be solved by adequate balancing or by installation of air extractors in the system.  
 
5.2.3 Pumps 
Pumps transport fluid through the pipe system and the selection of the correct pumps have major 
influence on the overall efficiency and energy consumption of the system. In cooling systems, the 
pumping energy costs represent up to between 7% and 17% of the total cooling energy consumption 
(Carette et al. 2013). Accurate pipe network design and correct pump selection can ease the balancing of 
the hydraulic system and lead to substantial saving over the life time of the system. 
According to Commission Regulation (EC) 641/2009 (EU 2009) pumps installed in systems with varying 
flow only operates at full-load less than 6% of the time resulting in part-load operation for the majority of 
the time (Table 31). The Energy Efficiency Index (EEI) for pumps can be calculated based on these 
values and it is the best way to compare the actual performance of different pumps. The value is usually 
given by the pump manufacturer. 
 
Table 31: Estimated operation time for pumps in variable flow systems (EU 2009) 
Flow rate 
100 % 
75 % 
50 % 
 25 % 
Time 
6 % 
15 % 
35 % 
44 % 
 
Generally, pumps are installed in two fundamentally different systems types with either requirement of a 
constant flow or a variable flow. 
Constant speed pump or multi speed pumps, with two/three steps, may be used for applications requiring 
constant flow and constant pressure such as primary loops of chillers, heat recovery systems, and cooling 
towers. However, they are still applied in a (too) broad range of systems. The majority of constant speed 
pumps run at its design speed regardless of load condition and cannot adapt to the fluctuations of the 
pressure in the system, resulting it being ineffective for 94% of the time due to part load conditions 
(Grundfos 2014).  
Variable speed pumps (VSP) with either EC motors or highly efficient AC motor with variable speed 
drive (VSD) are recommended for every system with variable loads and thereby always supply just the 
necessary flow. However VSPs with EC motors are always the best choice. EC motors are superior to AC 
==PAGE:116==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
115 of 168 
 
motor with VSD due to their very high efficiency in part load operation and their better, but not as 
obvious, full load operation efficiency. 
As illustrated in Figure 50 the energy consumption of VSP with EC motor (VS-EC) are significantly 
more efficient at any load condition, resulting in yearly energy savings above 50% compared to effective 
AC motors with VSD (Bertilsson et al. 2012). 
 
Figure 50: Comparison of input power values of four different pump types and 5000 hours of annual operation; standard 
constant-speed pump (ST), constant-speed pump with improved hydraulic efficiency (IM), variable speed pump with AC motor 
and VSD (VS-AC), and variable speed pump with EC motor (VS-EC) (Bertilsson et al. 2012) 
 
5.2.3.1 
Control  
A VSP acts on the total flow rate going through them where they are installed based on inputs from one 
or multiple differential pressure (dp) sensors located in the distribution network. However, a VSP can 
only adapt to one sensor input at the time. The dp sensor(s) should be placed strategically in the network 
to resample the pressure level in the entire system.  
Nevertheless, VSP cannot solely ensure correct pressure levels in all part of the distribution network and 
have to be combined with differential pressure controllers to protect the local control valves from large 
differential pressure variations when the load is varying (Carette et al. 2013). 
The process to optimize pump energy savings in the distribution network and obtain sufficiently flow and 
differential pressure to all terminals even at low partial loads can be explained in three basic steps 
(Carette et al. 2013): 
1. Use pressure independent control valves 
2. Install dp sensor on the index branch(es) 
3. Adjust VSP set-point to the large differential pressure 
Results obtained after renovation with dp controllers based on the three steps have demonstrated a 32% 
reduction of pumping energy (Carette et al. 2013). 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:117==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
116 of 168 
 
5.2.4 Balancing 
Hydraulic systems must be appropriately balanced in order to provide a correct flow and differential 
pressure to each terminal, regardless of the load condition in the total system. Thus, maintaining thermal 
comfort in the spaces and avoiding pumps operating on higher speeds than necessary.  
Inadequate balancing of the hydraulic system may lead to fluctuating temperatures and deviations in the 
cooling capacity which may lead to uncomfortable indoor environments and higher energy consumption.  
If the room temperature is just 1oC lower than the design value the energy consumption will increase with 
12-18% and on the other hand a reduction of the supply temperature by 1oC will result in a decreased 
efficiency of 4% in the refrigeration system (TA Hydronics 2015). 
Any hydraulic system must at least fulfill the following three criteria to provide a good indoor 
environment, minimize energy costs and prevent operative problems (Hydronic Engineering 2015): 
1. Design flows must be available at all terminals at full flow 
2. Differential pressure across the control valves must not vary too much 
3. Flow must be compatible at system interfaces 
In general, there are two ways to balance a system and a rule of thumbs selection principle is illustrated in 
Figure 51: 
On/off balancing should be selected if the requirements for precision are limited and the corresponding 
thermal unit has a long response time, e.g. embedded radiant systems.  
Proportional balancing should be selected for systems with larger requirements for balancing precision 
and the corresponding system is more sensitive or difficult to balance with fast response times, e.g. 
cooling coils in the AHU. The overall target for proportional balancing is to achieve the most linear 
regulation as possible by selecting a balancing valve with correct valve characteristics suited for the 
characteristics of specific unit.  
Also, the total hydraulic system design has a major influence on the control of each unit. Generally, the 
hydraulic design should minimize the importance of the valve authority (Hydronic Engineering 2015) . 
 
 
Figure 51: Rule of thumb for control (Hydronic Engineering 2015) 
 
==PAGE:118==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
117 of 168 
 
For cooling systems with rapidly varying loads modulating balancing valves which continuously adapts to 
the changing flows and pressures conditions, should be used to avoid imbalances in the system.  
The most common modulating balancing valve is a differential pressure controller which controls the 
pressure over a terminal and/or a regulation valve ensuring a constant preset differential pressure. A 
constant pressure results in a linear control characteristic of the regulation valve assuring more precise 
flow to the terminal. This system requires no additional balancing valves between the pump and the 
differential pressure controller ‚Äì it can however be necessary in very large hydraulic system.  
The best way to balance a system is by utilizing so called pressure independent balancing and control 
valves (Figure 52). Essentially it is a combination of a regulation valve and a differential pressure 
controller in one device. Thereby the installation cost is halved, the pressure loss is reduced, no other 
balancing valve or flow controllers are needed in the system, and the commissioning is eased by setting 
the maximum flow rate directly unto each valve. 
Pressure independent balancing and control valves can be applied either as a constant flow control 
controller or as a balancing and control valve if equipped with a motorized actuator. It can sometimes be 
built directly into the terminal units. 
                            
 
Figure 52: Pressure independent balancing and control valve (left) and differential pressure controller (right) from Danfoss 
 
 
==PAGE:119==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
118 of 168 
 
5.2.5 Buffer Tank  
A buffer tank is a thermal storage for chilled water on the distribution side. In cases with variable cooling 
loads it can be an efficient way to significantly reduce the peak production capacity and enable a more 
constant production over the day. Thus reduces the size and/or numbers of refrigeration systems and 
thereby also the investment costs.  
The reduction in peak load depends on load scenarios and the capacity of the buffer tank(s) and must be 
investigated for each specific case. One should also keep in might that buffer tanks may be a very space 
consuming mean to reduce the peak load demands due to often large volumetric capacity.  
The buffer tank is cooled when the cooling demand is low. Typically during the night where, in case of 
air cooled condenser, it benefits from lower outdoor air temperatures which enables an increased EER by 
decreased condensation temperature. The cold water from the tank is then discharged per demand. 
Furthermore, it may also benefit from free cooling when the sink temperatures are sufficiently low. In 
some cases with low peak load and high temperature cooling systems a cooling strategy only combining 
free cooling and buffer tanks may be sufficient to cover the cooling loads. 
Buffer tanks are also suitable where the cooling loads are small since it may reduce the number of starts 
of the chiller, thus decreases wear and energy consumption. 
Buffer tanks are mostly serial connected to the chilled water distribution system right after the evaporator 
as displayed in Figure 53, but they may also be parallel connected to the chilled water circuit. Parallel 
connected is only used for peak load and should be frequently recharged to maintain a sufficient 
temperature due to heat losses, while serial connected is continuously used as mixed tanks. It is important 
to note that serial connected buffer tanks should not be used in reversible heat pump systems. 
Buffer tanks should be designed to have a minimum temperature equal or slightly lower than the desired 
chilled water supply temperature. Lower temperature in the tank enables higher energy storage and thus 
less volume is required. This requires a mixing device to achieve the correct supply temperature and it 
have to be recharge when the refrigeration system can operate with lower evaporating i.e. when there is 
no cooling demand.  
 
 
Figure 53: Series connected chilled water buffer tank (Bertilsson et al. 2012) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:120==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
119 of 168 
 
5.2.6 Importance of Return Water Temperature 
Adequate balancing and selection of efficient pumps are important. However, to ensure the highest 
possible Energy Efficiency Ratio (EER) of the chiller unit is even more important in terms of the overall 
energy consumption for the cooling system.  
A constant log mean temperature difference between the chilled water and the refrigerant is essential for 
the EER. Cooling systems are typically designed with constant chilled water supply temperature. Thus it 
is important to control the return temperature, especially at partial load to obtain a constant log mean 
temperature difference. For a typical 7/12oC (supply/return) system, studies indicate a 15% drop in EER 
when the return temperature is decreased by just 2oC to 10oC (Carette et al. 2013).  
At partial load some terminals are turned off and some are running at low capacity resulting in a smaller 
take-off of chilled water. As mentioned before, it is recommended to use variable flow controllers and 
proportional control in systems with partial load conditions. For a terminals connected to a chilled water 
circuit by two-way valve (Figure 54) the flow rate will decrease at decreasing loads and the return 
temperature from each unit will increase leading to a higher return temperature benefitting the EER of the 
chiller. 
Terminals, placed in the end of a branch, can instead be equipped with three-way valves (Figure 54) to 
bypass some of the flow at low loads ensuring a minimum flow rate for the pumps and avoiding excessive 
heating of the supply temperature. This system enable fast reaction to changing conditions even in the end 
of each branch but when the three-way valve is closed the bypass flow is increased leading to undesirable 
lower return temperatures and thereby lower EER. Therefore, the use of three-way valves should be 
limited and should only be placed strategically where a minimum flow rate have to be maintained.  
If on/off control valves were used instead of proportional controlled valves the return temperature will 
decrease below the design value at partial loads which affects the EER (Carette et al. 2013).  
 
Figure 54: Two-way valve (left) and three-way valve (right) (Carette et al. 2013) 
 
 
==PAGE:121==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
120 of 168 
 
5.3 Recommendations 
This part will summarize the described distribution systems and highlight recommendations.  
Water and air based distribution systems should be carefully balanced and controlled in order to provide 
the most accurate flow to all terminals under any given condition. Furthermore, the system design should 
be simple and optimized in terms of pressure losses to improve the energy efficiency of the overall 
cooling systems. Complex systems might be better in theory and on the drawing board, but the designer 
should remember that the project also has to be realized on an actual construction site with various 
simultaneous works going on. Simplicity may also benefit the balancing, operation and maintenance of 
the systems.  
Following is a list of the most relevant design criteria for air and water distribution systems, respectively: 
 
Air system 
ÔÇ∑ 
For spaces with varying occupancy and cooling loads VAV or DCV control strategies are 
recommended to continuously optimize the energy consumption and indoor environment.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Step-less venturi dampers (LeanVent) seem to be the best damper for air distribution systems for 
flow control.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Air systems are heading towards more intelligent air terminals without the need for additional 
dampers in the branches, which reduce the amount of components and ease the balancing.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Cooling coils should be designed to a ~0.5-1.0oC lower temperature than the desired room supply 
temperature to overcome heat gains in the duct systems and from the fan. The coil should 
preferably be designed to handle high temperature water supply temperature above 14oC, 
otherwise a separate chilled water system is required in buildings with water based cooling supply 
terminals. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Implementation of change-over coils in the AHU can be a beneficial way to increase the heat 
transfer area and thereby utilize higher water temperatures for cooling and lower temperature for 
heating without imposing excessive pressure losses to the system due to increased separate coil 
sizes. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Fans in the AHU should have step-less regulation to optimize the ventilation flow and remove 
unnecessary pressure losses in the duct system. The best selection is radial fans with backward 
curved blades connected to an EC motor. On/off fans should in general be avoided. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Heat recovery is essential for energy consumption to achieve the correct cooling capacity in cold 
periods by increasing the supply temperature without applying heat. It may also be beneficial way 
to cool the intake air in very warm periods where the outdoor temperature is above the exhaust 
temperature. 
ÔÇ∑ 
AHU should be equipped with a bypass to utilize free cooling, reduce heat recovery rate and 
reduce pressure loss in the system especially when the outdoor temperatures are suitable for direct 
supply to the spaces. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:122==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
121 of 168 
 
Water system 
ÔÇ∑ 
Hydronic systems should be equipped with balancing valves. Variable flow systems should at 
least be equipped with differential pressure controllers. Pressure independent balancing and 
control valves are however recommended, thus no other balancing valves are required in the 
system.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Adapt the design of the hydronic system to the pump control mode which best suits the given 
applications in the system. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Variable speed pumps (VSP) should always be used since most pumps operate at part-load 94% 
of the time. EC motors are the most efficient compared to AC motor VSD with saving in the 
range of ~50%. 
ÔÇ∑ 
It is recommended to connect the pump(s) to remote dp sensors in the index branches to adjust 
the VSP set-point according to the largest differential pressure in the system, and thereby 
significantly reduce the pumping energy required compared to traditional solutions. This setup 
requires pressure independent control valves in the system, 
ÔÇ∑ 
Attention should be paid to maintain a high hydraulic efficiency in water distribution systems by 
utilizing water treatment and/or air extractors to remove dirt and air bubbles causing corrosion 
and buildups. Poor water quality and air ingress may lead to ~35% raise in pump energy by the 
first year of operation in new systems due increased resistance throughout the system.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Implementation of chilled water buffer tanks may be a good solution to reduce the peak cooling 
production capacity. However, it can be very space consuming.  
ÔÇ∑ 
It is important to control the return water temperature to the chiller and keep it as high as possible 
within the design limits. A 2oC decrease may result in ~15% drop in the EER of the chiller.

MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016

6 Refrigeration 
This chapter will describe the best practice design approaches and the available options for refrigeration 
systems. The focus will be on larger refrigeration systems with evaporation temperature (‹∂‡Øò) above 0oC 
aimed for comfort cooling in multiple spaces.  
A refrigeration system is often divided into two parts; a primary system which is the refrigerant cycle and 
a secondary system which is connecting the refrigeration cycle to a heat sink through a cooling 
water/brine circuit. The primary side may also be in direct contact with the heat sink with e.g. air cooled 
condensers. 
Additionally, it must be stated that a chiller and a heat pump have essentially the same system properties, 
where chillers are for cooling and heat pumps are for heating.  
According to the HarmonAC project the averaged installed refrigeration system capacity was 29 W/m2 
while the averaged used capacity was 2.9 W/m2 in the EU (Knight et al. 2010). This clearly illustrated 
that low part-load conditions are the most common condition for refrigeration systems in buildings and it 
also indicates a large design focus on the peak load capacity. 
This report will focus on 1-step refrigeration cycles with direct expansion. Cascade, 2-step, booster, and 
transcritical systems are not considered since they are mostly suitable for low temperature cooling and 
freezing application.  
 
 
6.1 Rating 
All new products for cooling production used in the European Union should be rated and labeled 
according to the Ecodesign directive (EU 2016) and it is recommended that the test is performed and 
certified by Eurovent (Eurovent Certification 2016). 
Chillers and reversible heat pumps for cases with variable load conditions should be selected with the 
highest possible ESEER and not based on the EER. However, the EER should not be completely 
neglected due its evaluation of peak load energy consumption which may be very relevant in certain cases 
with constantly high loads. 
Analysis of chillers available on the market in 2012 have ESEER in the range of 2-7 (Bertilsson et al. 
2012). The current development and regulations generally lead to higher ESEER sorting out the worst 
products and providing more products with ESEER above 7. Nevertheless, it is out most important for 
building designers to specify requirements to ESEER and EER along with the cooling capacity to avoid 
contractors installing low performing chillers.  
 
6.1.1 Energy Efficiency Ratio (EER) 
The Energy Efficiency Ratio (EER) is a way to evaluate and compare the cooling efficiency of chillers 
and reversible heat pump units ‚Äì similar to COP for heating ‚Äì based steady-state peak load conditions 
defined by Eurovent as displayed in Table 32.  
EER is the ratio between cooling power output and effective power input: 
‹ß‹ß‹¥= ‹≥‡Øñ‡Ø¢‡Ø¢‡Øü‡Øú‡Ø°‡Øö
‹π‡Øú‡Ø°
 
==PAGE:124==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
123 of 168 
 
Where; the cooling power output ‹≥‡Øñ‡Ø¢‡Ø¢‡Øü‡Øú‡Ø°‡Øö is evaluated directly at the evaporator, and the effective power 
input ‹π‡Øú‡Ø° is a combination of power input to compressor(s), controls and safety devices, and condenser 
fans. 
Table 32: Test conditions for EER according to Eurovent (Eurovent Certification 2016)

35 oC 
 
The EER enables benchmark comparison of the efficiencies of different chillers. However, the EER only 
include one specific full load condition which potentially represents only 1% of the operation time over 
its life time. Thereby, the EER in itself is not an adequate index for comparison or for estimations of the 
installed energy consumption. 
 
6.1.2 Seasonal Energy Efficiency Ratio (SEER) 
The Seasonal Energy Efficiency Ratio (SEER) evaluates the seasonal cooling efficiency based on the load 
profile and temperature variations of the heat sink (air/water) over a period. The SEER of a unit is the 
seasonal cooling output in European Average Climate (Strasbourg) divided by the total electric energy 
input including losses during the same period. 
The use of SEER is stated in EU regulation No 626/2011 (EU 2011) and currently it only concerns energy 
labelling of air conditioners for cooling of less than 12 kW. In the near future larger units will also be 
included. 
 
6.1.3 European Seasonal Energy Efficiency Ratio (ESEER) 
The European Seasonal Energy Efficiency Ratio (ESEER) is currently the most precise index for 
evaluation of the efficiency of air and water cooled chillers. ESEER takes into account a weighted 
performance at four different load conditions and varying outdoor temperatures in European Climates. 
The corresponding American version of ESEER is the Integrated Part Load Value (IPLV). 
The ESEER is determined based on the following formula and the given test conditions in Table 33:

ESEER gives a realistic indication of the overall chiller performance and is currently the best tool to 
compare the performance of different chiller units. However, the ESEER cannot be used to determine the 
exact energy consumption of a chiller at a specific geographic location. 
Furthermore, it is limited to standard temperatures with a minimum air temperature of 20oC which is 
unrealistic for major part of Europe. The fixed supply/return temperature is essentially also limiting the 
compatibility of the index with various applications. Consequently, the ESEER is not particularly suitable 
to evaluate high temperature cooling applications. Also, the incorporation of free cooling is necessary to 
make a full evaluation of the performance of a chiller.  
 
6.1.4 Free Cooling Seasonal Energy Efficiency Ratio (FSEER) 
A new index the Free Cooling Seasonal Energy Efficiency Ratio (FSEER) suggested by Airedale aims for 
the northern European climate where the use of free cooling chillers are relevant as illustrated in Figure 
55 (Airedale 2012). 
 
Figure 55: Ambient air temperature band used in FSEER (Airedale 2012) 
 
The test procedure is similar to ESEER however the conditions are slightly different as illustrated in 
Table 34. The test calculation indicates that on average 5% of the time it is not possible to use free 
cooling when the temperatures are above 25oC. 
The FSEER gives good representation of the energy efficiency of free cooling chillers. However, it is not 
yet broadly utilized by the industry.

6.2 Heat Sink 
To remove the absorbed heat from the refrigeration cycle the condenser must to be in contact with 
something colder ‚Äì a so called heat sink. In practice, there are four options for heat sinks displayed in 
Table 35. Depending on the geographic local there can be large variations in the availability, the 
temperatures and the initial costs for the different heat sink. For all systems if frosting is possible instead 
of water a water/brine solution has to be used.  
The different heat sink and their corresponding systems will be discussed later in this chapter.

6.3 Chilled Water 
Chilled water supply temperature (‹∂‡Øñ‡Ø™‡Ø¶) design is traditionally in the range of 6-10 oC with a ‚àÜT to the 
return water in the range of 6-8oC depending on the system design. Energistyrelsen recommends always 
to use ‹∂‡Øñ‡Ø™‡Ø¶ higher than 7oC with a minimum ‚àÜT on 5oC (Energistyrelsen 2013). These values are however 
mostly consistent with fan coils.  
It is important to design a chilled water system with as high a temperature as possible to optimize the 
energy efficiency of the production and distribution.  
For high temperature cooling applications the ‹∂‡Øñ‡Ø™‡Ø¶ should be minimum 14oC with a minimum ‚àÜT on 5oC 
which correspond to the minimum requirements for high temperature cooling supply solutions discussed 
in chapter 4 Supply of Cooling.  
The temperature difference between the ‹∂‡Øò and ‹∂‡Øñ‡Ø™‡Ø¶ should not exceed 5oC (Energistyrelsen 2013). This 
facilitates minimum ‹∂‡Øò between 10-15oC for high temperature cooling applications under peak load 
conditions. 
 
 
==PAGE:127==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
126 of 168 
 
6.4 Chiller 
The primary side of a refrigeration system typically consists of a vapor-compression refrigeration unit - a 
so called chiller (Figure 56). Traditionally, chillers are designed and optimized for operation at full-load 
conditions. However, the majority of chillers installed for building application will operate at full load 
conditions for less than 3% of the time, thus operating for most of the time under part-load conditions 
(Eurovent Certification 2016). 
The chiller(s) should therefore be installed according to the full-load capacity, but be optimized to run at 
part-load. The efficiency under part-load depends on the ability of the equipment to handle part-load 
operation. It should be able to vary the pressure levels and flow rate, thus varying the temperature and the 
cooling capacity. Especially the properties of the compressor(s) and the expansion valve are important for 
controlling the condensing (‹∂‡Øñ) and evaporation temperature (‹∂‡Øò) in terms of the overall energy efficiency. 
 
 
Figure 56: Chiller (Geoclimate 2015) 
 
6.4.1 Reversed Rankine Cycle 
Refrigeration is based on the second law of thermodynamic. Essentially, to cool an object it must be 
brought it in contact with something colder. The chilled water used in the building must be in contact with 
a colder object namely a heat exchanger in the refrigeration system controlled by the ‹∂‡Øò of the refrigerant. 
The vast majority of non-industrial refrigeration plants are 1-step cycles. The process in a refrigeration 
system is known as a reversed Rankine process where heat transfer occurs as a result of phase change in 
the refrigerant. The reversed Rankin Process is illustrated in Figure 57 and can be explained with 
temperatures as following:  
‹∂‡Øò< ‹∂‡Øñ‡Ø™‡Ø¶< ‹∂‡Ø¶‡Øú‡Ø°‡Øû< ‹∂‡Øñ 
The refrigerant is absorbing and releasing heat by means of enthalpy when phase changing. Liquid 
refrigerant enters the evaporator and absorbs heat from the connected water circuit resulting in a phase 
change to gas. The temperature of gaseous refrigerant is then increased by pressurization in the 
compressor before entering the condenser. The warm pressurized refrigerant releases heat in the 
condenser by contact with colder ambient air or water resulting in a phase change back to liquid. The 
refrigerant is then depressurized by an expansion valve to a lower temperature and sent back to the 
evaporator were the cycle is repeated. 
 
==PAGE:128==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
127 of 168 
 
 
Figure 57: Illustration of a reversed Rankine process in a log(p)-h diagram (Markussen & Skovrup 2014) 
 1: Superheated gas at low pressure and low temperature after the evaporator, 2: Saturated gas at high pressure and high 
temperature, 3: Sub-cooled liquid after the condenser, 4. Liquid entering the evaporator at low pressure and low temperature 
after the expansion valve. 
 
The pressure difference must be large enough for the fluid to condense at the warm high pressure side and 
still be able to evaporate at the low pressure side. Larger temperature difference between evaporation and 
condensation requires higher pressure difference and consequently higher energy consumption to 
compress the fluid. Thereby, the energy efficiency decreases with increasing temperature difference and 
increases with decreasing temperature differences. 
Lastly, it is important to note that there is a minimum temperature difference between evaporator and 
condenser of ~10 K for systems with mechanical expansion valves. 
 
6.4.2 Evaporator 
The evaporator is to absorb heat from the chilled water circuit by phase change from liquid to saturated 
gas. The evaporation temperature (‹∂‡Øò) is determined by the pressure level and the type of refrigerant and 
it must always be lower than the chilled water supply temperature. It is recommended that the temperature 
difference between ‹∂‡Øò and chilled water supply temperature should not exceed 5oC (Energistyrelsen 
2013).  
For comfort cooling applications ‹∂‡Øò is traditionally in the range of 0-15oC depending on the application. 
However, high temperature cooling applications with chilled water supply temperatures above 14oC 
facilitates ‹∂‡Øò in the range of 9-20oC. 
In general, high ‹∂‡Øò for cooling will increase the performance of the chiller. As a rule of thumb, a 1oC 
increase in the ‹∂‡Øò results in ~3% less energy consumption. Increasing the ‹∂‡Øò may require a larger 
evaporator which will increase the investment cost, but by using a larger evaporator, the compressor size 
may be reduced, thus potentially reducing overall cost. 
The two most common evaporator types are direct expansion (DX) and flooded. DX evaporators are 
mostly used in refrigeration systems with HFC or HFO refrigerants. Flooded evaporators are mostly used 
in refrigeration systems with ammonia.  
==PAGE:129==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
128 of 168 
 
6.4.3 Condenser 
The task of the condenser is to reject the absorbed heat from the evaporator by phase change from gas to 
saturated liquid. The condensing temperature (‹∂‡Øñ) is determined by the pressure level and the type of 
refrigerant and it must always be higher than the available heat sink.  
For comfort cooling applications ‹∂‡Øñ is typically in the range of 20-35oC depending on the outdoor 
temperature. The efficiency of chiller increases with closing temperatures between the condenser and the 
evaporator. The lower limit for the ‹∂‡Øñ is decided by the saturation temperature of the refrigerant, thus the 
pressure in the system, and the sink temperature. 
Air cooled condenser is dominant in smaller systems and should have a maximum ‚àÜT on 10oC between 
air and refrigerant to ensure an efficient operation (Energistyrelsen 2013). An air cooled condenser should 
only be used for units placed outdoors. 
Water cooled condensers through a water circuit connected e.g. to dry cooler, cooling tower, ground, or 
sea, is preferable for larger systems for capacity reasons. Water cooled condensers should have a 
maximum ‚àÜT of 6oC between the refrigerant and cooling water (Energistyrelsen 2013).  
For sizing of water cooled condensers the largest practical condenser should be selected since the energy 
efficiency is higher at the lower condensing pressures. The operating costs are very important when 
selecting condensers thus a higher investment costs of a larger condenser can usually be regained by a 
reduction in the operation costs within a reasonable time period (British Refrigeration Association 2009). 
 
 
Figure 58: Example of the EER as a function of the condensation temperature with constant evaporation temperature (Bertilsson 
et al. 2012) 
 
6.4.3.1 
Receiver 
The refrigerant is stored in a receiver at standstill to avoid liquid in the compressor.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:130==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
129 of 168 
 
6.4.4 Sub-cooling 
Sub-cooling occurs when the gaseous refrigerant saturates in the condenser and only liquid is further 
cooled. A small degree of sub‚Äêcooling is always necessary to avoid flash gas in the liquid line and it also 
increases the system capacity without increasing the power consumption. To assess the correct chiller 
capacity and efficiency, the effect of sub‚Äêcooling must be known. When calculating the capacity gain 
resulting from liquid sub‚Äêcooling it is important that the actual liquid temperature at the expansion valve 
entry is used, not the temperature of liquid leaving the sub‚Äêcooler (British Refrigeration Association 
2009). 
 
6.4.5 Superheat 
Superheat occurs when the entire liquid refrigerant is evaporated in the evaporator and only gas is further 
heated. A small degree of superheat is always necessary in order to avoid liquid droplets to the 
compressor. It is recommended to have 1-5oC superheat (British Refrigeration Association 2009).  
 
6.4.6 Expansion Valve 
The expansion valve is used to depressurize the liquid refrigerant after leaving the condenser. Basically, 
the expansion valve is used to control the flow rate of the refrigerant which regulates the capacity and the 
degree of superheat after the evaporator. 
Thermostatic expansion valves (TEV) must have a pressure difference over the valve of 6-8 bar to operate 
efficiently (Nielsen 2013a). This leads to a high necessary pressure ratio in the system and thereby more 
compressor power. The TEV is typically set to maintain a degree of superheat. 
Electronic expansion valves (EEV) are less reliant on large pressure differences and thereby the system 
can operate at lower pressure ratio. An EEV can regulate both the condensing pressure and the degree of 
superheat and thereby vary the ‹∂‡Øñ according to variations in temperature of the heat sink and the required 
cooling capacity.  
This greatly affects the system performance at varying conditions. Using an EEV instead of a TEV in a 
chiller may increase the performance by ~15% when operating with variable loads and conditions 
(Bertilsson et al. 2012). 
 
6.4.7 Types of Chillers 
This report will distinguish between four types of chiller setups: 
Air-to-Air: Direct air cooled condenser and direct air heated evaporator. Mostly found in small 
residential units or directly connected to the AHU.  
Air-to-Water: Direct air cooled condenser and water heated evaporator. Widely used in smaller 
refrigeration systems.  
Water-to-Water: Water cooled condenser and water heated evaporator. Broadly used in all types of 
refrigeration systems, especially in large systems. 
Water-to-Air: Water cooled condenser and direct air heated evaporator. Used in DX-split units and in 
water loop systems with small decentralized air condition units.  
 
==PAGE:131==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
130 of 168 
 
6.4.8 DX Spilt System 
A DX split system is essentially an air-to-air or water-to-air chiller/heat pump with a separation (split) of 
condenser and evaporator into two different places.  It consists of pipes with refrigerant passing through 
an outdoor condenser in connection to one or multiple indoor evaporators as illustrated in Figure 59. The 
evaporator is typically a fan coil unit and the condenser is typically air cooled. 
The system setup is very simple without additional heat exchangers as found in most chiller systems. The 
simplicity results in large charges of refrigerant due to the length of circulation pipes inside the building 
when multiple evaporators (multi-split) are connected.  
Refrigerant transported around a building may pose a larger safety risk and is generally not 
recommended. Consequently, DX split units are mostly found as small residential units with one 
condenser and one evaporator with a short distance in between.  
 
 
Figure 59: DX spilt system (EBC 2015) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:132==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
131 of 168 
 
6.5 Refrigerant 
Properties and behavior of the refrigerant are important for the design of a refrigeration system and its 
components.  
As a rule of thumb a 10 kg charge of HFC is equivalent to ~35-45kW refrigeration above 0ÀöC in an 
optimized HVAC systems (Exhausto 2012). However, according to a conversation with experienced 
refrigeration engineer Stig Hansen from EKJ 10 kg HFC correspond to ~80 kW for high temperature 
cooling. 
 
6.5.1 Volumetric Refrigerating Capacity 
The volumetric refrigeration capacity of a refrigerant describes how much heat a refrigerant can absorb 
per volume. The higher the volumetric refrigerating capacity (latent heat through the changing of phases), 
the smaller the compressor displacement can be, leading to smaller compressors and lower mass flow 
rates, resulting in smaller charge of refrigerants, smaller pipes and less energy for compressor (Bitzer 
2015b).  
The volumetric refrigeration capacity ›ç‡¨¥‡Ø© of a refrigerant under given conditions can be determined as: 
›ç‡¨¥‡Ø©= ‚àÜ‚Ñé‡Øò
›í‡Øò,‡Ø¢‡Ø®‡Øß
 [›á‹¨/›â‡¨∑] 
Where ‚àÜ‚Ñé‡Øò is enthalpy difference over the evaporator [kJ/kg] and ›í‡Øò,‡Ø¢‡Ø®‡Øß is specific volume out of the 
evaporator [m3/kg]. 
 
6.5.2 Pressure  
The pressure ratio is the ratio between the condensing and evaporating pressure. The low pressure side 
(evaporator) should preferably not be below the atmospheric pressure which may lead to air entering the 
system (Nielsen 2013a).  The condensation pressure should be well below the critical pressure of the 
refrigerant to be able to condense the refrigerant (Nielsen 2013a).  
 
6.5.3 Evaluation of Refrigerants 
Refrigerants are developed and optimized for different conditions and for different applications. For 
comfort cooling application it is important to select a refrigerant that is capable of operating with ‹∂‡Øò 
above 0oC and up to between 10-20oC. 
The evaluation of which refrigerant is the most optimal for a given refrigeration system is complicated.  
The evaluation could be based on the following points: 
ÔÇ∑ 
GWP lower than 2500 to comply with the F-gas regulation in 2020 ‚Äì preferably much lower 
ÔÇ∑ 
Charge limit for HFC and HFO is 10 kg per separate unit without dispensation 
ÔÇ∑ 
Charge limit for natural refrigerants and HC are at the practical limit 
ÔÇ∑

ÔÇ∑ 
Low pressure (reduced component cost) 
ÔÇ∑

ÔÇ∑

ÔÇ∑

and corrosiveness (material choice) 
ÔÇ∑ 
Refrigerants should never be selected based on a single parameter e.g. GWP

To wrap it up, it is not possible to make a recommendation between the included refrigerants since they 
all achieved similar EER. The difference was in how they achieved the same EER.  
To make a better evaluation of the refrigerants dynamic simulations of each refrigerant including precise 
properties of all equipment (evaporator, condenser, compressor, expansion valve, pipes, receiver, etc.) are 
necessary. Thus, the market is relying on data from the manufacturer in combination with standardized 
rating and test conditions such as Eurovent.   
In general, it seems like the manufacturer tends to focus on developing chillers for HFC and especially 
HFO due to their low flammability and low toxicity.  
In the end, selection of refrigerants depends on the cost of the safety precautions, specifications and costs 
of the chillers, size of the compressor, and the operational conditions. However, this list has narrowed the 
number of options for suitable refrigerants for high temperature cooling chillers.

6.6 Compressor 
The compressor(s) is the most important component in regards to the energy consumption and 
performance of the refrigeration system. The selection of compressors should focus on the part-load 
conditions rather than the full-load to achieve the highest ESEER.  
The most efficient control technology for chillers work with varying loads condition is variable speed 
compressors. Variable pumps are used to adjust ‹∂‡Øò and the cooling capacity based on load condition and 
external condition. All other technologies have lower efficiency and higher energy use (Bertilsson et al. 
2012). Similar to circulation pumps in the distribution system, EC motors are the most efficient to 
continuously adapt to changing conditions and to the required pressure ratio. 
For part-load control in larger systems two or more compressors may be coupled in parallel. In a system 
with a constant minimum load it can be beneficial to install a single speed compressor to cover this load 
and install one or more parallel coupled variable speed compressors to deal with the variable load. The 
design may result in more optimal operation of all the compressors leading to a higher overall efficiency 
compared to one large variable speed compressor. 
All new chillers should be equipped with electronic controls on compressor, fans, electronic expansion 
valve, etc., capable of dynamically adapting the temperature of condensation and evaporation to the 
changing conditions. 
When the temperature of the heat sink is low the ‹∂‡Øñ may also be lowered by reducing the pressure on the 
condenser side resulting in less compressor energy required, thus energy savings.  
Lastly, it is important to notice that the compressor should be selected based on the properties of the 
selected refrigerant. Most often a compressor has to be adapted to work with a given refrigerant and some 
types of compressors are not compatible with some refrigerants, such as ammonia with scroll compressor. 
 
6.6.1 Isentropic Efficiency 
The isentropic efficiency of a compressor should be as high as possible inside its working area and it is an 
important parameter for achieving a high EER and ESEER in the refrigeration system.  
An isentropic efficiency of 1 means there is no losses in the compressor. However, there will always be 
losses in a compressor from irreversible processes such as friction, heat transfer, stamps, bearings, etc. 
Most new compressors can obtain isentropic efficiency between 0.6-0.9 throughout their working area.  
 
6.6.2 Limits  
The maximum and minimum pressure and temperature levels in the system are determined by the ability 
of the selected compressor(s).  The compressor should be selected so it can handle all possible variations 
of temperature and pressure for the given application. For high temperature cooling applications mainly 
Te between 0-20oC and Tc between 15-35oC, thus the corresponding pressure levels. 
 
6.6.3 Oil 
It is important to select the correct oil (lubrication) for the compressor and for the refrigerant. A wrong 
selection of lubrication can destroy both compressor and refrigerant. Overview of lubrication of each type 
of refrigerant can be found in e.g. Bitzer Refrigerant Report 18 (Bitzer 2015b). 
==PAGE:136==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
135 of 168 
 
6.6.4 Noise and Vibration 
The compressor is the only moving part in the refrigeration cycle and thus the only source of noise and 
vibration. Excessive noise and vibrations from the compressor often leads to a long range of precautions 
in terms of separate machine rooms, sound insulation and heavy concrete beds to absorb the vibrations.  
The evaluation of noise and vibrations from compressors is therefore important to include in the overall 
assessment when selecting refrigeration system. 
 
6.6.5 Compressor Types 
Traditionally, five compressor types are used in chillers: rotary vanes, reciprocating (piston), scroll, 
screw, and centrifugal (Figure 60). They can all be found in a broad variety as open, semi-hermetic and 
hermetic. Detailed description of each compressor type can be found in various literature e.g. in Noget om 
k√∏leteknik (Nielsen 2013a).  
An overview of the performance ranges for different compressor types are displayed in Table 37.

State-of-the-art in variable speed compressor technology is an oil-free centrifugal compressor with 
magnetic bearings such as Turbocor from Danfoss (Danfoss 2016). It is optimized to work with R134a 
and R1234ze which means that a dispensation is currently required in Denmark for systems above 10kg 
charge.  
R134a have a higher capacity of around 25% compared to R1234ze in the same compressor as illustrated 
in the evaluation of refrigerant in the previous section due to higher volumetric refrigerant capacity. 
Consequently, the compressor has to be larger to achieve the same cooling capacity. However, R1234ze 
will achieve 5% higher EER compared to R134a (Geoclimate 2015).  
==PAGE:137==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
136 of 168 
 
It has been difficult to obtain truly impartial studies comparing different compressor performances. Some 
less impartial studies involving the manufacturer have indicated that Turbocor is able to reduce the 
energy consumption by 30-50 % for chillers with mainly part-load operation compared to conventional 
screw or reciprocating compressor (Geoclimate 2015).  
Figure 61 illustrates the relationship in performance (COP/EER) between a Turbocor and a high 
performance screw compressor (similar pattern can be found with reciprocating compressor). It is clear to 
see a significant difference in performance under part-load conditions leading to a large difference in the 
ESEER. However, the difference is more insignificant in the range of 80-100% resulting in a more 
difficult choice for chillers operating at constant high loads. 
With the outstanding part-load performance of the Turbocor it is worth to consider an excessive over 
sizing to achieve higher EER. However, it will lead to a much smaller operational space for the 
compressor to adjust upon and basically require high basic loads. Alternatively, a number of parallel 
coupled Turbocor compressor which together are oversized may be an optimal solution for improved 
energy performance. The extra/oversized compressor(s) will result in higher initial cost, and must be 
compared to the potential energy savings to find the optimal solution.  
Turbocor is not only superior in performance. The following benefits are well worth to include in the 
comparison (Geoclimate 2015): 
ÔÇ∑ 
Reduced maintenance as a result of oil-free operation and limited number of moving parts. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Small size and only 1/5 of the weight compared to screw and reciprocating at same capacity. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Low noise down to 67-70 dB at 5 meter distance. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Almost vibration free operation due to the magnetic bearings removes the need for anti-vibration 
mounting and heavy absorbing supports.  
 
 
Figure 61: Comparison in performance for water cooled chiller with Turbocor (TT500) and leading screw compressor, 
respectively (Danfoss 2016)

6.7 Passive Cooling 
The potential for passive or free cooling should always be one of the initial investigations for any case. 
When the heat sink is colder than the desired ‹∂‡Øò it is possible to bypass the refrigeration cycle and 
exchange heat directly between the secondary system and the chilled water circuit. 
The cooling is done solely by means of auxiliary energy from circulation pumps and fans in dry coolers, 
or from circulation pumps to ground or sea water heat exchangers.  
The implementation of passive cooling may result in higher initial costs for the system. Usually, the 
additional costs are insignificant compared to potential energy savings from passive cooling.  
Passive cooling is mostly only possible in water-to-water system (Stephan et al. 2011). For air cooled 
condensers (air-to-water) a dry cooler or cooling tower needs to be implemented to utilize passive 
cooling. 
Utilizing higher temperature cooling application enables longer operation time for passive cooling and 
open up for different low quality source with temperatures in the range of 10-15oC, and thereby saves 
energy. Passive cooling is thereby feasible in combination with a long list of space supply options as 
mentioned in chapter 5 Supply of Cooling. 
 
6.8 Water Heat Exchanger 
Refrigerant-to-water heat exchangers are dominated by plate, coaxial, or shell-and-tube heat exchanger 
(Figure 62). The heat exchanger in both evaporator and condenser may also be a refrigerant-to-air heat 
exchanger such as a finned tube coil placed integrated in an AHU (Figure 63).  
The most common heat exchanger for high temperature cooling systems are plate heat exchangers due to 
its compact design, small content of refrigerant, and high overall heat transfer coefficient (Markussen & 
Skovrup 2014). Shell-in-tube heat exchangers are also very common but they typically require a larger 
charge of refrigerant in the system compared to plate heat exchangers (Markussen & Skovrup 2014). 
The efficiency of the different heat exchangers is very product specific and depended on the type of 
refrigerant, temperatures, pressure level and exchange media (water/air).  
  
 
Shell-in-tube 
 
Plate 
 
Coaxial (tube-in-tube) 
Figure 62: Evaporator heat exchangers (Markussen 2014) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:139==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
138 of 168 
 
6.9 Air Cooled Condenser 
The ambient air is the most commonly used heat sink for refrigeration due to its easy access and unlimited 
availability. However, the ambient temperature and humidity frequently fluctuate over the day and over 
the year leading to large variations in the energy efficiency.  
Air cooled condensers rely on forced convection from fans to directly cool the condenser. As a rule of 
thumb the temperature difference between the condensing refrigerant and the air should not exceed 10oC 
(Energistyrelsen 2013).  
In general, air intake should be placed away from other heat producing applications and warm spots 
which can increase the air temperature and thus lower the efficiency. Furthermore, direct sun should be 
limited or avoided due to interference with the heat transfer process and decrease the efficiency. 
Typically, air cooled condensers are integrated in the refrigeration unit, as displayed in Figure 63, and 
thereby it have to be placed outside. Refrigeration systems placed indoor can still have an air cooled 
condenser by a finned tube coil placed in a ventilation duct with a fan going through the space and 
directly to the ambient.  Lastly, the condenser can be a separate dry cooler where the refrigerant is 
refrigerant is circulated directly instead of through a secondary water/brine circuit ‚Äì essentially a DX spilt 
system. 
 
 
Finned tube coil for duct connection 
 
Integrated unit 
 
Dry cooler 
Figure 63: Air cooled condenser (Markussen 2014) 
 
 
==PAGE:140==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
139 of 168 
 
6.10 Water Cooled Condenser 
Water cooled condensers are connected to a secondary water/brine circuit typically through a plate heat 
exchanger which is in contact with a heat sink to reject the heat. It is the most widespread solution for 
larger refrigerant systems.  
As a rule of thumb the temperature difference between the condensing refrigerant and the cooling water 
should not exceed 5-6oC (Energistyrelsen 2013).  
The secondary circuit may consist of one or more of the following devices: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Cooling Tower 
ÔÇ∑ 
Dry Cooler 
ÔÇ∑ 
Ground heat exchanger 
ÔÇ∑ 
ATES 
ÔÇ∑ 
Surface water heat exchanger 
It is important to control the capacity of the secondary system in continuous collaboration with load 
variations in the refrigeration system to achieve the highest overall efficiency.  
Higher ‹∂‡Øñ may lower the heat exchange area of the secondary system due to higher temperatures between 
sink and condenser.   
Remote air cooled condensers, such as cooling tower and dry coolers, should not be positioned in full sun 
as this can add up to 10¬∞C in solar gain and will affect performance (British Refrigeration Association 
2009). Screens should be considered to reduce solar gain. 
 
6.10.1 Cooling Tower 
Cooling Tower is an evaporative condenser with forced convection by a fan. It utilizes water spray to 
lower the inlet air temperature before it passes the heat exchanger connected to the condenser. There are 
several methods to do so and one is illustrated in Figure 64. It is generally necessary to implement a water 
treatment system to avoid build-up of deposits and reduce bacteria and especially legionella growth.   
Evaporative condensers are traditionally not applied on refrigeration systems in northern Europe, but they 
are frequently used in dry warm climates where the use of dry coolers can be difficult and require very 
high condensation temperatures.  
 
 
 
 
Figure 64: Cooling Tower (Markussen 2014) 
 
==PAGE:141==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
140 of 168 
 
6.10.2 Dry Cooler 
Dry coolers are based on forced convection across e.g. finned tubes with water or brine to avoid freezing, 
from the condenser. The refrigerant may also be circulated directly to the dry cooler. There are several 
design types of dry cooler and since the capacity is mainly given by the heat exchange surface the most 
space effective type is a so call V-coil with finned tubes (Figure 65).  
Dry coolers are broadly used in larger refrigeration systems where direct air cooled condensers have 
insufficient capacity or if the refrigerant system needs to be placed indoor for safety, noise, or practical 
reasons. The size, shape and number of dry coolers are determined based on the cooling demand, the 
ambient temperatures, and the water flow rates. 
The capacity of dry coolers can be regulated by changing the speed of the fan(s) and/or the circulation 
pump(s), thus both fans and pumps must have variable speed drive.  
It is recommended to have a temperature difference on the air side less than 10K between inlet and outlet 
to achieve the highest efficiency (Stephan et al. 2011).  
 
Figure 65: V-coil dry cooler (Markussen 2014) 
 
6.10.3 Ground Heat exchanger 
The ground is a stable heat sink with temperatures fluctuating only a few degrees over the year in the 
range of 8-12oC in the upper layers and more constant with increasing depths (Stephan et al. 2011). The 
low temperatures make it ideal for passive cooling and as a heat sink. However, it has not been possible to 
find specific design guideline values for utilizing the ground as a heat sink. 
There are two ways to utilize the ground as heat sink: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Vertical: Pipes placed in often >100m deep boreholes to achieve constant ground temperatures. It 
requires small surface area per borehole, making it suitable for urban areas, but each borehole 
should be placed with a certain distance to avoid interference of the heat exchange. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Horizontal: Pipes placed parallel and close to the surface with fluctuating ground temperatures. It 
requires large surface areas making it suitable for small applications in urban areas or large 
applications in rural areas.  
Studies indicate that up to 80% of the yearly cooling demand can be delivered using vertical boreholes 
(Stephan et al. 2011). The capacity of a ground heat exchange system depends on the pipe length and 
different soil parameters. The practical maximum capacity of ground heat exchangers is limited by the 
investment costs and the running cost of pumps due to increasing pressure losses in longer pipes.  
==PAGE:142==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
141 of 168 
 
 
 
Figure 66: Ground water heat exchange system setup (Stephan et al. 2011) 
 
Careful attention should be paid to the heat balance in the ground. Excessive use of the ground as a heat 
sink will over time increase the mean temperature in the ground leading to various issues depending on 
soil properties and ground water levels.  
For constant and/or excessive use it is advisable to extract the added heat (or more) from the ground 
outside the cooling season to achieve equilibrium over the year. This can be done with a reversible heat 
pump for heating purposes or by circulation of water/brine cooled by a dry cooler.  
Consequently, the ground can also act as a thermal storage by cooling the ground more than necessary 
outside the cooling season and extract it again inside the cooling season. Thereby the system should 
consider both the peak load and the average load. However, this will require a non-constant cooling 
demand over the year 
In general, utilizing the ground as heat sink implies high investment costs and it can be challenging to 
make precise predictions of the thermal behavior in the ground even with thermal simulations and field 
studies.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:143==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
142 of 168 
 
6.10.4 Aquifer Thermal Energy Storage (ATES)  
Aquifer Thermal Energy Storage (ATES) is a ground water cooling system. The ground water is a stable 
heat sink with a constant temperature in the range of 8-10oC. The low temperatures make it ideal for 
passive cooling and as a heat sink for high temperature cooling. 
ATES systems should ensure thermal balance (equilibrium) of the ground water over a yearly cycle ‚Äì 
meaning no net-gain of heat. The basic cooling principle is to subtract ground water from a cold well, 
pass it through a heat exchanger in a closed loop and reinject it in a warm well as illustrated in Figure 67. 
The essential is that there is not consumed any ground water in the process.  
The process can be reversed outside the cooling season to produce heat and thereby cool the ground water 
to achieve thermal balance. The ground water can also be cooled by means of dry coolers during winter.  
Several combinations with ground water as heat sink and heat source is possible. This report will not go 
into further details and additional information can be found here (Niemi et al. 2009). 
ATES is only economically feasible when the cooling load is above ~300 kW (Niemi et al. 2009). 
Currently the largest ATES system in Denmark is at CHP airport with 5 MW cooling effect. The expected 
pay back compared to traditional compression cooling is 4-10 year depending on the setup (Enopsol 
2016). 
 
Figure 67: ATES in cooling mode (Niemi et al. 2009) 
 
In Denmark, Milj√∏styrelsen and the local government have to approve the use of ground water as heat 
sink/source according to BEK 1716 (Milj√∏styrelsen 2016b). This requires well-documented test by 
boreholes to make a detailed analysis of the underground, which is expensive to perform. However, if 
successful the test boreholes can be used for the system and thereby regain some of the costs.  
Special attention should be paid to local requirements to drinking water. If the ground water in the area is 
used for drinking water or the use of the area can influence the quality or the temperature by more than 
0.5oC of nearby drinking water wells then the construct request will most certainly be denied 
(Milj√∏styrelsen 2016b).  
The ground water system must be a closed system and the water must be reinjected back to the same 
ground water storage as taken from. It is not allowed to reinject ground water with temperatures higher 
than 25oC and 20oC on average over a month. Likewise it is not allowed to reinject water colder than 2 oC 
on average over a month 
 
 
==PAGE:144==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
143 of 168 
 
6.10.5 Surface Water 
Surface water from sea, river or large lakes where a large quantity of water is present can be a good heat 
sink. The water temperature is very depending on the geographic location and the water source. The 
temperature may typically vary over the season in northern Europe from 4-6 oC in the winter to 15-20oC 
in the summer. However, for the majority of the year the temperature is below 10oC. Due to the high 
summer temperatures if may be necessary to combine it with compression cooling and it is therefore most 
suitable for building with high cooling loads in all seasons.  
The heat exchanger can either be an open or a closed circuit. In open circuits water is either pumped 
directly to the condenser or to a heat exchanger in a secondary circuit. Careful attention should be paid to 
the materials of the heat exchanger to avoid corrosion and deposition of minerals and other contaminants. 
In closed loops cooling water in a secondary system is either circulated in a long pipe dispatched in the 
bottom of the sea/river acting as a large heat exchanger or to a heat exchanger place in the sea/river.  
Surface water systems do not have to maintain thermal balance compared to ATES due to the large 
quantity of water, but it must still be approved by the local government. However, it can be the case when 
using lakes as heat sink depending on its size and the water flowing through it.  
The location of the building in relation to the surface water is essential for the feasibility of the system. 
Longer distances require higher investment cost in pipes and high pump energy consumption which may 
reduce the overall efficiency quite significantly. It is therefore both suitable for buildings placed next to 
surface water, e.g. on the waterfront in Copenhagen harbor.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:145==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
144 of 168 
 
6.11 District Cooling 
The following is based on the current district cooling situation in Copenhagen and focuses on terms and 
conditions of the sole distributor, HOFOR. District cooling is mainly used for comfort cooling and server 
cooling in commercial buildings.  
The content is written based on correspondence and meetings with the head of district cooling at HOFOR, 
Henrik B√∏geskov.  
 
6.11.1 Definition 
District cooling is based on the same principles as district heating, but delivers cold water instead of hot 
water. However, it is not as widely spread or needed as district heating in Denmark. The concept is still 
relatively new in large scale and started in Copenhagen in 2008. Therefore, the distribution network in 
Copenhagen is still quite limited, but steadily expanding. Additionally, smaller setups of district cooling 
can be found around the country.  
Sharing a central cooling production facility gives certain advantages compared to a local cooling unit in 
terms of energy efficiency (COP/EER), operation and maintenance when scaling production units and 
potentially reducing the total number of units in operation.  
Furthermore, cogeneration of heating and cooling has large potential but is generally limited by low 
simultaneous demand of both heating and cooling. In summer heating is mainly only used for domestic 
hot water production whilst the cooling demand is high. In winter the heating demand for space heating is 
large whilst the cooling demand is mostly insignificant.  
 
6.11.2 Environmental 
District cooling was introduced as an initiative to reduce the overall CO2 emission in Copenhagen and 
thereby as an environmentally friendly alternative to compressor based cooling. District cooling can due 
to extensive use of free cooling maintain a very high average EER and a low environmental impact 
(HOFOR 2015). Even though a large part of the cooling is produced by free cooling, significant energy 
usage of electrical driven distribution pumps have to be taken into account. 
According to HOFOR (HOFOR 2015) an average CO2 emission reduction of 70% could be achieved 
when comparing district cooling to a standard compression system with a EER on 2.5. It is probably not 
the whole truth. The actual difference depends on the EER of the decentralized unit, which today can 
achieve ESEER even above 10, the part of sustainable energy production (wind, water, etc.) in the 
electricity network and how  the production.  
It is impossible to generalize the difference in environmental impact and it has to be calculated based on 
each specific case. For new installations the favor could potentially be in the hands of highly efficient 
decentralized units. 
 
6.11.3 Production 
District cooling is typically produced in three ways: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Free cooling; direct heat exchange with groundwater, sea water, or air 
ÔÇ∑ 
Absorption cooling; combination of surplus heat from district heating and cooling from 
seawater/air 
==PAGE:146==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
145 of 168 
 
ÔÇ∑ 
Compression cooling; electricity driven cooling, where groundwater, seawater or air is 
cooling the condenser 
The production could be a simultaneous mix of two or three of the production options depending on a 
combination of peak-load condition, surplus heat, and temperature of sea, ground and air. Typically the 
production is solely based on free cooling with aid from compression during peak load. 
The overall cooling production efficiency becomes increasingly more effective in line with additional 
customers connection to the existing network.  
Furthermore, large storage tanks can be incorporated in the production system enabling the possibility to 
produce some of the cold water when energy prices and/or source temperatures are favorable.  
The EER of the production should be weighted based on the specific EER and output effect from each 
production unit. An example can be seen in Table 39. Additionally, the energy consumption of 
distribution pumps and energy loss in pipes have to be included to evaluate the overall EER of the system.

6.11.4 Installation 
The distributor is in charge of installing supply and return pipes ended with cut off valves into the desired 
place in the building including the required measuring devices to control supplied effect and temperature 
differences.  
The customer is responsible for the primary side of the installation in the building, namely heat 
exchanger. This way the customers are able to stop the contract and remove his part of the installation 
without involving the distributor. Counter wise the distributor does not have to invest in equipment inside 
the building and does not end up with old heat exchanger.  
A comparison between a 1 MW installation of a conventional refrigeration system and 1 MW district 
cooling suggests that a total area reduction on staggering 95%, from 205 m2 to just 10 m2, can be achieved 
(HOFOR 2015). The savings consists mainly of space used for chiller unit, cooling tower/dry cooler and 
related electrical installation. Instead only a heat exchanger is required. For smaller units the expected 
percentage reduction will be less since the heat exchanger for the district cooling does not change in size 
as much as the refrigeration system.  
Table 39: Design temperatures for district cooling given by HOFOR.

be given to the requirement of minimum 10oC heating of the supply water. If failing to comply 
continuously a penalty tariff for ‚Äòenergy waste‚Äô will be enforced the customer (as also known from district 
heating).

6.11.5 Contract 
Due to legal restrictions, reasons of confidentiality and pure business it has not been possible to get access 
to detailed contract information concerning prices and terms.  
Nevertheless the essence of the contractual content made between client and distributor, in this case 
HOFOR, is listed below. The aim of the contract is to keep the price close to cost neutral compared to 
buying, operating, and maintaining a decentralized cooling unit.  
The contract will according to HOFOR consist of the following: 
ÔÇ∑ 
An initial fee equivalent to the investment and installation cost of a compression cooling system 
ÔÇ∑ 
Yearly cost based on predicted takeoff effect 
ÔÇ∑ 
Yearly cost based on the location of the building in relation to the distribution center 
ÔÇ∑ 
Yearly penalty fee for ‚Äòenergy waste‚Äô if failing to heat the water sufficiently (‚àÜT‚â•10oC) 
The cost per effect is adjusted according to the price of electricity. When the price of electricity is low the 
production of distributed district cooling is cheaper and vice versa. 
 
6.11.6 Limitations 
Limitations for the spread of the cooling distribution network are primarily based on two things: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Distance between production facility and customer; if the distance becomes too large the 
combination of energy losses in pipes and energy consumption of pumps will ruin the 
effectiveness of the system. HOFOR operate with a maximum distance of 5 km. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Placement of cooling source; large scale district cooling systems relies on constant available 
sources at low temperatures in order to maintain sufficient energy efficiency to avoid 
undermining the economy of the system.  
 
Advantages: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Very high security of supply 
ÔÇ∑ 
Flexible supply - possible to adjust the supplied effect according to shifting needs 
ÔÇ∑ 
No unexpected costs for maintenance of decentralized refrigeration plants 
ÔÇ∑ 
Reduced use of space - allocating extra space for other purposes 
ÔÇ∑ 
Potentially more environmentally friendly (less CO2 emission) 
 
Disadvantages: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Currently only available in limited geographical location 
 
 
==PAGE:148==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
147 of 168 
 
6.12 Absorption Chiller 
Absorption chillers are basically similar to a conventional compression chiller. However instead of using 
a compressor driven by expensive high quality electricity it utilizes heat as a thermal energy driver.  
Absorption chillers are usually found at power plants to increase effectiveness of boilers producing hot 
water for district heating or to eliminate extensive surplus heat with chilled water as a byproduct.  
Absorption chillers most commonly utilize a mixture of Lithium Bromide (LiBr) and water. Thereby 
safety precautions for the installation of an absorption 
chiller are very limited. Units with mixtures of water 
and ammonia also exist.  
Since 
absorption 
chillers 
are 
not 
driven 
by 
compressors the generation of noise and vibrations are 
very limited compared to a conventional compressor 
chiller and thus fewer requirements for the technical 
room.  
There are generally three main types of absorption 
chillers available: single-effect indirect-fired (steam, 
hot water), double-effect indirect-fired (steam, hot 
water), and double-effect direct-fired (gas and/or oil 
burner). Specific explanations of the three types are 
outside the scope of this task, but information can be 
found here (Sakraida 2009; Thermax 2008). In the 
following a simplified explanation will presented.  
An 
absorption 
chiller 
consists 
of 
four 
main 
components; Condenser, evaporator, absorber, and 
generator. In the generator the LiBr-solution is heated 
by hot water or steam to a point where the liquid 
refrigerant (water) vaporizes and moves to the 
condenser. In the condenser cooling water, connected 
to a cooling tower or a dry cooler, absorb heat from 
the vaporized refrigerant resulting in liquefying of the 
refrigerant. The liquid moves through an expansion 
valve during which both the temperature and pressure 
drops. It is then pumped into the evaporator where the 
liquid refrigerant vaporizes resulting in the absorption 
of energy from the chilled water running through the 
evaporator. The chilled water is then circulated to the 
cooling system in the building. In the absorber a liquid 
LiBr-solution absorbs the vaporized refrigerant from the evaporator and the released energy from the 
absorption process is removed by cooling water.  
Generally, absorption chillers rely on a high (hot water), a medium (cooling water) and a low temperature 
source (chilled water).  The energy efficiency of absorption chillers relay on the temperature of the supply 
hot water or steam. Higher supply temperatures provide more effect and potentially a higher EER. 
A list of supply temperatures and EER for the three system types compared to conventional compression 
chillers can be found in Table 40. Some single-effect chillers allow hot water down to 55oC though 
resulting in a EER of 0.35 (SEG A/S 2015). 
 
Figure 68: Illustration of an absorption chiller with water-
LiBr as refrigerant(Nielsen 2013b)

In an energy-efficiency competition conventional compression chillers will beat absorption chillers every 
time by up to a factor of 15. However, there are specific applications where absorption chillers have a 
substantial advantage over compression chillers: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Facilities with cogeneration power plants or other thermal energy generating processes (e.g. 
server room). 
ÔÇ∑ 
Facilities with high electrical cost and low fuel/thermal energy cost.  
The internal electricity consumption of an absorption chiller, consisting of two small pumps, is rather 
insignificant. For larger machines (>1 MW) the electricity consumption is approximately 0.2% of the 
cooling effect. For very small machines (<100 kW) consumption is, however, about 1% of the cooling 
capacity.  This is greatly surpassed by the effect required for circulation pumps to circulate water from 
externally coupled circuits (hot water, cooling water, and chilled water) through the machines (SEG A/S 
2015). 
 
6.12.1 Absorption Cooling in the Building Industry 
In perspective of a building designer the hot water single-effect chiller is potentially the most interesting 
as a competitor to conventional compression chillers, since the necessary supply temperature is relatively 
low. It is for instance possible to implement absorption chillers in areas where district heating is available 
with supply temperatures above 70oC (preferably higher) or if the building has heat producing 
applications like server rooms. But cooling via an absorption cooling system from a district heating 
system where surplus heat from waste incineration or industry is used to produce cooling water is not 
particularly energy efficient, but makes sense during periods when the alternative is to reduce surplus heat 
by cooling. 
The most common temperature levels for a hot water single-effect chiller are displayed in Table 41.  
 
Table 41: Temperature level of a hot water single-effect chiller (SEG A/S 2015)

(supply)

(return)
