==PAGE:150==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
149 of 168 
 
However, utilizing district heating for absorption cooling raises numerous issues that have to be tackled 
before it will have a place in the market.  
For the majority of buildings today the main cooling demand occurs during summer, where requirement 
for heating is very low. Thereby, some district heating plants have surplus heat and the required supply 
network to supply high temperature hot water to absorption plants.  
One of the main issues is the requirement to the return temperature of hot water in district heating. For 
instance HOFOR  requires a return temperature of 45oC in the heating season and 35oC outside the 
heating season (HOFOR 2007).  The problem is to obtain sufficient cooling of the hot water in the 
generator. It could potentially be used for domestic hot water production after the generator. If failing to 
comply with the return temperature a penalty tariff will be imposed to the customer. 
Another related issue is the drift in hot water supply temperature. HOFOR allows their supply 
temperature to vary between 95oC in the heating season and 65oC outside the heating season, which 
means the EER will drop significantly outside the heating season and thereby remove the economical 
foundation. 
Special agreements with the local district heating supplier has to made in order to enable absorption 
cooling to be an alternative for compression cooling.  
 
Advantages: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Natural refrigerant (Li-Br and water) 
ÔÇ∑ 
Very few building related precautions (low noise, vibration, and non-toxic) have to be taken 
ÔÇ∑ 
Good economy when free/cheap heat is available 
ÔÇ∑ 
Low primary energy factor when using district heating (BR15/BR20 = 0.8/0.6) compared to 
compression cooling (BR15/BR20 = 2.5/1.8) 
 
Disadvantages:  
ÔÇ∑ 
Issues with tariffs in relation to utilizing surplus heat and district heating 
ÔÇ∑ 
Low EER compared to compression cooling. The price of heat has to be a significantly lower 
than the price of electricity 
 
 
 
==PAGE:151==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
150 of 168 
 
6.13 Reversible Chiller  
A chiller can be reversible turning it into a heat pump making it able to produce heat when cooling is not 
needed. A reversible chiller is similar to a reversible heat pump just sized for the peak cooling load 
instead of the peak heating load. Special types of reversible chillers are able to supply simultaneous 
heating and cooling, but they are not further discussed in this report. 
Reversible chillers should be connected to a heat sink which can perform as a heat source, such as air or 
ground water (ATES). ‹∂‡Øò must be lower than the heat source for the refrigerant to absorb heat and the ‹∂‡Øñ 
has to be higher than the heating system on the supply side for the refrigerant to release heat. 
A reversible chiller is typically able to inverse the direction of the refrigerant flow thus ‚Äúswapping‚Äù the 
evaporator and condenser as illustrated with a water-to-water chiller in Figure 69.  
Figure 69: Example of reversible water-to-water chiller (Stephan et al. 2011).  
1+3 is evaporator/condenser, 2 is expansion valve, 4 is compressor, and 5 is 4-way valve. 
Various combinations exist depending on the system setup. Reversible chillers can be made without 
inversing the flow direction of the refrigerant. The reversibility is done by a change over on the water 
side. Evaporator and condenser are connected to both heat source and heat sink, thus both cooling water 
and chilled water circuit. This setup allows heat recovery and thereby some degree of simultaneous 
heating and cooling.  
Currently, a large offer of reversible chiller units are available on the market with investment costs 
comparable to non-reversible units (Stephan et al. 2011). 
 
6.13.1 Reversibility Potential (REV) 
The reversibility potential (REV) of a chiller depends on the available heat source and occurrence of 
simultaneous heating and cooling demands. REV potential is generally high in moderate and cold 
climates with seasonal heating and cooling dependency. Thereby, REV is most suitable for buildings with 
none or low (requires more than one reversible chiller) simultaneous heating and cooling demand.  
Reversible chiller systems operating in heating mode may require a supplementary boiler to maintain 
sufficient heating supply under all conditions.  
The achievable temperature for heating is in the range between 25-50¬∞C but up to 65 ¬∞C is possible 
(Stephan et al. 2011). However, the COP of the reversed chiller in heating mode increases with lower 
condenser temperature. Therefore, the heating system should be designed as a low temperature heating 
system. 
For the reversibility to be economically feasible it is required that the seasonal energy efficiency (SCOP) 
of the system in heating mode is higher than boiler efficiency. 
==PAGE:152==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
151 of 168 
 
The minimum required SCOP of the reversible chiller in heating mode in relation to the boiler efficiency 
can be described as (Stephan et al. 2011): 
‹µ‹•‹±‹≤> ‹•›ã›è›ê‡Øò‡Øü‡Øò‡Øñ‡Øß‡Ø•‡Øú‡Øñ‡Øú‡Øß‡Ø¨
‹•›ã›è›ê‡Øô‡Ø®‡Øò‡Øü
‚àô≈ã‡Øï‡Ø¢‡Øú‡Øü‡Øò‡Ø• 
New boilers have efficiencies above 95% and the ratio between electricity and e.g. district heating is 
around 3 in Denmark. Thereby, the SCOP should be above 3.0 to make the reversibility feasible in 
operation. If also adjusting for the primary energy factor in the Danish building regulation which is 2.5 
for electricity and 0.8 for district heating then the SCOP have to be 9 to make it feasible.  
 
 
6.14 Heat Recovery (REC) 
Heat Recovery (REC) in chiller and reversible chiller systems is usually achieved by connecting the 
heating system to the secondary cooling water circuit as illustrated in Figure 70. The connection can 
either be direct or through a heat exchanger.  
 
Figure 70: Example of reversible chiller with heat recovery (Stephan et al. 2011) 
 
The potential for REC is generally very low in office buildings and similar building types where co-
demands for heating and cooling are limited. However, the potential can be significantly increased if the 
building has a server room or similar activities that require constant cooling throughout the year. 
The REC capacity is limited by the ‹∂‡Øñ which is most often below 35oC when the chiller is designed for 
comfort cooling application. Thereby the available water temperatures for heating are typically in the 
range of 20-30oC. The heating capacity is thereby relatively small and it may be necessary to combine 
REC with a second heat source e.g. a boiler. 
Consequently, REC is mostly suitable for low temperature heating applications and for heating of 
domestic hot water (DHW). Especially the potential energy savings for DHW can be significant for 
buildings such as hospitals with a high consumption of DHW due to high simultaneous demands for 
cooling and DHW.   
 
 
==PAGE:153==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
152 of 168 
 
6.15 Single or Multi System 
In large refrigeration systems with high demands for supply security the traditional design rule of thumb 
was to size the system according to the peak load condition and install a number of parallel coupled 
chillers. In case of part-load condition one or more chillers shuts down to reduce the capacity. To ensure 
supply security in case of breakdown or maintenance one extra chiller was installed as backup ‚Äì the so 
called (N+1)-rule.  
Studies on load distributions for chillers resulted in the frequency curve shown in Figure 71 which 
illustrates that a chiller sized to just 60% of the peak cooling load results in a reduction of the yearly 
energy produced by less than 10% (Stephan et al. 2011). Thereby a system with two smaller chillers may 
be preferred. However, chillers with the new Turbocor compressor may benefit from this distribution due 
to its superior part-load performance thus only one chiller will be optimal.  
 
 
Figure 71: Cumulative frequency curve for cooling demand (Stephan et al. 2011) 
 
Refrigeration systems with reversible chillers may indeed benefit for having multiple chillers installed to 
cover simultaneous heating and cooling loads which can be relevant for larger commercial buildings 
especially during the transition seasons.  
Selecting multiple chiller systems may result in more complex hydraulic installations and controls 
potentially resulting in increased investment costs which may be difficult to regain in reduced operation 
costs. Another important consideration is that multiple smaller chiller units may perform worse than one 
single larger unit. Thus careful analysis of load conditions, installation cost and operational cost must be 
carried out to determine which combination is the most optimal for a given building. 
 
 
==PAGE:154==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
153 of 168 
 
6.16 Installation 
This part will consist of some relevant considerations when installing refrigeration systems. 
General: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Refrigerant system at a certain capacity may require both ventilation and cooling of the machine 
room.  
Vibrations: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Vibration absorption support beneath the chiller may be necessary. Typically a concreate bed 
with 2-3 times the weight of the chiller.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Pipe bearings in the machine room should be designed in a way that does not distribute vibrations 
further out in the distribution system. The pipe bearing may be mounted on metal stands with 
vibration dampers. 
Noise:  
ÔÇ∑ 
Acoustical absorbers may be necessary for both indoor and outdoor units. Noise level for dry 
cooler and cooling tower may be in the range of 50-70 dB(A) (Stephan et al. 2011). The upper 
outdoor noise level requirement is 40 dB(A) at 10 meter distance. Refrigeration systems may 
have noise levels above 90dB(A) and should be placed inside machine rooms with the necessary 
acoustical precaution.  
ÔÇ∑ 
In general, units should be placed away from recreational spaces and strategically according to 
windows and neighbors  
ÔÇ∑ 
Low noise generating components are typically more expensive.  
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:155==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
154 of 168 
 
6.17 Recommendations 
This part will summarize the described refrigeration systems and highlight the recommended aspects.  
There is no general way to select a refrigeration system. A wide range of parameters have to be assessed 
such as temperatures, passive cooling opportunities, available heat sinks, life cycle cost (investment, 
operation and maintenance), additional heating/cooling demands, reversibility potential, heat recovery 
potential, environmental impact, and so forth. 
 
Refrigerant 
ÔÇ∑ 
Refrigerants in 1-stage chillers should have GWP below 2500 to follow the f-gas regulation in 
2020. However, this limit is expected to be set even lower by the upcoming regulation. Therefore 
it is recommended to select refrigerants with the lowest possible GWP, such as 1234ze, HC‚Äôs, 
CO2 and ammonia. It is expected to see an increasing amount of new HFC and HFO blends with 
low GWP in the near future to be available on the market. 
ÔÇ∑ 
The charge limit on 10 kg for HFC‚Äôs and HFO‚Äôs is a large issue when selecting chillers. 10 kg 
HFC corresponds to a cooling effect in the range of ~80 kW cooling. Higher capacity can be 
achieved by installing multiple HFC/HFO chillers in parallel connected to the same chiller water 
network, but the refrigerant circuits have to be completely separated. The 10 kg rule is likely to 
be altered in the future in favor of very low GWP HFC‚Äôs and HFO‚Äôs to allow larger charge sizes. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Dispensation may be granted for larger charge size of HFC/HFO if it is possible to document, e.g. 
through a TEWI calculation, that the system performs better and with lower environmental 
impact compared to the best legal alternative which usually is ammonia.  
ÔÇ∑ 
A list of refrigerants suitable for high temperature cooling has been made. All refrigerants were 
able to achieve similar EER rating in a stationary load calculation in a fictive chiller setup. The 
selection of refrigerants is largely depended on the component specification. Refrigerant which 
require large pressure or large volumetric flow rate may increase the component cost and the size 
of the system. In the end a dynamic simulation with real components is necessary to determine 
which refrigerant is the most optimal of the given situation. The component cost and the safety 
requirement of the corresponding is of very high importance for the selection. 
ÔÇ∑ 
The cost and tax rate of the refrigerants should also be considered since large variations exist. 
 
Chiller 
ÔÇ∑ 
Chillers should generally be selected based on the highest possible ESEER. The EER should not 
be neglected. However, it can be a good idea to demand a simulation of the chiller performance 
from the manufacturer with the real design loads and weather conditions, due to the rigid 
calculation method of ESEER and EER.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Variable speed compressors with high isentropic efficiency throughout its working area should be 
selected. Centrifugal compressors with magnetic bearings such as Turbocor are currently the best 
selection for chillers with primarily part-load operation. The full-load performance of different 
compressor types is very similar, but much diverged in part-load operation. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Chillers should be installed with an electronic expansion valve (EEV) to enable variable control 
of the pressure level and thus ‹∂‡Øò. An EEV compared to a TEV may increase the performance by 
~15% when operating in variable loads conditions 
ÔÇ∑ 
Increase ‹∂‡Øò and decrease ‹∂‡Øñ to maximize the chiller performance. A 1oC increase in the ‹∂‡Øò results 
in ~3% less energy consumption. 
 
==PAGE:156==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
155 of 168 
 
Heat sink 
ÔÇ∑ 
Selection of heat sinks and condenser unit depends on various factors such as the availability, 
temperatures and the initial costs of the system.  
ÔÇ∑ 
Air cooled condensers are the most widely used due to the high availability of colder air in the 
northern countries, but the efficiency varies depending on the season. Available outdoor space is 
required for the dry coolers but then it is relatively cheap to install. However, it may have 
relatively high noise levels which decrease the possible placement especially in urban 
environments.  
ÔÇ∑ 
The potential for passive cooling should always be investigated since it may reduce significantly 
the chiller operation when implementing high temperature cooling application and thereby lead to 
significant energy savings. It is therefore recommended for any case to investigate the 
opportunities and costs of utilizing ground water, ground, and sea water. All three are stable low 
temperature heat sinks which are very suitable for passive cooling in high temperature cooling, 
but these sinks vary significantly in availability and cost depending on the location. Passive 
cooling may also be achieved by dry coolers for long periods when the outdoor temperature is 
below 10-15oC depending on the chilled water temperature. 
 
Temperature ranges 
The following is a short summary of the most important design parameters for high temperature 
refrigeration systems, illustrated in Figure 72: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Condensation temperature, ‹∂‡Øñ: 20-35oC 
ÔÇ∑ 
Evaporation temperature, ‹∂‡Øò: 9-15oC 
ÔÇ∑ 
Min. ‚àÜT of 10oC between  ‹∂‡Øñ and ‹∂‡Øò with mechanical expansion valve 
ÔÇ∑ 
Max. ‚àÜT of 5oC between ‹∂‡Øò and chilled water supply temperature  
ÔÇ∑ 
Max. ‚àÜT of 6oC between the refrigerant and cooling water for water cooled condensers 
ÔÇ∑ 
Max. ‚àÜT of 10oC between air and refrigerant in air cooled condenser 
ÔÇ∑ 
0-5oC superheat 
ÔÇ∑ 
> 0oC sub-cooling 
ÔÇ∑ 
Chilled water supply temperature, ‹∂‡Øñ‡Ø™‡Ø¶: 14-20oC 
ÔÇ∑ 
Min. ‚àÜT of 5oC for chilled water supply/return

==PAGE:157==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
156 of 168 
 
Alternative refrigeration system 
ÔÇ∑ 
District cooling is in many ways superior to chillers and other means of cooling. It is 
recommended to use district cooling network to achieve a constant cold water supply with high 
security of supply without the need of any machinery except a heat exchanger. District cooling 
removes the need for precautions in relation to maintenance, space consumption, safety 
requirement, and noise of the traditional refrigeration systems. Unfortunately, district cooling is 
currently limited to a few locations in Denmark. Careful attention should be paid to the contract 
with the distributor to enable flexibility in the take-off and avoid legal bindings for very long 
period. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Absorption chillers are not recommended in commercial buildings and are mostly interesting for 
buildings with waste heat available such as production facilities and especially in combination 
with waste incineration plants. Examples with absorption chillers utilizing district heating can be 
found but the efficiency and economy is surpassed by high efficiency chillers. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Reversible chillers are attractive when heating and cooling demands are divided in separate 
periods with no simultaneous demands. Simultaneous demand would require multiple parallel 
coupled reversible chillers. A reversible chiller can typically supply heating with temperature in 
the range of 25-50oC, thus the heating system should be designed for low temperature heating. 
ÔÇ∑ 
Heat recovery on the condenser side of chillers is attractive when it can be combined with 
production of larger amounts of domestic hot water at e.g. hospitals. Here significant energy can 
be saved for the hot water production.   
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:158==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
157 of 168 
 
7 Discussion 
The discussion will focus on the supply, distribution and production of cooling. 
Utilizing high temperatures for cooling seems to be slowly developing in the building industry. 
Researchers have indicated the possibilities for a long time, but the development of new products does not 
seem to follow so quickly. The reason for the slow development toward high temperature cooling 
products might be found in the building regulations around the world and the fact that the increased focus 
on energy savings and NZEB is still fairly new. Also, the traditional manufacturers seem to want to 
remain in the business by slowly improving their low temperature equipment instead of new thinking.  
In general, thorough unbiased independent reviews of components related to high temperature cooling 
have been difficult to come by. Mostly, new components are highlighted by a comparison to ‚Äúthe 
conventional solution‚Äù which is rarely particularly specified and is typically an older solution with 
average specifications. These kinds of comparisons are generally not interesting since they do not prove 
anything other than that the new product is better than a previous product on the market.  
Selection of components should not alone be based on the fact that it is better than the old one, because it 
most likely is, but the selection should rather focus on the alternative products available on the market. 
However, direct comparison with other new state-of-the-art components from different manufacturers are 
very rarely seen, which makes it difficult to evaluate which product is actually the best in class for a given 
task. It seems like a general tendency across the industry from compressors to chilled beams and no one 
seems to be interested in a face-to-face comparison with one‚Äôs direct components.  
 
7.1 Selection and Knowledge Sharing 
This report has illustrated a large number of possible cooling solutions. The obvious question is why 
aren‚Äôt a broader range of these cooling solutions incorporated into the Danish building industry?  
A very common aspect could be the lack of time for engineers to obtain new knowledge. Often consulting 
companies are running with focus on effectivity and earnings rather than spending sufficiently on 
developing their primary asset ‚Äì knowledge of the employees. In the current situation with a booming 
industry with lack of qualified engineers and piles of tasks for the companies to do, very little is done to 
further educate their employees even though the companies have a general desire to do so with finances 
set aside but the engineers time and focus is spent on completing projects. Furthermore, young engineers 
tend to bring new ideas and theory from university directly into a company but they are most often put 
into job positions without decision-making and rely on senior engineers to select the solutions and leave 
the young engineers to do calculations and drawings.  
Consequently, a side effect of the lack of time is definitely insufficient knowledge sharing between 
engineers even within a company, but also from one company to another, and especially between the 
companies and the universities.  
Even if a new solution was designed after the given standards and guidelines and integrated into a new 
building, the involved engineers are mostly moved on to new tasks as soon as the system is balanced and 
the building is handed over to the client. Afterwards, it is most often is up to the contractor to fix potential 
problems and thereby the engineers are rarely involved in the post-processing of the systems and the 
building. The next task may be completely different with different systems to be used where by the 
experiences with the new system sticks to the involved engineers. More time and attention should be 
invested in the post-processing of a building ‚Äì especially when new alternative solutions are implemented 
‚Äì in order to spread knowledge and experiences.  
==PAGE:159==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
158 of 168 
 
The universities and the various interest groups of the industry have to take a larger responsibility and be 
a more active part in knowledge sharing and providing of the newest knowledge in a tangible way to the 
consulting companies. This could be done through easy accessible knowledge databases and more 
presentations at the consulting companies minded direct implementation in buildings. Also higher 
involvement in specific projects could be a good solution for both researchers at universities and the 
consulting engineers to learn something new and mutually share experience and knowledge.  
Another important aspect is the lack of willingness from the clients to ‚Äòexperiment‚Äô with new solutions 
which may involve slightly higher investment cost in solutions that potentially will be better for indoor 
comfort and lead to long-term savings. This aspect makes perfectly sense since most clients have limited 
funds available to immediate invest in their buildings and cannot afford mistakes which leads back to 
selecting the common well tested solutions.  
The investment cost may most often become the largest hurdle for building owners due to the fact that it 
can be difficult to borrow more money than the initial budget to get the slightly more expensive system 
which might be much cheaper over its life time. Therefore, to properly evaluate a cooling solution a life-
cycle-cost analysis for each of the available options is necessary. 
New technologies often have higher initial costs for the first few clients before benefitting from 
experiences, craftsman skills and mass production. Without previous large scale successful references 
new solutions are difficult to sell. Essentially, a vicious circle that often rely on dedicated manufactures 
who offer new solutions in small scale free of charge or very cheap to convince potential clients that the 
new technology will worth further investment.  
 
7.2 Distribution 
An important part of a cooling system is the design of the distribution system in terms of pipes, ducts, 
dampers and valves. However, surprisingly small amounts of adequate literature and studies are available 
for the design of distribution systems in relation to high temperature cooling. It has only been possible to 
find rule of thumbs together with simple advices, but no comprehensive holistic design guides have been 
found in relation the best design of distribution systems.  
More attention should be paid to develop routing strategies for distribution networks which minimize 
spatial needs, reduce interactions, ease the construction phase, optimize the building layout, and at the 
same time satisfies the energy requirements and optimize the control. All of which can facilitate potential 
cost savings especially in terms of reduced material, labor time, conflicts at the construction site, and in 
the end may lead to significant energy savings in the operation and maintenance   
It should be possible to develop more standardized solutions for optimal pipe and duct routing and sizing, 
shaft design, placement of technical/machine rooms, etc. Instead it is left to the individual engineers and 
companies to develop their own strategies based on experiences and rule of thumbs.  
In general, the design of a cooling system should overall strive for simplicity and aim for few heat 
transfers, few components, avoid very complex control strategies and avoid complex installations. 
Optimally a building should have one ventilation system for IAQ and one thermal system in connection to 
solar shadings to balance both heating and cooling load. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:160==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
159 of 168 
 
7.3 Refrigeration 
Traditionally, a high temperature difference between the heating system and the cooling system has been 
used. Manufacturers for chiller and related equipment are not yet focusing on the use of high temperature 
cooling. In most product catalogues the maximum evaporation temperature stated is 10oC, and not much 
attention is paid to the operation above 5oC temperatures. Similarly, the condenser temperatures are 
mostly starting from 35oC and up with very little attention to the operation below this level. The reason is 
probably the fixed rating conditions and calculation methods for EER and ESEER.  
The EER and ESEER are a good start when comparing chillers. However, it is still only a narrow 
guideline for the actual functioning of the product under different operational conditions than the standard 
conditions in the test. It is of course not possible to do test ratings for every possible scenario, but it 
would be a good idea to at least make standard test ratings for different climate zones with different 
temperature set.  
Further improvements to the test could be to include a range of different load scenarios for each climate 
zone. However, this is up to the manufacturers if they want to pay for these extra rating procedures, but 
also up to the local governments and the EU to set requirements for more tests ratings.  
 
7.4 Refrigerants 
The regulation on refrigerants charge limits in Denmark is somehow out of proportion. There is no reason 
in having a general 10 kg limit for all HFC‚Äôs and HFO‚Äôs when some of them are obviously nearly as 
environmentally friendly as HC‚Äôs and the natural refrigerants. Of course the regulation is to prevent the 
use of high GWP substances, which is working, but it is limiting the use of perfectly good refrigerant with 
very low GWP, such as R1234ze, which even has lower flammability and toxicity, thus higher safety, 
compared to HC‚Äôs and the natural refrigerants.  
It seems like a political decision affected by Danish manufacturers of especially ammonia systems, which 
have been market dominating for a long time with strong traditions and high quality and high efficiency 
products. Even though ammonia is a superior refrigerant its use is limited by it safety level B2 ‚Äì higher 
toxicity and lower flammability ‚Äì requiring a range of safety precautions which consequently is 
undesirable/impossible in certain building locations and types.  
If performing a TEWI calculation it will illustrate that the equivalent CO2 emission from the refrigerant 
itself is almost none compared to the CO2 emission from the production of the electricity that it consumes 
in its life time. Second of all, the refrigerant is no harm to the environment as long as it is inside a well-
designed and proper maintained refrigeration system. The refrigerant is only to harm if it is released into 
the atmosphere. But with the regulations for maintenance and control of refrigeration systems in Denmark 
the leak rate is small and when the refrigeration system is taken out of production the refrigerant will be 
recycled or destroyed in a proper manner.  
At the bottom line refrigerants should of course strive towards a low GWP, but more relevant is the 
energy consumption of the system, which essentially has a way more significant influence on the 
environment.  
 
==PAGE:161==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
160 of 168 
 
7.5 The Every Day Challenge 
At the bottom line the everyday challenges of cooling supply contains numerous interests and 
requirements and could look like this: 
ÔÇ∑ 
Clients:  
Excellent indoor environment, low cost, low maintenance, high flexibility 
ÔÇ∑ 
Architect: 
Pretty (invisible), flexible spaces 
ÔÇ∑ 
Engineer: 
Previous experiences, well-tested solutions, buildability 
ÔÇ∑ 
Contractor: 
Cheapest adequate solution 
ÔÇ∑ 
Regulation: 
Low energy, good indoor environment 
The main question is how to solve this challenge? The easy answer is it cannot be solved completely - 
simply not all requirements can be fully satisfied without comprising another requirement or interest. 
Essentially, three parameters are overshadowing the rest; energy consumption, indoor environment, and 
investment cost. The rest is more or less one big pile of compromises, which outcome depends on 
multiple parametric decisions.  One parametric decision may not necessarily out rule another, but it may 
very well limit its boundaries.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:162==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
161 of 168 
 
8 Conclusion 
The following is an overall conclusion for this study. Detailed conclusions are found in the 
recommendations section at the end of the respective chapters.  
The report focused on high temperature cooling of office buildings and similar building types. However, 
many of the ideas and approaches may be applied universally when designing cooling systems. 
The use of mechanical cooling should in general be avoided by minimizing the heat load already in the 
design phase. Nevertheless, high requirements to the indoor environment and the nature of modern 
architecture (large window areas) make it difficult to completely avoid some degree of mechanical 
cooling. 
 
For cases with high cooling loads a combination of a ventilation system, to handle the latent load, the 
IAQ and a minor part for the sensible loads, and a water based system, to handle the main part of the 
sensible loads (both heating and cooling), seems like the optimal solution to achieve a good constant 
indoor environment with high energy efficiency. However, careful attention should be paid to the control 
and the interaction between the two systems to obtain an optimized and satisfying operation.  
For cases with low cooling loads all-air systems may be the most energy, spatial and cost efficient, since 
air should be supplied to any occupied spaces. However, air heating must not be the only source of 
heating according to BR20, thus a sort of radiant system must be installed anyway.  
The selection of a space cooling strategy is strongly dependent on the space dimensions, the layout, the 
occupancy, the use and especially the heat load ‚Äì every solution has advantages, disadvantages and 
limitations. Therefore, schemes with options for cooling supply according to some standard spaces have 
been developed to illustrate possibilities, limitations, and compatibility between the options. 
 
To improve the energy efficiency of cooling systems the focus should be to minimize the temperature 
difference from heat sink to space supply with as few heat transfers as possible. Lower temperature 
differences increase the efficiency of chillers.  
Chillers with mainly part-load operation should be selected based on highest ESEER with variable speed 
compressors and electronic expansion valve to improve the energy efficiency and the control. 
High temperature cooling enables more sustainable/low quality source such as ground, ground water, sea, 
and air, while increasing the possible use of free cooling to a much wider extent, thus leading to energy 
savings. However, it requires larger heat transfer areas for space supply, e.g. chilled ceilings. The 
increased size of the cooling coil in the air handling unit may however impose larger pressure loss to the 
ventilation system. Therefore, it is suggested to utilize two charge-over coils instead of one heating and 
one cooling coil to increase the heat transfer area for both cooling and heating without increasing the total 
size and thus the pressure loss.  
High temperature cooling facilitates a higher degree of passive cooling compared to traditional low 
temperature systems which may lead to significant energy savings. Therefore, the potential for passive 
cooling should always be investigated according to the available heat sinks (i.e. ground, ground water, sea 
water and air) in relation to their respective cost.  
 
Every building design process includes an extensive amount of parameters which can greatly influence 
the selection and operation of a cooling system. Therefore, no unambiguous answer can be made to which 
selections is the best overall. Nevertheless, it is possible to significantly narrow down the number of 
==PAGE:163==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
162 of 168 
 
possibilities already in the early design phases depending on building type, use and location, as well as 
specific space dimensions.   
 
A holistic design approach is necessary ‚Äì building design, supply of cooling, distribution and production 
should be designed in close collaboration between engineers and architects, not separately, to be able for 
the cooling system to constantly ensure a satisfying indoor environment while achieving the best overall 
system performance in terms of energy efficiency, precision, reliability and cost.  
A good building design, in terms of layout, orientation, window design, and especially solar protection, is 
essential to remove or strongly minimize the need for mechanical cooling. A poor building design cannot 
necessarily be ‚Äúsaved‚Äù by means of an excellent cooling system design with outstanding controls without 
destroying the energy frame. 
Solar radiation usually is the highest contributor to the cooling load and must be minimized by proper 
solar protection. Mechanical solar shading, either externally or in double skin fa√ßades, are the best way to 
reduce the solar heat gains and attention should be paid to the control of the shading in collaboration with 
the cooling system. Alternatively, the solar heat gain can be reduced by nano-film or electrochromic 
glazing, but they are not yet nearly as effective as mechanical shadings. They may however be applied in 
combination with architectural shadings and internal blinds.  
The building layout should take into account the most sensitive spaces and the spaces with high internal 
heat loads, and places these strategically according to solar load and cooling supply (i.e. close to shafts 
and technical room. 
 
Overall this study has illustrated the very broad knowledge required to be able to guide the building and 
system design processes towards satisfactory and energy efficient cooling solutions.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:164==
MSc project ‚Äì Cooling of buildings 
 
September 2016 
163 of 168 
 
9 Further Research 
This section will briefly touch upon relevant further research related to the selection and design of cooling 
systems. 
 
The investment cost for every part of a cooling system is of high importance for the selection. A 
component may prove to have outstanding performance, energy efficiency and controllability, but if it is 
made of pure gold or have to be connected to diamonds then no one can afford it in the first place, thus it 
is not a real alternative.   
Therefore, a study of the energy performance and effect on the indoor environment in relation to 
investment cost of the overall systems could be very relevant. Most studies only focus the operation cost 
and energy consumption, but neglect the initial investment costs, which is essential for the majority of 
building owners. It may also be the reason why the traditional solutions still have a large place in the 
market even though better options may be available.  
 
Another general issue which is relevant to study is the control of cooling supply to a space in the 
transition periods where the demand is balancing between either a low heating demand or a low cooling 
demand. The demands are often varying on/off with swiftly changing conditions is a struggling task to 
control with high temperature cooling and low temperature heating applications.  
This issue is equally struggling for the control of chillers, heat pumps, and heaters to avoid unnecessary 
energy consumption.
